Conceivability as a Condition for Oath Validity
Establishes that an oath requires a conceivable act and explains disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition that an oath requires a conceivable act, distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, Abu Yusuf, Zufar, and al-Shafi'i, and apply the ruling to examples such as ascending the sky or turning stone to gold.

Core Idea
An oath requires a conceivable act; customarily impossible acts can still be valid if actually possible.

Oaths (ayman) are serious commitments, and the Hanafi school has established specific conditions for their validity. One key condition is that the sworn act must be conceivable (mutasawwar) in reality. This lesson unpacks the principle and the intra-Hanafi disagreement over whether this condition is necessary, using vivid examples like swearing to ascend the sky or turn stone to gold.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
What is the disputed point? It is whether the conceivability (tasawwur) of the sworn act is a condition for the validity of an oath.
2
State the position of each Hanafi imam
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require tasawwur; Abu Yusuf does not; Zufar and al-Shafi'i (in one opinion) require it and go further to deem oaths on customarily impossible acts invalid.
Note the difference between requiring tasawwur and its application to customarily impossible acts.
3
Analyze the proofs
The two argue that the purpose of an oath is to fulfill the act; expiation is a substitute. If the act is inconceivable, the oath cannot be valid. Abu Yusuf argues that oaths on customarily impossible acts are valid and breach occurs immediately.
The two respond that such acts are actually possible (angels ascended, Allah can transform stone).
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In the case of an oath on something customarily impossible (like ascending sky), the two say valid and breach immediately; Zufar and al-Shafi'i say invalid; Abu Yusuf says valid and breach immediately but for a different reason (no tasawwur condition).
The practical difference is whether expiation is due.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is the relied-upon position, as maintained by later Hanafi authorities like Al-Marghinani.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان فصب حنث. حلف ليصعدن السماء أو ليقلبن هذا الحجر ذهباً حنث في الحال. والأصل في هذا وأمثاله أن كون البر متصوراً شرط في انعقاد اليمين المطلقة عن الوقت، وفي بقاء المقيدة بالوقت عند الطرفين؛ لأن حكم اليمين وجوب البر، فأما الكفارة فهي خلف عنه عند عجزه، فإذا لم يمكن البر لعدم تصوره لا تجب الكفارة. وعند أبي يوسف كونه متصوراً ليس بشرط؛ لأن اليمين قد ينعقد على ما لا يتصور عادة كقوله: "والله لأصعدن السماء أو لأحولن هذا الحجر ذهباً" ينعقد ويحنث في الحال لعدم إمكان البر عادة. ثم فيما إذا لم يكن في الكوز ماء فالبر غير ممكن، فلا يحنث عندهما خلافاً له سواء ذكر اليوم أو لا. وإن كان فيه ماء، فإن ذكر اليوم فالبر إنما يجب في الجزء الآخر من اليوم، فإذا صب لم يكن البر متصوراً، فلا يحنث عندهما خلافاً له. وإن لم يذكر اليوم، فالبر إنما يجب عليه إذا فرغ من التكلم لكن موسعاً بشرط أن لا يفوت في مدة عمره، والبر متصور عند الفراغ من التكلم فانعقد اليمين، فإذا صب فقد فات البر فيحنث إجماعاً. والحاصل أن عند أبي يوسف: يحنث في الكل، لكن في المؤقت بعد الوقت، وفي غير المؤقت في الحال. فصب حنث فيما إذا لم يقل اليوم اتفاقاً لانعقاد اليمين وفوت البر بالإراقة حنث في الحال للعجز؛ لأنه متصور في الجملة. أي لو حلف ليصعدن السماء أو ليقلبن هذا الحجر ذهباً انعقدت يمينه وحنث في الحال، قيد بالفعل؛ لأنه لو حلف على الترك بأن قال: إن تركت مس السماء فكذا لم تنعقد يمينه؛ لأن الترك لا يتصور في غير المقدور. ووجه الانعقاد في "ليصعدن" ونحوه؛ لأن صعود السماء ممكن، ولهذا صعدتها الملائكة وبعض الأنبياء، وكذا تحويل الحجر ذهباً بقدرة الله فإذا كان متصوراً انعقد اليمين ويحنث في الحال للعجز الثابت عادة. ووضع اليمين في المطلقة؛ لأن المقيدة يتوقف حنثه فيها على مضي الوقت حتى لو مات قبله لم يحنث. وقال زفر: لا ينعقد؛ لأنه مستحيل عادة فأشبه المستحيل حقيقة. قلنا: إن البر متصور حقيقة ويحنث للعجز العادي. وقال الشافعي في قول: لا ينعقد ولا يحنث.
And if he poured it, he breaks the oath. If he swore to ascend the sky or to turn this stone into gold, he breaks the oath immediately. The principle in this and similar cases is that the act being conceivable is a condition for the validity of an absolute oath (unrestricted by time) and for the continuation of a time-bound oath according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Because the ruling of an oath is the obligation of fulfilling the sworn act, and the expiation is a substitute upon inability. So if the act is impossible due to inconceivability, expiation is not obligatory. According to Abu Yusuf, its conceivability is not a condition, because an oath may be valid upon something customarily inconceivable, like if one says: 'By Allah, I will ascend the sky or turn this stone into gold'—it is valid and he breaks the oath immediately due to the customary impossibility of fulfillment. Then, if there is no water in the jug, the act is impossible, so he does not break the oath according to the two, contrary to him, whether he mentioned 'today' or not. If there is water, and he mentioned 'today', then the act is only required in the remaining part of the day; if he poured it, the act is not conceivable, so he does not break the oath according to the two, contrary to him. If he did not mention 'today', the act is required upon him after finishing speaking, but with extensiveness provided it does not lapse within his lifetime; the act is conceivable at the time of finishing speaking, so the oath is valid. If he poured it, the act has been missed, so he breaks the oath by consensus. The summary is that according to Abu Yusuf, he breaks the oath in all cases, but in the time-bound case after the time, and in the non-time-bound immediately. So if he poured it, he breaks the oath when he did not say 'today' by agreement due to the validity of the oath and the loss of the act by pouring; he breaks the oath immediately due to inability, because it is conceivable in general. That is, if he swore to ascend the sky or turn this stone into gold, his oath is valid and he breaks the oath immediately, restricted to an affirmative act; because if he swore on abstention by saying 'if I leave touching the sky, then...' his oath does not become valid, because abstention from something not in one's power is not conceivable. The reason for validity in 'I will ascend' and the like is that ascending the sky is possible, as angels and some prophets have ascended it, and similarly turning stone into gold is within Allah's power; so it is conceivable, so the oath is valid and he breaks the oath immediately due to customary inability. And the oath in the absolute case is established; because in the time-bound case, breach depends on the passage of time, so if he dies before that, he does not breach. Zufar said: it is not valid, because it is customarily impossible, thus similar to actually impossible. We said: the act is actually conceivable, and he breaks the oath due to customary inability. Al-Shafi'i said in one opinion: it is not valid and he does not break the oath.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling on oaths, specifically addressing the condition that the sworn act must be conceivable (mutasawwar) for the oath to be valid. The author begins with an example: if someone swears to pour water from a jug and then pours it, he breaks the oath. Then he presents the fundamental principle: for an oath unrestricted by time, the act must be conceivable for the oath to be valid; for a time-bound oath, it must remain conceivable throughout the period. This is the view of Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad (the two, الطرفين). The reasoning is that the purpose of an oath is to fulfill the sworn act, and if that is impossible due to inconceivability, the oath is invalid and no expiation is required. Abu Yusuf opposes this, arguing that an oath can be valid even on customarily impossible acts, as in the example of swearing to ascend the sky or turn stone to gold—the oath is valid and breach occurs immediately due to customary inability. The passage then details the application to the case of water in a jug: if there is no water, the act is impossible, so according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) the oath does not become valid; Abu Yusuf disagrees. If there is water and the swearer mentions 'today', the oath requires him to drink it in the remaining part of the day; if he pours it, the act is no longer conceivable, so again no breach according to the two. If he does not mention 'today', the act is required after he finishes speaking but with a broad timeframe; the act is conceivable at that moment, so the oath is valid, and pouring it constitutes a breach by consensus. The passage then addresses the specific case of swearing to ascend the sky or turn stone to gold: the ruling is that the oath is valid and breach occurs immediately. The author notes that this applies only to affirmative acts, not to abstention (e.g., swearing to leave touching the sky), because abstention from an impossible act is not conceivable. The validity is justified by the possibility of such acts in reality—angels and prophets have ascended the sky, and Allah can transform stone to gold. The breach is due to customary inability. The passage also includes a dispute with Zufar, who argues that such an oath is invalid because the act is customarily impossible, and with al-Shafi'i (in one narration) who also denies validity and breach. The Hanafi response is that the act is actually conceivable, so the oath is valid, and breach occurs due to customary inability. This lesson establishes the Hanafi position that conceivability is a condition for oath validity, with detailed applications and intra-Hanafi conflicts.

Key Terms
البر (al-birr): The sworn act that must be performed to fulfill the oath.
التصور (al-tasawwur): The possibility of the act in reality, even if customarily unlikely.
المطلقة (al-mutlaqah): An oath unrestricted by a time limit.
المقيدة (al-muqayyadah): An oath restricted by a time limit (e.g., 'today').
الحنث (al-hinth): The act of breaking the oath by failing to perform the sworn act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, what is the ruling if someone swears 'By Allah, I will ascend the sky'?
A) The oath is invalid because it is impossible.
B) The oath is valid and he breaks it immediately.
C) The oath is valid but he only breaks it after death.
D) The oath is invalid unless he specifies a time.

Key Takeaway
An oath is only valid if the sworn act is conceivable in reality (even if customarily improbable); otherwise, no breach or expiation is due.

Oath Not to Speak: Sleep and Permission Cases
Intra-Hanafi rulings on oath breaches

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for two scenarios of an oath not to speak and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the knowledge requirement for permission.

Core Idea
Speaking to a sleeping person only breaks an oath if you wake him; permission requires the swearer's knowledge.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq examines two boundary cases of an oath not to speak. When the person is asleep, waking him counts as speech, but speaking without waking may be disputed. When permission is given, does the swearer need to know? Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf differ on whether permission requires knowledge or is like internal consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two subcases
The passage covers two distinct scenarios: (1) swearing not to speak to someone who is asleep, and (2) swearing not to speak except with permission, where permission is given but the swearer does not know.
2
Analyze the sleeping person scenario
Note the condition: waking the sleeper by calling constitutes speech (because the swearer intentionally makes himself heard). If the sleeper is not woken, there is a dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
Imam Abu Hanifa: breach if the sleeper would have heard if awake; Sahibayn: no breach unless woken.
3
Understand the linguistic basis for permission
The word 'idhn' (permission) is derived from 'adhan' (to inform) or from falling into the ear. Both imply knowledge. Therefore, Imam Abu Hanifa holds that permission is ineffective without the swearer's knowledge.
Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing permission is like release (itlaq).
4
Distinguish permission from consent
Consent (rida) is an internal state of the heart, effective without external knowledge. Permission (idhn) is external and requires awareness. This distinction is key to the Hanafi position.
The text contrasts idhn with rida to justify the different ruling.
5
Apply the rulings to practical cases
Given the scenario, identify which imam's view is relied upon (mufta bihi) and why.
The text states that for the sleeper case, the chosen view is no breach if not woken; for permission, the view of breach (Abu Hanifa) is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يكلمه أي لو حلف لا يكلم فلاناً. فأيقظه بندائه، فلو لم يوقظه لم يحنث وهو المختار. فناداه أو إلا بإذنه: أي أو حلف لا يكلمه إلا بإذنه. حنث أي في الوجهين: الأول: أنه حلف لا يكلم فلاناً، وهو نائم فأيقظه بندائه حنث؛ لأنه كلمه وأسمعه، ولو لم يوقظه فإذا كان بحيث يسمع لو لم يكن نائماً يحنث عند الإمام خلافاً لهما. والثاني: لو حلف لا يكلم إلا بإذنه فأذن له ولم يعلم الحالف بالإذن حتى كلمه حنث؛ لأن الإذن مشتق من الأذان الذي هو الإعلام، أو من الوقوع في الإذن، وكل ذلك لا يتحقق إلا بعد العلم. وقال أبو يوسف لا يحنث؛ لأن الإذن هو الإطلاق، وإنه يتم بالإذن كالرضاء فيما إذا حلف لا يكلم إلا برضاه فرضي المحلوف عليه ولم يعلم الحالف فكلمه حيث لا يحنث؛ لأن الرضاء يتم بالراضي. قلنا: الرضاء من أعمال القلب ولا كذلك الإذن؛ لأنه من الأذان وهو الإعلام، ولا يتحقق بدون العلم.
It means if he swore not to speak to someone. He woke him by calling him; if he did not wake him, he does not break the oath, and that is the chosen view. Or (he swore) not except with his permission: i.e. he swore not to speak to him except with his permission. He breaks the oath in both cases: First, he swore not to speak to someone, and he is asleep so he woke him by calling him, he breaks the oath because he spoke to him and made him hear. If he did not wake him, but he was such that he would hear if he were not asleep, he breaks the oath according to Imam Abu Hanifa contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Second, if he swore not to speak except with his permission, and he gave permission but the swearer did not know of the permission until he spoke, he breaks the oath because 'permission' (idhn) is derived from 'adhan' which means informing, or from falling into the ear, and all that is not realized except after knowledge. Abu Yusuf said he does not break the oath, because permission is release, and it is completed by the act of permitting, like consent in the case where he swore not to speak except with his satisfaction, and the person satisfied, but the swearer did not know, so he spoke, he does not break the oath because satisfaction is completed by the one satisfied. We said: satisfaction is an act of the heart, unlike permission, because it derives from 'adhan' which is informing, and it is not realized without knowledge.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage addresses two subcases of an oath not to speak to someone. First, if the swearer swears not to speak to a person, and that person is asleep, waking him by calling him counts as speaking because the swearer intentionally makes himself heard. According to the chosen view, if he wakes him, he breaks the oath; if he speaks without waking him (e.g., while the other is in deep sleep), he does not break the oath. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that if the sleeper would normally hear if awake, then speaking to him while he sleeps still constitutes a breach, but the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree. Second, if the swearer swears not to speak to someone except with his permission, and the person gives permission without the swearer's knowledge, then the swearer breaks the oath when he speaks. The reasoning is that the word 'idhn' (permission) derives from 'adhan' (announcement) or from falling into the ear, both implying knowledge. Abu Yusuf argues that permission is merely a release or authorization, similar to consent (rida), which is effective even without the swearer's knowledge (as in the case of satisfaction). The Hanafi response distinguishes that satisfaction is internal, while permission is an external act requiring knowledge.

Key Terms
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission given by the person to whom the oath relates, interpreted as requiring knowledge for effectiveness.
الرضاء (al-rida): Satisfaction, an internal state of the heart, effective without the swearer's knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position of the Hanafi school, if a person swears not to speak to someone and then speaks to that person while they are asleep, but does not wake them, does the oath break?
A) Yes, because speaking occurred.
B) No, because the sleeper was not woken.
C) Yes, because the sleeper would have heard if awake.
D) No, because the sleeper's hearing is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
In oaths not to speak, waking a sleeping person counts as speech, but speaking without waking does not break the oath according to the preferred view; permission requires the swearer's knowledge to be effective.

Start of a Month-Long Oath: Indefinite vs Definite Period
Ruling on start of month-long oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an indefinite 'month' in an oath starts immediately from the time of swearing, whereas a definite 'the month' applies to the remainder of the current month.

Core Idea
An indefinite month begins at the oath; a definite month binds the remaining days.

This lesson examines a key principle in Hanafi fiqh regarding oaths: how to determine the start of a month-long period. The passage draws a sharp distinction between using the indefinite form 'a month' (shahr) and the definite form 'the month' (al-shahr). The ruling hinges on linguistic form and the implied context of anger.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath formula
Note whether the swearer said 'shahr' (indefinite) or 'al-shahr' (definite). The ruling changes accordingly.
2
Apply the indefinite rule
If indefinite, the oath begins from the moment of swearing, covering the next full 30 days from that instant, regardless of the current month.
The rationale is that the indefinite form indicates a new period following the oath, as confirmed by context of anger.
3
Apply the definite rule
If definite (e.g., 'the month'), the oath applies only to the remainder of the current (lunar) month.
4
Distinguish from affirmative oaths
Contrast with an oath to do something positive (e.g., 'I will fast a month') – if left indefinite, the oath becomes perpetual because affirmative acts require specification.
Negative oaths (like not speaking) are all-encompassing by nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يُكلمه شهراً فهو من حين حلف لا يتكلم فقرأ القرآن أو سبح لم يحنث يوم أكلم فلاناً: أي لو حلف لا يتكلم على الجديدين فإن عنى النهار خاصة. أو هلل أو كبر سبح بالإشارة ولا الكتابة، فلو كاتبه أو أرسل إليه رسولاً أو أشار إليه لا يحنث خلافاً لأحمد، وعن مالك: في الكتابة يحنث لا في الرسالة وفي الإشارة عنه روايتان ولو سلم على جماعة والمحلوف عليه فيهم حنث؛ لأن السلام للجميع فإن نواهم دونه دين ولو قال: السلام عليكم إلا واحداً لا يحنث، ولو قرع المحلوف عليه الباب، فقال الحالف: "من هذا ؟" يحنث، ولو ناداه المحلوف عليه، فقال: "لبيك"، أو "لبى" بلا كاف يحنث، ولو كلمه بكلامه ولا يفهمه المحلوف عليه فيه اختلاف الروايتين. فهو من حين حلف: أي من حلف لا يكلم فلاناً شهراً، فابتداء اليمين يكون من وقت حلف الحالف، وهذا إذا نكر لفظ الشهر، ولو عرفه بأن قال: لا يكلمه الشهر كان اليمين على باقي الشهر، وأما في صورة التنكير فالمراد الوقت الذي يلي يمينه بدلالة الحال وهو الغيظ الذي لحقه في الحال فمنع عنه عن التكلم بخلاف قوله: "والله لأصومن شهرا"؛ لأنه لو لم يذكر الشهر يتأبد اليمين؛ لأن قوله: "لأصومن" إثباتي، وقوله: "لا يتكلم" عدمي والعدمي يستغرق دون الإثباتي. لم يحنث أي من حلف لا يتكلم، فقرأ القرآن أو سبح لم يحنث، وقال الشافعي: يحنث مطلقاً؛ لأنه كلام حقيقة ولنا: أنه في الصلاة ليس بكلام عرفاً ولا شرعاً، قال علي: "إن صلاتنا هذه لا يصلح فيها شيء من كلام الناس"، وفي غير الصلاة لا يسمى متكلماً عرفاً بل يسمى قارئاً ومسبحاً، ومثل التسبيح التكبير والتهليل.
If he swore not to speak to him for a month, it is from the time he swore. If he swore not to speak and then recited Quran or glorified (Allah), he does not break the oath. 'The day I speak to so-and-so' meaning if he swore not to speak, on the two new (interpretations) if he meant the daytime specifically. Or if he said 'there is no god but Allah' or 'Allahu Akbar', he glorified by gesture and not by writing. So if he wrote to him, or sent a messenger, or gestured to him, he does not break the oath contrary to Ahmad. From Malik: in writing he breaks, not in sending a message, and regarding gesture there are two narrations. If he greeted a group and the person subject to the oath was among them, he breaks the oath because the greeting is for all; if he intended them without him, he is a sinner. If he said 'peace be upon you except one', he does not break. If the person subject to the oath knocked on the door and the swearer said 'Who is this?', he breaks. If the person called him and he said 'Labbaik' or 'Labbay' without the 'kaf', he breaks. If he spoke to him with his speech but the person did not understand, there is a difference of two narrations. It (the month) is from the time he swore: meaning if he swore not to speak to someone for a month, the beginning of the oath is from the time of swearing, and this is when the word 'month' is indefinite (nakirah). If he made it definite by saying 'not speak to him the month', the oath is on the remainder of the month. In the indefinite form, the intended meaning is the time following his oath by indication of the situation, which is the anger that befell him at that moment preventing him from speaking, unlike if he said 'By Allah, I will fast a month' because if he did not mention the month, the oath would be perpetual; because 'I will fast' is affirmative and 'I will not speak' is negative, and the negative is all-encompassing without the affirmative. He does not break: meaning if he swore not to speak and recited Quran or glorified, he does not break the oath. Al-Shafi'i said: he breaks absolutely because it is speech in reality. Our argument: in prayer it is not considered speech customarily or legally; Ali said 'this prayer of ours is not proper for any speech of people', and outside prayer he is not called a speaker customarily but is called a reciter and glorifier. Similar to glorification are takbir and tahlil.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive treatment of various issues related to oaths of not speaking. It begins by clarifying the start of a month-long oath: it begins at the moment of swearing when 'month' is indefinite (nakirah). If definite (ma'rifah, e.g., 'the month'), it applies to the remainder of the current month. The reason for the difference is that in the indefinite form, the situation (like anger) dictates that the oath takes effect immediately. The passage then addresses what constitutes speech in such an oath. Reciting Quran, saying tasbih (subhanallah), tahlil (la ilaha illallah), takbir (allahu akbar) do not break the oath because they are not considered speech customarily or legally, especially in prayer. Al-Shafi'i disagrees, holding that any utterance of words is speech. The Hanafi proof includes the hadith of Ali that prayer is not for human speech. Outside prayer, such utterances are classified as dhikr, not speech. The passage also covers gestures, writing, sending messengers, salutations, knocking, responding to calls, and speaking in a language the other does not understand. In each case, the ruling depends on whether the action is considered 'speech' (kalam) in the relevant context. For example, writing is not speech, so no breach; but answering 'Who is this?' when the person knocks is considered speech. Greeting a group containing the person breaks the oath because the greeting addresses all. The passage concludes with the ruling on indefinite vs. definite month and the distinction between affirmative and negative oaths.

Key Terms
التنكير (al-tankir): Using the noun in an indefinite form (e.g., 'shahr') indicates the oath begins immediately.
التعريف (al-ta'rif): Using the definite form (e.g., 'al-shahr') refers to the current month and applies to its remainder.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

The indefinite form in a negative oath starts from the moment of swearing; the definite form applies to the current month's remainder.

Does Reciting Quran Break an Oath Not to Speak? Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Hanafi ruling on speech in oaths with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on what constitutes speech in oaths, distinguish it from the Shafi'i view, and apply the rule to recitation, dhikr, gestures, and writing.

Core Idea
Reciting Quran or tasbih does not break an oath not to speak; only conversational speech does.

This lesson addresses a classic dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: does reciting Quran or engaging in dhikr break an oath of silence? The Hanafi school holds that such acts are not 'speech' (kalam) in the relevant legal sense, supported by the hadith of Ali and customary usage. The passage also extends this principle to gestures, writing, and other non-verbal communications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act in question
The passage considers Quran recitation, tasbih, tahlil, takbir, gestures, writing, and sending messengers. Determine whether each is classified as kalam (speech) or not.
2
Understand the Hanafi definition of kalam
Kalam is communicative speech intended for conversation. Dhikr and Quran recitation are not considered kalam, especially in prayer.
The hadith of Ali: 'This prayer of ours is not proper for any speech of people.'
3
Apply the definition to ritual utterances
Reciting Quran, saying subhanallah, la ilaha illallah, Allahu akbar – these do not break the oath because they are acts of worship, not conversational speech.
Even outside prayer, such a person is called a 'reciter' or 'glorifier', not a 'speaker'.
4
Apply to non-verbal acts
Gesturing, writing, sending a message – these are not speech, so no breach. However, responding to a knock with 'Who is this?' or saying 'Labbaik' when called is considered speech.
There is a difference of narration on whether speaking in an unintelligible language counts.
5
Contrast with the Shafi'i view
Al-Shafi'i considers all verbal utterances as speech in reality (haqiqah), so any utterance breaks the oath. The Hanafi response relies on custom and legal usage.
The practical difference (thamarah) is that under Hanafi, no expiation is needed for such recitations; under Shafi'i, expiation is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If he swore not to speak to him for a month, it is from the time he swore. If he swore not to speak and then recited Quran or glorified (Allah), he does not break the oath. 'The day I speak to so-and-so' meaning if he swore not to speak, on the two new (interpretations) if he meant the daytime specifically. Or if he said 'there is no god but Allah' or 'Allahu Akbar', he glorified by gesture and not by writing. Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive treatment of various issues related to oaths of not speaking. It begins by clarifying the start of a month-long oath: it begins at the moment of swearing when 'month' is indefinite (nakirah). If definite (ma'rifah, e.g., 'the month'), it applies to the remainder of the current month. The reason for the difference is that in the indefinite form, the situation (like anger) dictates that the oath takes effect immediately. The passage then addresses what constitutes speech in such an oath. Reciting Quran, saying tasbih (subhanallah), tahlil (la ilaha illallah), takbir (allahu akbar) do not break the oath because they are not considered speech customarily or legally, especially in prayer. Al-Shafi'i disagrees, holding that any utterance of words is speech. The Hanafi proof includes the hadith of Ali that prayer is not for human speech. Outside prayer, such utterances are classified as dhikr, not speech. The passage also covers gestures, writing, sending messengers, salutations, knocking, responding to calls, and speaking in a language the other does not understand. In each case, the ruling depends on whether the action is considered 'speech' (kalam) in the relevant context. For example, writing is not speech, so no breach; but answering 'Who is this?' when the person knocks is considered speech. Greeting a group containing the person breaks the oath because the greeting addresses all. The passage concludes with the ruling on indefinite vs. definite month and the distinction between affirmative and negative oaths.

Key Terms
الكلام (al-kalam): Speech in the legal sense: communicative utterance that is not dhikr or Quranic recitation.

Oath to Speak at Night: Meaning of 'Laylah'
Explanation that 'laylah' in an oath means only nighttime specifically

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for an oath using the singular 'laylah' and distinguish it from the plural form and the word 'yawm'.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, 'laylah' means only nighttime; speaking during the day does not break the oath.

When you swear using time words like 'night', the precise legal meaning matters. In Hanafi fiqh, the singular 'laylah' retains its literal meaning of nighttime only, unlike 'yawm' which can sometimes denote absolute time. This lesson clarifies the default ruling and highlights the contrast with the plural form and the role of intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath wording
Recognize that the oath uses the singular 'laylah' followed by an action (e.g., 'the night I speak to so-and-so').
The word 'laylah' is the key term determining the scope.
2
Recall the literal meaning
In Arabic, 'layl' refers to the period of darkness from sunset to dawn, just as 'nahar' refers to daylight.
This literal meaning is the starting point for the ruling.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule
The oath is confined to nighttime; speaking during the day does not break the oath.
This is the default ruling unless intention indicates otherwise.
4
Contrast with plural and 'yawm'
The plural 'layali' encompasses both night and day because it refers to multiple nights and their corresponding days. The word 'yawm' in its singular form can sometimes denote absolute time in certain contexts.
This highlights why singular 'laylah' is unique.
5
Consider the exception of intention
If the swearer intended absolute time by 'laylah', his intention is respected, but the default is night only.
Intention can override the literal meaning, but it must be proven.

Source Text

'Night I speak to him': meaning if he said: 'The night I speak to so-and-so, my slave is free', the oath is specifically on the night, so if he speaks to him during the day, he does not break his oath; because 'layl' (night) is literally the darkness of night specifically, just as 'nahar' (day) is for the whiteness specifically. And its usage for absolute time does not occur when mentioned in the singular form. When mentioned in the plural form, it is not specific to the darkness of night because the plural form encompasses what corresponds to it of days, unlike the singular, contrary to 'yawm'. Commentary
This passage clarifies the ruling for an oath using the word 'laylah' (a single night). Unlike 'yawm', which can be interpreted as absolute time when associated with non-extended actions, 'laylah' retains its literal meaning of the nighttime period only. Therefore, if one swears 'the night I speak to so-and-so, my slave is free', and then speaks during the day, the oath is not broken. The reason given is that 'layl' is a name for the darkness of night specifically, just as 'nahar' is for the brightness of day. The singular form does not extend to cover the opposite, unlike the plural 'layali' which encompasses both night and day. This distinguishes it from 'yawm', which in its singular form can denote absolute time in certain contexts. The commentator also notes that if the swearer intends absolute time by his statement, his intention is respected, but the default ruling is that it applies only to night.

Key Terms

نهار (nahar): Daytime, the period of light from dawn to sunset.

Oaths with Conditional Clauses: 'Except' and 'Until'
Four scenarios of conditional oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'illa an' as an exception and as a limit (ghayah) in oaths, and apply the Hanafi ruling to determine whether an oath is broken when speech occurs before or after the specified event.

Core Idea
'Illa an' and 'hatta' in oaths function as limits; the oath is broken only if the act precedes the event.

Oaths often include conditional phrases like 'except that' or 'until'. In Hanafi fiqh, these are not treated as exceptions but as time limits (ghayah) when the events are not homogeneous. This lesson walks through four common scenarios and explains why the oath remains binding until the limit is reached, and terminates thereafter—supported by a Quranic precedent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four oath scenarios
The passage presents four combinations: 'illa an' with arrival, 'hatta' with arrival, 'illa an' with permission, and 'hatta' with permission. Note the verbatim wording for each.
All four share the same structure: swearing not to speak until a condition is met.
2
Determine the ruling when speech occurs before the event
In all four scenarios, if the swearer speaks before the specified event (arrival or permission), the oath is broken. Reason: the limit (ghayah) has not yet occurred, so the oath remains in force.
The consequence (e.g., slave free) applies immediately.
3
Determine the ruling when speech occurs after or simultaneously with the event
If speech occurs after the event, or at the exact same time, the oath is not broken. The limit ends the oath's restriction.
Simultaneity is treated as after the limit, not before.
4
Analyze why 'illa an' functions as a limit here
True exception (istithna') requires homogeneity between the excluded and the thing excluded. Since speaking and arriving/permission are not homogeneous, 'illa an' cannot be exception; it must be interpreted as a limit. 'Hatta' is inherently a limit particle.
The Quranic verse (al-Ahzab 33:53) uses 'illa an' in a similar limit sense.
5
Apply the rule to new scenarios
Given any oath with 'illa an' or 'hatta' followed by a time-bound event, determine if the condition is homogeneous. If not, treat as ghayah. The oath ends at the event and speech thereafter is permissible.
If the condition is homogeneous, it may be a true exception (less common in oaths).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن كلمته إلخ: ههنا أربع صور: الأولى حلف إن كلمت فلاناً إلا أن يقدم زيد الثانية: حلف: إن كلمت فلاناً حتى يقدم زيد، الثالثة حلف إن كلمت فلاناً إلا أن يأذن زيد الرابعة: حلف: إن كلمت فلاناً حتى يأذن زيد والجزاء في كلها قوله: "فكذا" أي فعبدي حر مثلاً، فكلم الحالف فلاناً قبل قدوم زيد في الأولى والثانية، أو كلم فلاناً قبل إذنه في الثالثة والرابعة، حنث في الوجوه كلها؛ لبقاء اليمين قبل وجود الغاية. (مستخلص، عيني) أو حتى أي أو قال: إن كلمته حتى يقدم زيد (عيني) أو إلا : أي أو قال: إن كلمته إلا أن يأذن زيد. (عيني) فكذا: أي أو قال: إن كلمته حتى يأذن زيد فامرأته طالق أو عبده حر. (مستخلص، فتح)
'If I speak to him etc.': Here are four scenarios: First: he swore 'If I speak to so-and-so except that Zaid arrives'; Second: he swore 'If I speak to so-and-so until Zaid arrives'; Third: he swore 'If I speak to so-and-so except that Zaid permits'; Fourth: he swore 'If I speak to so-and-so until Zaid permits'. And the consequence in all is his statement 'then such' meaning 'my slave is free' for example. So if the swearer spoke to so-and-so before Zaid's arrival in the first and second, or before Zaid's permission in the third and fourth, he breaks his oath in all cases because the oath remains before the existence of the limit. (Mukhtasar, Ayni) 'Or until' meaning he said 'If I speak to him until Zaid arrives' (Ayni) 'Or except' meaning he said 'If I speak to him except that Zaid permits' (Ayni) 'Then such' meaning he said 'If I speak to him until Zaid permits, my wife is divorced or my slave is free' (Ayni) 'Or his permission' meaning he spoke before Zaid's permission. (Ayni) He breaks his oath in all cases because of the remaining of the oath. (Ayni) 'And after it, no': meaning if he speaks after Zaid's arrival and permission, he does not break his oath, and likewise if his speech and Zaid's arrival are simultaneous. The reason for no breaking: that arrival and permission become a limit for the oath due to the inclusion of the particle of limit in them. As for 'hatta' it is apparent because it is for limit. As for 'illa an', its origin is for exception, but it is borrowed for condition and limit when exception is impossible. Exception is impossible due to lack of homogeneity between what precedes and what follows. So when exception is impossible and it enters upon that which is not time-bound, it is for condition; and if it enters upon that which is time-bound, it is for limit. In the case of the book, exception is impossible due to lack of homogeneity between permission and speech, so it is interpreted as limit because it entered upon the oath, and the oath accepts limit, as if he swore 'I will not speak to him until Rajab'. So interpreting it as limit is more appropriate than interpreting it as condition. Thus the oath ends with Zaid's arrival and permission, so he does not break. And this is like the statement of Allah Most High: 'And do not enter the houses of the Prophet except when you are permitted' (al-Ahzab 33:53), which is interpreted as limit. (Mukhtasar, Fath)
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage examines four common conditional oaths: using 'illa an' (except that) or 'hatta' (until) with either an arrival or a permission event. The commentator explains that if the oath-taker speaks before the specified event occurs, he breaks the oath because the condition (the event) has not yet materialized. However, if he speaks after the event, or simultaneously with it, the oath is not broken. The reason is that 'hatta' is a clear limit particle, while 'illa an' is originally for exception but is used here as a limit because there is no homogeneity between the action (speaking) and the event (arrival/permission), making true exception impossible. The Quranic verse (33:53) is cited as evidence that 'illa an' can indicate a limit after which the prohibition ends. The commentator concludes that interpreting 'illa an' as a limit is more appropriate than as a condition, as the oath naturally accepts a time limit (ghayah).

Key Terms
غاية (ghayah): A limit or end point for an oath; occurrence of the event terminates the oath's restriction.
إلا أن (illa an): A compound particle that here functions as a limit (ghayah) rather than exception, due to lack of homogeneity between the prohibited action and the condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) No, because 'hatta' indicates a limit, and after the limit the oath is no longer binding

In Hanafi fiqh, 'illa an' and 'hatta' in oaths create a limit; the oath is only broken if the prohibited act occurs before the specified event.

Oaths Using Yawm and Layla: Day vs Night
Ruling on the scope of oaths with day and night

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the scope of oaths using 'yawm' and 'layla', and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement concerning the swearer's intention in court.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, 'yawm' defaults to 24 hours but intention is accepted; 'layla' in singular is exclusively night.

Oaths sworn using time words require careful interpretation. The Hanafi school distinguishes between 'yawm' (day) and 'layla' (night) in their linguistic defaults. This lesson unpacks the ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, including the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on whether the swearer's intended meaning is accepted in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath phrase and the contested word
Read the passage and identify the exact wording of the oath (e.g., 'yawm ukallimuhu' or 'layla ukallimuhu'). Determine which time word is used and its form (singular/plural).
The ruling depends on whether the word is 'yawm' or 'layla' and its grammatical number.
2
Determine the default meaning based on linguistic usage
For 'yawm' in the singular combined with a non-extended action, the default is absolute time (24 hours). For 'layla' in the singular, the default is exclusively the night period. Understand that 'yawm' can be used for daytime specifically, but 'layla' in singular is not used for absolute time.
The passage contrasts 'yawm' with 'layla' in their customary and literal meanings.
3
Apply the principle that the swearer is believed in his intention
If the swearer claims he intended a specific part (e.g., only the daytime), he is believed both religiously and in court according to the relied-upon position, because his intention is a valid interpretation of his words.
Abu Yusuf disagrees regarding judicial acceptance.
4
Note the exception for 'layla' in the singular
When 'layla' is used in the singular, the oath is contracted upon the night only. Speaking during the day does not break the oath. This is because the singular form does not extend to the corresponding daytime, unlike the plural 'layali' which does.
The passage provides the reason: 'layla' is the opposite of 'yawm' and is not used for absolute time in the singular.
5
Compare with Abu Yusuf's position on judicial belief
Abu Yusuf holds that the swearer is not believed in court when he intends a meaning contrary to the apparent customary usage. Understand the basis: the word's customary meaning takes precedence over a private intention in a judicial setting.
The matn (author) omits 'judicially' to signal the ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صدق، و"ليلة أكلمه على الليل. إن كلمته إلا أن يقدم زيد أو حتى أو إلا أن يأذن أو حتى فكذا، فكلّم قبل قدومه أو إذنه حنث، وبعدهما لا، وإن مات الخالف فلانا أي قدوم زيد أي لا يحنث صدق [أي قضاء وديانة وقيل لا يصدق قضاء. رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٠/١] لم يقل المصنف: "قضاء"؛ لما فيه من الخلاف أي لو قال :الحالف نويت بقولي يوم أكلم النهار خاصة صدق في القضاء لما قلنا: إن اليوم يستعمل للوقت المطلق إذا قرن بالفعل الغير الممتد، ومطلق الوقت يتناول الليل والنهار، فلما قال :الحالف نويت النهار خاصة يصدق؛ لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه، وعن أبي يوسف : أنه لا يصدق قضاء؛ لأنه نوى خلاف الظاهر المتعارف. (مستخلص) ليلة أكلمه: أي لو قال: "ليلة أكلم فلاناً فعبدي حر" فاليمين على الليل خاصة حتى لو كلمه نهاراً لا يحنث؛ لأن الليل حقيقة في سواد الليل خاصة كالنهار للبياض خاصة، ولم يجئ استعماله في مطلق الوقت إذا ذكر بلفظ المفرد، وإذا ذكر بلفظ الجمع فلا يختص سواد الليل؛ لأن صيغة الجمع ينتظم ما بإزائه من الأيام، ولا كذلك المفرد بخلاف اليوم، وهما ضدان. [تبيين الحقائق: ٤٨٤/٣] (فتح، مستخلص) على الليل: أي تنعقد يمينه على الليل وحدها ما لم ينو به مطلق الوقت. (ط)
He is believed (in his intention). And 'night I will speak to him' is upon the night. 'If I speak to him except that Zaid arrives, or until, or except that he permits, or until, then such-and-such.' So he spoke before his arrival or his permission, he broke the oath; after both, no. And if the one who made the condition die? i.e., Zaid's arrival, meaning no breaking of oath. He is believed judicially and religiously; and it is said he is not believed judicially. The author did not say 'judicially' because of the difference of opinion. That is, if the swearer said: 'By my saying 'day I will speak' I intended only the daytime,' he is believed in court because we say: 'day' is used for absolute time when combined with a non-extended action, and absolute time includes day and night. So when the swearer said he intended only the daytime, he is believed because he intended the literal meaning of his words. And from Abu Yusuf: he is not believed in court because he intended contrary to the apparent customary usage. (Mukhtalas) 'Night I will speak to him': that is, if he said: 'By night I will speak to Zaid, and my slave is free,' the oath is specific to night, so if he spoke to him during the day, he does not break the oath; because 'night' in reality is specifically the darkness of night, just as 'day' is specifically the whiteness of day. And its usage for absolute time has not come when mentioned in the singular. But when mentioned in the plural, it does not exclusively apply to the darkness of night, because the plural form encompasses the corresponding days, unlike the singular. This differs from 'day,' and they are opposites. (Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: 3/484) (Fath, Mukhtalas) 'Upon the night': that is, his oath is contracted upon the night alone unless he intends absolute time. (T)
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses the scope of oaths using the words 'yawm' (day) and 'layla' (night). The author explains that if someone swears using 'yawm' in a way that does not imply an extended action, the default is that 'yawm' refers to the full 24-hour period (similar to 'absolute time'). However, if the swearer later claims he intended only the daytime (nahar), he is believed both religiously and in court, because the word 'yawm' can be used for the daytime specifically. This is based on linguistic usage. There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf holds that he is not believed in court because his intended meaning contradicts the customary apparent meaning. For 'layla' (night) in the singular, the oath is restricted to the night only, so speaking during the day does not break the oath. This is because 'layla' is not used for absolute time in the singular, unlike 'yawm'. When 'layla' is used in the plural, it can cover the corresponding days.

Key Terms
اليوم (al-yawm): In this context, 'yawm' refers to the full 24-hour period but can be specified to daytime by intention.
الليلة (al-layla): In the singular, 'layla' specifically means the night period, not the full day.
صدق (saddaqa): The swearer is believed in his stated intention regarding the scope of his oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'Bi'l-layli ukallimuhu' (by night I will speak to him) and then speaks during the day, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because the oath covers any time.
B) No, because 'layla' in the singular refers only to night.
C) Yes, because speaking during the day is still within the general time.
D) No, unless he specifically intended night only.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school gives weight to the linguistic default for time words in oaths but also accepts the swearer's intention, with a debate on judicial acceptance.

Oaths Tied to Property and Change of Ownership: Intra-Hanafi Disputes
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oaths and change of ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether an oath on a specific person's property is broken after ownership changes, and identify the points of disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad/Zufar, as well as between the two elders and Abu Yusuf regarding the death of the conditional person.

Core Idea
When ownership ends, the oath ends; pointing does not bind the oath to the item itself.

Oaths often involve property owned by others. What happens if the owner sells the property? Does the swearer break the oath by using it? The Hanafi school has an internal debate, primarily between the two elders (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Muhammad with Zufar. Additionally, there is a dispute about oaths conditioned on a person's arrival or permission when that person dies. This lesson unpacks these disputes and the underlying principles.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the scenario
The swearer says 'I will not eat the food of Zaid' and points to a specific loaf. Then Zaid sells that loaf.
The oath includes both addition (to Zaid) and pointing (to the specific loaf).
2
State the position of the two elders
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule that the oath is not broken after the sale, because the addition to Zaid ceases and the condition for breaking is not met.
They consider the addition (idafah) to be effective.
3
Contrast with Muhammad and Zufar
For slaves and houses, Muhammad and Zufar rule that the oath is broken even after change of ownership, because the pointing (isharah) overrides the addition.
They prioritize the physical identification over the owner's relationship.
4
Analyze the rationales
The two elders argue that the motive for the oath is the harm from the owner, not the item itself. Muhammad and Zufar argue that pointing is stronger for identification.
The two elders' reasoning is based on ta'lil (cause), while the others focus on linguistic strength.
5
Apply to the death of the conditional person
If the oath is conditioned on Zaid's arrival or permission, and Zaid dies, the two elders say the oath falls because the possibility of fulfillment is lost. Abu Yusuf disagrees, holding that the oath becomes permanent.
This is another intra-Hanafi dispute within the same chapter.
6
Note the consensus on mutajaddid
All scholars agree that if the swearer did not point, newly acquired items of the same type are not covered by the oath.
This is the principle of al-mutajaddid (newly acquired property).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يأكل طعام فلان، أو لا يدخل داره، أو لا يلبس ثوبه، أو لا يركب دابته، أو لا يكلم عبده إن أشار وزال ملكه ففعل لا يحنث كما في المتجدد أي دابة فلان زيد: الذي قدومه شرط في الوجه الأول والثاني، وإذنه شرط في الثالث والرابع أي مات قبل القدوم والإذن. (عيني) سقط الحلف أي عند الطرفين؛ لأن الممنوع عنه الكلام المنتهي بالإذن والقدوم ، و لم يبق بعد الموت متصور الوجود، ففات إمكان تصور البر وهو شرط للانعقاد ابتداء وبقاء فسقطت اليمين، وعند أبي يوسف: لما لم يكن تصور البر شرطاً تتأبد اليمين عند سقوط الغاية، ففي أي وقت كلم فلاناً ولو بعد موت زيد حنث عنده ولا يحنث عندهما، والأصل أن الحالف إذا جعل ليمينه غاية وفاتت الغاية بطلت اليمين عندهما خلافاً له. (فتح، عيني) لا يأكل طعام فلان إلخ: أي من حلف لا يأكل طعام فلان، أو لا يدخل داره، وكذا البواقي، وأشار إلى الطعام بعينه، أو إلى دار بعينها، أو إلى ثوب بعينه، أو إلى دابة بعينها، أو إلى عبد بعينه، فزال ملك فلان . هذه الأشياء عن بأن باعها أو وهبها ثم أكل الحالف ذلك الطعام، أو دخل تلك الدار، أو لبس ذلك الثوب، أو ركب تلك الدابة، أو كلم ذلك العبد بعد زوال ملكه لا يحنث عند الشيخين؛ لأن للإضافة تأثيراً كما للإشارة فيعتبران، ويكون شرط الحنث أكل الطعام المضاف إلى فلان. وكذا البواقي، فلم ،يوجد، فبطلت اليمين، وعند محمد و زفر يحنث في العبد والدار؛ لأن الإضافة للتعريف، والإشارة أبلغ في التعريف من الإضافة؛ لكونها قاطعة للشركة، فاعتبرت الإشارة ولغت الإضافة، فصار كالمرأة والصديق في المسألة الآتية، فتعلقت اليمين بالعين، وبه قالت الثلاثة ولهما أن الداعي إلى اليمين لمعنى في المضاف إليه؛ لأن هذه الأشياء لا تعادى ولا تهجر لذواتها، بل لأذى من ملاكها، وكذا العبد؛ لأنه لخسته ألحق بالجمادات، فكانت الإضافة معتبرة، وتتقيد اليمين بحال قيام الملك، فلا يحنث بعد زواله. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني) أو لا يدخل أي أو حلف لا يدخل دار فلان (عيني) أو لا يلبس أي أو حلف لا يلبس ثوب فلان. (عيني) أو لا يكلم أي لو حلف لا يكلم عبد فلان عيني) إن أشار : أي ينظر إن أشار الحالف بأن قال: لا أكل طعام فلان هذا، وكذا في جميع الصور الباقية. (ط، عيني) وزال ملكه أي ملك فلان عن هذه الأشياء بأن باعها ففعل الحالف بعد ذلك بأن أكل ذلك الطعام أو دخل تلك الدار، وكذا البواقي. (عيني) لا يحنث: لأن إضافة الملك قد زالت فبطلت اليمين خلافاً لمحمد. (عيني) كما في المتجدد: [ أي كما لا يحنث بالمستحدث من الطعام والدار وغيرهما من هذه الأشياء إجماعاً. (ط)] أي كما لا يحنث إن تجدد الملك في مثل هذه الأشياء إجماعاً، فلو حلف لا يكلم عبد فلان هذا، فاشترى فلان عبداً آخر فكلم الحالف، لم يحنث، وكذا إذا اشترى طعاماً آخر أو داراً أخرى أو ثوباً آخر أو عبداً آخر؛ لأن حلفه وقع على شيء مملوك لفلان وقت الحلف، وهذه ليست كذلك ؛ لأنها ليست بمشار إليها. (مستخلص، عيني)
He does not eat the food of so-and-so, or does not enter his house, or does not wear his garment, or does not ride his mount, or does not speak to his slave. If he pointed (to the item) and his ownership ceased, and then he did (the act), he does not break the oath, as in the case of newly acquired property. That is, the mount of so-and-so Zaid: whose arrival is a condition in the first and second issues, and his permission in the third and fourth, i.e., if he died before arrival and permission. (Ayni) The oath falls (becomes void) according to both parties, because the prohibited thing is speech that ends with permission and arrival, and after death there remains no conceivable existence. So the possibility of fulfilling the oath (al-birr) is lost, and that is a condition for the validity of the oath initially and perpetually, so the oath falls. According to Abu Yusuf, since the possibility of fulfilling is not a condition, the oath becomes permanent when the limit disappears, so whenever he speaks to so-and-so, even after Zaid's death, he breaks the oath according to him; but according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf in the latter view?) he does not break. The principle is that if the swearer sets a limit for his oath and the limit disappears, the oath becomes void according to the two, contrary to him (Abu Yusuf). (Fath, Ayni) 'He does not eat the food of so-and-so', etc.: i.e., whoever swears not to eat the food of so-and-so, or not to enter his house, and similarly the rest, and he pointed to the specific food, or to a specific house, or to a specific garment, or to a specific mount, or to a specific slave, and then the ownership of so-and-so over these things ceases by his selling or gifting them, and then the swearer eats that food, enters that house, wears that garment, rides that mount, or speaks to that slave after the cessation of ownership, he does not break the oath according to the two elders (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) because the addition (idafah) has effect just as the pointing has effect, and both are considered. The condition for breaking the oath is eating the food that is added (ascribed) to so-and-so. Similarly for the rest, and it (the condition) is not found, so the oath becomes void. According to Muhammad and Zufar, he breaks the oath in the case of the slave and the house, because the addition is for identification, and pointing is stronger in identification than addition because it removes ambiguity. So the pointing is considered and the addition is disregarded, making it like the case of a woman and a friend in the following issue. Thus the oath attaches to the specific entity, and the three (Muhammad, Zufar, and Abu Yusuf?) said this. But according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), the motive for the oath is a meaning pertaining to the one to whom it is added, because these things are not hated or avoided for themselves, but due to the harm of their owners. Similarly, the slave, due to his lowness, is attached to inanimate objects. Thus the addition is considered, and the oath is restricted to the state of existence of ownership, so he does not break the oath after its cessation. (Mukhtalas, Fath, Ayni) 'Or does not enter', i.e., or he swore not to enter the house of so-and-so. (Ayni) 'Or does not wear', i.e., or he swore not to wear the garment of so-and-so. (Ayni) 'Or does not speak to', i.e., if he swore not to speak to the slave of so-and-so. (Ayni) 'If he pointed', i.e., consider if the swearer pointed by saying, 'I will not eat this food of so-and-so,' and similarly in all remaining scenarios. (T, Ayni) 'And his ownership ceased', i.e., the ownership of so-and-so over these things ceased by his selling them, and the swearer then did (the act) by eating that food or entering that house, etc. (Ayni) 'He does not break the oath', because the addition of ownership has ceased, so the oath becomes void, contrary to Muhammad. (Ayni) 'As in the case of newly acquired property', i.e., just as he does not break the oath by newly produced food, house, etc., by consensus. (T) i.e., just as he does not break the oath if ownership is newly acquired in similar things by consensus. So if he swore not to speak to this slave of so-and-so, then so-and-so bought another slave and the swearer spoke to him, he does not break the oath. Similarly, if he bought other food, another house, another garment, or another slave, because his oath fell upon something owned by so-and-so at the time of the oath, and these are not like that because they are not the pointed item. (Mukhtalas, Ayni)
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage deals with an oath that is tied to the property of a specific person, such as 'I will not eat the food of Zaid.' If the swearer also points to a specific item (e.g., 'this food'), and then Zaid sells that item, does the oath apply to the item after ownership changes? According to the two elders (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), the oath is tied to the addition (idafah) to the owner, so if ownership ceases, the oath becomes void and the swearer does not break it by eating that food. They argue that the motive for the oath is the harm from the owner, not the item itself. Muhammad and Zufar disagree, especially for slaves and houses: they say the pointing (isharah) is stronger, so the oath attaches to the specific entity (al-'ayn), and change of ownership does not matter. However, all agree that if the swearer did not point, and a new item of the same type is acquired, the oath does not apply. This is the principle of 'mutajaddid' (newly acquired). The passage also discusses the case where the conditional person dies before the limit; according to the two elders, the oath falls because the possibility of fulfillment is lost, but Abu Yusuf holds that the oath becomes permanent (ta'abbud). This intra-Hanafi dispute is detailed. The relied-upon position is that of the two elders.

Key Terms
الإضافة (al-idafah): The attribution of property to its owner (e.g., 'food of Zaid'). In oaths, it ties the oath to the owner's relationship.
الإشارة (al-isharah): Pointing to a specific item (e.g., 'this food'). It strengthens the connection to the specific entity.
المتجدد (al-mutajaddid): Newly acquired property of the same type after the oath; the oath does not apply to it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not eat the food of Zaid' while pointing to a specific loaf of bread, and Zaid then sells that bread, does the swearer break his oath by eating it?
A) Yes, according to all Hanafi scholars.
B) No, according to the two elders; yes according to Muhammad and Zufar for some items.
C) Yes, because the oath was on the specific bread.
D) No, because oaths are always void after ownership changes.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the oath tied to a specific person's property becomes void when ownership ceases, even if the swearer pointed to the item; the two elders' view is the mufta bihi.

Oath Void When Condition Impossible by Death
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath voiding when condition fails due to death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when an oath's condition becomes impossible due to death, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference between the two imams and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
If the condition of an oath becomes impossible, the oath drops—except according to Abu Yusuf.

When someone swears an oath conditional on a future event—like 'I will not eat until Zayd arrives'—and Zayd dies before the event occurs, does the oath remain binding? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement. This lesson unpacks the dispute and the relied-upon ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its condition
Locate the ghayah (limit or condition) in the oath, e.g., 'until Zayd arrives' or 'until Zayd permits'.
2
Understand tasawwur al-birr
Recognize that the possibility of fulfilling the oath (birr) is considered a condition for the oath's inception and continuity by the two imams.
3
State the position of the two
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani rule that the oath drops (saghat al-yamin) because tasawwur al-birr is lost.
4
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf argues that tasawwur al-birr is not a condition; thus the oath becomes perpetual and violation occurs even after death.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of the two: the oath becomes void when the limit is lost.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زيد سقط الحلفُ. لا يأكل طعام فلان، أو لا يدخل داره، أو لا يلبس ثوبه، أو لا خلافاً لأبي يوسف أي لو حلف: لا يأكل إلخ. يركب دابته، أو لا يكلم عبده إن أشار وزال ملكه ففعل لا يحنث كما في المتجدد أي دابة فلان زيد: الذي قدومه شرط في الوجه الأول والثاني، وإذنه شرط في الثالث والرابع أي مات قبل القدوم والإذن. (عيني) سقط الحلف أي عند الطرفين؛ لأن الممنوع عنه الكلام المنتهي بالإذن والقدوم ، و لم يبق بعد الموت متصور الوجود، ففات إمكان تصور البر وهو شرط للانعقاد ابتداء وبقاء فسقطت اليمين، وعند أبي يوسف: لما لم يكن تصور البر شرطاً تتأبد اليمين عند سقوط الغاية، ففي أي وقت كلم فلاناً ولو بعد موت زيد حنث عنده ولا يحنث عندهما، والأصل أن الحالف إذا جعل ليمينه غاية وفاتت الغاية بطلت اليمين عندهما خلافاً له. (فتح، عيني)
Zayd: The oath drops. He swore: I will not eat the food of so-and-so, or not enter his house, or not wear his garment, or not—contrary to Abu Yusuf—i.e. if he swore: I will not eat etc. He rides his mount, or does not speak to his slave, if he pointed and his ownership ceased, then he did it, he does not break the oath, as in the newly acquired, i.e. the mount of so-and-so Zayd: whose arrival is a condition in the first and second scenarios, and his permission is a condition in the third and fourth scenarios, i.e. he died before arrival and permission. (Ayni) The oath drops, i.e. according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), because what is prohibited is speech that ends with permission and arrival, and after death there remains no conceivable existence, so the possibility of fulfilling the oath (birr) is lost, and it is a condition for the inception and continuity, so the oath becomes void. According to Abu Yusuf: Since the possibility of fulfillment is not a condition, the oath becomes perpetual when the limit is lost, so whenever he speaks to so-and-so, even after Zayd's death, he breaks the oath according to him, but not according to the two. The principle is that if the oath-taker sets a limit for his oath and the limit is lost, the oath becomes void according to the two, contrary to Abu Yusuf. (Fath, Ayni)
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an oath that includes a condition or limit (ghayah) such as the arrival or permission of a specific person (Zayd). The example: someone swears, 'I will not eat so-and-so's food until Zayd comes' or 'I will not enter his house until Zayd permits.' If Zayd dies before the condition occurs, the two (Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani) rule that the oath drops (saghat al-yamin) because the possibility of fulfilling the oath (birr) is a condition for the oath to remain in force, and it is no longer possible. In contrast, Abu Yusuf argues that such possibility is not a condition; therefore the oath becomes perpetual, and violation occurs even after death. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of the two, as the text states 'contrary to Abu Yusuf' and gives the principle that an oath with a limit fails when the limit is lost.

Key Terms
الغاية (al-ghayah): The limit or condition upon which the oath is contingent, e.g., the arrival or permission of a person.
تصور البر (tasawwur al-birr): The possibility of fulfilling the oath in a manner that avoids sin and breaks the oath validly.
سقوط اليمين (suqut al-yamin): The oath becomes void and no longer binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not eat Zayd's food until Zayd arrives' and Zayd dies before arrival, what is the Hanafi ruling according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?
A) The oath remains in effect perpetually.
B) The oath drops and becomes void.
C) The oath applies only to the existing food.
D) The oath is suspended until the Day of Judgment.

Key Takeaway
When an oath's condition becomes impossible, the Hanafi ruling (mufta bihi) is that the oath drops and is no longer binding.

6 min
Oath on Pointed Property After Ownership Change
Ruling on oaths mentioning specific property after ownership passes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether an oath on pointed property is broken after ownership changes, and evaluate the intra-madhab dispute between the two sheikhs and Muhammad/Zufar.

Core Idea
Oath on pointed property is not broken after ownership change; addition restricts to ownership period.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute concerning oaths that both specify property by pointing ('this food') and by adding the owner's name ('of Zayd'). The key question: if the owner sells the property, does eating it break the oath? The two senior authorities (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) say no, while Muhammad and Zufar say yes for house and slave. Understanding the reasoning behind each position is crucial for applying Hanafi fiqh in domestic and commercial contexts.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the precise ruling being disputed: whether an oath that both adds the owner's name and points to a specific item is broken when the item changes ownership.
2
Identify the Hanafi position (two sheikhs)
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the oath is not broken because the addition (idafah) restricts the oath to the period of ownership. Once ownership passes, the condition for violation is lost.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Identify the opposing view
Muhammad and Zufar hold that for house and slave, the oath is broken even after ownership changes. They argue that pointing is stronger identification than addition, and these items are disliked for themselves.
They analogize to the case of woman and friend mentioned in the text.
4
Follow the talil (reasoning)
The two sheikhs argue that the motive for the oath is the owner's harm, not the item itself; addition remains effective. The opposing view prioritizes pointing as decisive.
The slave is compared to inanimate objects due to baseness.
5
Determine thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
Practical consequence: If owner sells, the oath-taker may consume without penalty according to the two sheikhs, but would break the oath according to Muhammad and Zufar for house and slave. The relied-upon position is that of the two sheikhs.
Tarjih is implicit in the text and supported by later scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يأكل طعام فلان إلخ: أي من حلف لا يأكل طعام فلان، أو لا يدخل داره، وكذا البواقي، وأشار إلى الطعام بعينه، أو إلى دار بعينها، أو إلى ثوب بعينه، أو إلى دابة بعينه، أو إلى عبد بعينه، فزال ملك فلان . هذه الأشياء عن بأن باعها أو وهبها ثم أكل الحالف ذلك الطعام، أو دخل تلك الدار، أو لبس ذلك الثوب، أو ركب تلك الدابة، أو كلم ذلك العبد بعد زوال ملكه لا يحنث عند الشيخين؛ لأن للإضافة تأثيراً كما للإشارة فيعتبران، ويكون شرط الحنث أكل الطعام المضاف إلى فلان. وكذا البواقي، فلم ،يوجد، فبطلت اليمين، وعند محمد و زفر يحنث في العبد والدار؛ لأن الإضافة للتعريف، والإشارة أبلغ في التعريف من الإضافة؛ لكونها قاطعة للشركة، فاعتبرت الإشارة ولغت الإضافة، فصار كالمرأة والصديق في المسألة الآتية، فتعلقت اليمين بالعين، وبه قالت الثلاثة ولهما أن الداعي إلى اليمين لمعنى في المضاف إليه؛ لأن هذه الأشياء لا تعادى ولا تهجر لذواتها، بل لأذى من ملاكها، وكذا العبد؛ لأنه لخسته ألحق بالجمادات، فكانت الإضافة معتبرة، وتتقيد اليمين بحال قيام الملك، فلا يحنث بعد زواله. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني)
He does not eat the food of so-and-so etc.: i.e., if someone swears he will not eat so-and-so's food, or not enter his house, and likewise the rest, and he points [by saying 'this'] to the specific food, or to a specific house, or to a specific garment, or to a specific mount, or to a specific slave, and then the ownership of so-and-so ceases from these things by his selling or giving them away, then the oath-taker ate that food, or entered that house, or wore that garment, or rode that mount, or spoke to that slave after the ownership ceased, he does not break the oath according to the two sheikhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), because the addition (idafah) has effect just like the pointing, so both are considered. The condition for breaking the oath is eating the food added to so-and-so, and likewise the rest, and that was not found, so the oath becomes void. According to Muhammad and Zufar, he does break the oath in the case of the slave and the house, because the addition is for identification, and pointing is stronger for identification than addition because it removes joint ownership, so the pointing is considered and the addition is ignored. Thus it becomes like the woman and the friend in the coming issue, so the oath is attached to the specific item. The three (Muhammad, Zufar, and Abu Yusuf in another view) said this. The two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) say that the motive for the oath is due to the meaning in the person to whom the item is added, because these things are not hated or abandoned for themselves, but because of the harm from their owners. Similarly the slave because of his base nature is annexed to inanimate objects, so addition is considered and the oath is restricted to the existence of ownership; thus he does not break it after it ceases.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding oaths that specify property by both addition (idafah, e.g., 'the food of so-and-so') and pointing (e.g., 'this food'), where the property changes ownership. According to the two sheikhs (Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf), both the addition and the pointing are effective; the condition for violation is that the property must still belong to the named person. Once ownership passes, the oath becomes void because the added item no longer exists as described. Therefore, if the oath-taker eats the same food after it is sold, he does not break his oath. However, Imam Muhammad and Imam Zufar differentiate between items: they rule that for house and slave, breaking occurs even after ownership changes. Their reasoning is that pointing is stronger for identification than addition, and since the house and slave are often disliked for themselves (the slave due to his base nature, the house for its own faults), the oath attaches to the physical item rather than the ownership relation. The two sheikhs maintain that the motive is the owner's harm, so addition remains relevant. The relied-upon position is that of the two sheikhs, as it is the safer and more consistent principle.

Key Terms
الإضافة (al-idafah): Grammatical addition linking the item to its owner, e.g., 'food of Zayd'.
الإشارة (al-isharah): Pointing to a specific item by saying 'this' in the oath.
الشيخان (al-shaykhan): Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf (the two senior authorities in the school).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two sheikhs, what is the condition for violating an oath that points to property and adds the owner's name?
A) The property must still be owned by the named person.
B) The property must be a house or slave.
C) Pointing alone determines the oath.
D) The property must be eaten or used.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when an oath combines addition and pointing, addition restricts the oath to the ownership period; thus, after ownership changes, the oath is not broken.

Oath on Friend or Wife Without Pointing
Ruling on oaths regarding a friend or wife after the relationship ends

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the ruling for an oath on a friend/wife without pointing after the relationship ends and EXPLAIN the intra-Hanafi dispute between the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
When pointing is absent in a friend/wife oath, doubt protects the oath; when pointing is present, the person is the object by consensus.

This passage tackles a nuanced scenario in oaths: if someone swears not to interact with 'the friend of Zayd' (without pointing), and later the friendship ends, does speaking to that person break the oath? The Hanafi school divides based on whether the oath includes a pointing word like 'this'. A strong consensus exists for the pointed case, but an intra-Hanafi dispute arises when pointing is omitted. The same principles apply to oaths on property that changes ownership, with an additional layered ruling from Abu Yusuf about houses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath type and its components
Read the oath formula: does it include a pointing word (e.g., 'this') or just an addition (e.g., 'of so-and-so')? Determine if the subject is a person (friend/wife) or property (food, house).
Pointing is indicated by words like 'this' (هذا/هذه).
2
Determine if pointing is present
If pointing is used, the consensus ruling applies: for friend/wife, the oath is broken after the relationship ends. For property without pointing, the oath is not broken if ownership changes, but it is broken with newly acquired items.
Pointing makes the identification direct; the addition becomes secondary.
3
Apply the default rule for the two sheikhs without pointing
When there is no pointing, the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) rule that the oath is not broken after the relationship ends, based on the possibility that the motive was the relationship, not the person. However, the oath is broken with a newly acquired friend/wife.
Abu Yusuf has a special exception for a newly acquired house: he restricts the oath to the house existing at the time.
4
Contrast Muhammad's position
Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that even without pointing, the oath is broken after the relationship ends, because the addition is merely for identification and the person is the intended object. He also rules that a newly acquired friend/wife does not break the oath.
Muhammad's view treats the addition like pointing.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and the thamarah al-khilaf
The relied-upon position is that of the two sheikhs: no breach without pointing after relationship ends, but breach with a newly acquired friend/wife. The practical difference: under the two, the oath-taker can speak after friendship ends without penalty; under Muhammad, he cannot.
Tarjih is based on the principle of doubt preserving the oath.
