وإن لم يشر لا يحنث بعد زوال الملك، وحنث بالمتجدد، وفي الصديق والزوجة في المشار حنث بعد الزوال، وفي غير المشار لا، وحنث بالمتجدد. بمباشرة ما حلف عليه الحالف بمباشرة المحلوف عليه أي لا يحنث عندهما خلافاً لمحمد وإن لم يشر إلخ أي لو قال: "لا يأكل طعام فلان" و لم يشر أي لم يقل لفظة "هذا" بعد المضاف إليه في الأمثلة المذكورة، فباع فلان الطعام وأخواته، ثم أكله الحالف بعد زوال الملك لا يحنث؛ لأنه انعقد يمينه على فعل واقع في محل مضاف إلى فلان ولم يوجد فلا يحنث، وقوله: "وحنث بالمتجدد" أي إذا حلف لا يدخل دار فلان، فباعها فلان واشترى داراً أخرى فدخلها الحالف، حنث؛ لأن يمينه انعقدت على فعل واقع في محل مضاف إلى فلان وقد وجد وكذا الحكم في أخواتها وعن أبي يوسف: أنه لا يحنث في الملك المتجدد له في الدار وحدها؛ لأن الملك لا يستحدث فيها عادة، فهي أول ما يشترى وآخر ما يباع فتقيدت اليمين المضافة إلى الدار بالقائمة في ملكه وقت ،اليمين وهما يقولان إن اللفظ مطلق، فيجري على إطلاقه، وعنه في رواية: ينعقد اليمين في الجميع بالقائم في ملكه وقت الحلف. (مستخلص، فتح) بعد زوال الملك أي بعد زوال ملك فلان عن الطعام وأخواته. (عيني) بالمتجدد لوجود شرط الحنث، وهو النسبة والإضافة إلى فلان وعدم الإشارة. (عيني) وفي الصديق والزوجة إلخ أي لو حلف لا يكلم صديق فلان هذا أو زوجة فلان هذه، وكلم بعد زوال الصداقة بين فلان وصديقه وبعد زوال الزوجية بين فلان وامرأته بالإبانة، حنث إجماعاً؛ لأن هذه الأشياء يمكن أن تهجر لذاتها، فإذا كانت الذات معتبرة كان الوصف وهو كونه مضافاً إلى فلان لغواً؛ لأن الحر مما يقصد بالهجر، فكانت الإضافة للتعريف، هذا إذا لم يقل: "فإن فلاناً عدو لي"، فإنه يحنث بعد الزوال؛ لظهور أن الداعي لمعنى في المضاف إليه، والحاصل أن الصديق والمرأة يقصدان بالهجران، فلا يشترط بقاء هذه الصفة في المشار إليه، فترجح جانب الإشارة؛ لكونها أبلغ في التعريف، ولا تأثير للإضافة فيهما، وهذا الحكم بالإجماع. (فتح عيني) في المشار: وهو قوله صديق فلان هذا أو امرأته هذه. (عيني) بعد الزوال: أي بعد زوال الصداقة والزوجية إجماعاً. وفي غير المشار :لا أي لا يحنث عند الشيخين فيما إذا قال: لا أكلم صديق فلان أو زوجته ولم يشر إليهما بقوله : "هذا" أو "هذه فزالت النسبة إليه بأن أعدى صديقه أو طلق زوجته فكلّمهما بعد ذلك، وقال محمد: يحنث؛ لأن المقصود هجران كل واحد والإضافة للتعريف، فصار كالمشار إليه في المسألة السابقة، ولهما: أن هجران كل واحد منهما لأجل الإضافة أيضاً محتمل، وترك الإشارة وعدم التسمية باسمه يدل على هذا الاحتمال، فلا يحنث مع الاحتمال بالشك. رمز الحقائق: ٣٦١/١] (فتح) وحنث بالمتجدد: [من صديق أو زوجة فيما إذا حلف لا يكلم صديق فلان أو زوجته ولم يشر. (عيني)] أي إذا حلف لا يكلم صديق فلان أو زوجته ولم يشر إليهما سواء كان له وقت اليمين صديق وزوجة أو لم يكن، يحنث بالمستحدث من صديق أو زوجة، وهذا أيضاً عند الشيخين وعند محمد لا يحنث؛ لأنه يتناول العين
And if he did not point, he does not break the oath after the ownership ceases, but he breaks with a newly acquired [item]. Regarding the friend and the wife: with the pointed one, he breaks after the relationship ends; without pointing, he does not break, but breaks with a newly acquired one. By directly doing what he swore upon, the oath-taker by directly doing the object of the oath, i.e., he does not break according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), contrary to Muhammad. 'And if he did not point' etc., i.e., if he said: 'I will not eat the food of so-and-so' and did not point, i.e., did not say the word 'this' after the add-adjective in the mentioned examples, so so-and-so sold the food and its likes, then the oath-taker ate it after ownership ceased, he does not break; because his oath was contracted on an action occurring in a place added to so-and-so, and it was not found, so he does not break. His saying: 'he breaks with a newly acquired one' means if he swore not to enter so-and-so's house, then so-and-so sold it and bought another house, and the oath-taker entered it, he breaks; because his oath was contracted on an action occurring in a place added to so-and-so, and that was found. Similarly for its analogues. From Abu Yusuf: he does not break with a newly acquired property for the house alone, because ownership is not customarily acquired new for it; it is the first thing bought and last thing sold, so the oath added to the house is restricted to the one existing in his ownership at the time of the oath. The two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) say the wording is absolute, so it runs absolutely. According to a narration from Abu Yusuf, the oath is contracted regarding all existing in his ownership at the time of the oath. (Mustakhlas, Fath) 'After the ownership ceases' i.e., after the ownership of so-and-so ceases over the food and its likes. (Ayni) 'He breaks with a newly acquired one' due to the presence of the condition for breach: the relation and addition to so-and-so and the absence of pointing. (Ayni) 'And regarding the friend and the wife' etc., i.e., if he swore not to speak to this friend of so-and-so or to this wife of so-and-so, and he spoke after the friendship between so-and-so and his friend ceased, and after the marriage bond between so-and-so and his wife ceased due to dissolution, he breaks by consensus; because these things can be abandoned for themselves. So when the essence is considered, the description (being added to so-and-so) is null. Because the free person can be intended for abandonment, so the addition is for identification. This is if he did not say: 'So-and-so is my enemy'; if he said that, he breaks after the relationship ends, because it shows the motive is due to the meaning in the added-to. The conclusion is that the friend and the woman are intended for abandonment, so the continuity of this attribute in what is pointed to is not required; thus the side of pointing prevails because it is stronger in identification, and the addition has no effect in them. This ruling is by consensus. (Fath, Ayni) 'In the pointed' i.e., his saying: this friend of so-and-so or this wife of his. (Ayni) 'After the relationship ends' i.e., after the friendship and marriage bonds cease, by consensus. 'And without pointing: no' i.e., he does not break according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) when he says: 'I will not speak to the friend of so-and-so or his wife' and did not point to them by saying 'this', then the relationship to him ended, e.g., he made his friend an enemy or divorced his wife, and he speaks to them after that. Muhammad said: he breaks, because the intention is to abandon each one, and the addition is for identification, so it becomes like the pointed one in the previous issue. The two say: abandoning each one due to the addition is also possible, and omitting the pointing and not naming him by name indicates this possibility, so he does not break with possibility by doubt. (Ramz al-Haqaiq: 361/1) (Fath) 'He breaks with a newly acquired one' i.e., from a friend or wife, in case he swore not to speak to the friend of so-and-so or his wife and did not point, whether or not he had a friend or wife at the time of the oath. He breaks with a newly acquired friend or wife. This is also according to the two; according to Muhammad he does not break because it applies to the specific person.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage first clarifies the ruling when the oath does not include pointing (e.g., saying 'I will not eat so-and-so's food' without 'this'). In such a case, if the property changes hands and the oath-taker then eats it, he does not break because the condition (the addition to so-and-so) is not met. However, if the owner acquires a new item of the same type (e.g., a new house), the oath-taker does break the oath by interacting with that new item, because the addition applies to any such item currently owned. Abu Yusuf has an exceptional ruling for the house: since houses are rarely newly acquired, the oath is restricted to the house existing at the time of the oath. The two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold the literal view that any house now owned by so-and-so is included.

Then the passage discusses oaths concerning a friend or wife. If the oath points to a specific friend/wife by saying 'this friend of so-and-so' or 'this wife of so-and-so', and the relationship ends (friendship ceases or divorce), the oath-taker breaks the oath by speaking to that person — by consensus. This is because the person himself/herself can be the object of avoidance, so the addition (being so-and-so's friend/wife) is merely identification and not a condition. However, if the oath does not point (e.g., 'I will not speak to the friend of so-and-so') and the relationship ends, the two sheikhs rule that he does not break, as the motive might be the relationship itself. Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees, considering the addition merely identificatory. If a new friend or wife is acquired by so-and-so after the oath, the oath-taker breaks by interacting with them (according to the two), even if no such person existed at the time of the oath; Muhammad holds the opposite view because the oath attaches to the specific person.

Key Terms
المشار إليه (al-mushar ilayh): The specific item or person pointed to by the word 'this' in the oath.
المتجدد (al-mutajaddid): Something newly acquired after the oath was taken, e.g., a new house or new friend.
العداوة (al-‘adawah): Enmity; if the oath-taker says 'so-and-so is my enemy', then the motive is personal, so the addition is condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not speak to this friend of Zayd' and later Zayd becomes his enemy, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) He breaks the oath by speaking to that friend by consensus.
B) He does not break the oath because the condition of friendship is lost.
C) He breaks only if he says 'Zayd is my enemy'.
D) He does not break because he did not point.

Key Takeaway
When an oath involves a friend or wife without pointing, the two sheikhs rule no breach after the relationship ends, but the oath is broken with a newly acquired friend/wife.

Oaths Involving Time: Dahr, Days, Months, Years
Intra-Hanafi dispute on time oaths with tarjih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on time oaths using 'dahr', 'days', 'months', and 'years' and explain the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Indefinite time (dahr) is ambiguous; the mufta bihi is six months, while Abu Hanifa suspended judgment.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq tackles a practical issue: when someone swears using time expressions like 'the days' or 'a month', how many days or months does the oath cover? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, rooted in whether linguistic principles or customary usage should determine the number. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to resolve oaths and appreciate the method of tarjih within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three time categories
The matn distinguishes: (1) 'dahr' (indefinite time), (2) definite plurals (al-ayyam, al-shuhur, al-sinin), and (3) indefinite plurals (ayyaman, shuhuran, sinin). Note the ruling for each.
The indefinite plural (nakirah) rule is unanimous: minimum three.
2
Analyse the dispute on dahr
Abu Hanifa said 'I do not know' – suspended judgment. The Sahibayn defined it as six months, and this is the mufta bihi. Understand why Abu Hanifa abstained: linguistic ambiguity and the hadith 'Do not curse time'.
This reflects Abu Hanifa's caution (wara') and the principle that a mujmal text requires bayan.
3
Analyse the dispute on definite plurals
For 'al-ayyam', 'al-shuhur', 'al-sinin', Abu Hanifa fixed 10 for each based on linguistic maximum of jam'. The Sahibayn used custom: days → 7, months → 12, years → indefinite (lifetime).
The thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference in number of days/months the oath covers.
4
Note the tarjih (preference)
For dahr, the text explicitly says 'wa bihi yafta' – the Sahibayn's view is given fatwa. For definite plurals, the matter is less clear; many later works follow Abu Hanifa's view as the relied-upon position.
The passage also lists eight other issues where Abu Hanifa suspended, teaching muftis to refrain from conjecture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان
٦٤
باب اليمين في الأكل والشرب.
العمر، ودهر مجمل. والأيام وأيام كثيرة، والشهور والسنون عشرة، ومنكرها ثلاثة.
العمر: خبر
أي النكرة من الدهر مبتدأ مع معطوفاتها
والدهور والأزمنة
أي منكر الأيام والشهور والسنين
المبتدأ أي فهو العمر، ففي المثالين المذكورين يقع الحلف على العمر. (عيني) ودهر مجمل: [قال الإمام: لا أدري، وقالا: هو ستة أشهر وبه يفتى. (ط)] مبتدأ وخبر، والمسوغ كون المبتدأ معطوفاً، أراد أن المنكر من الدهر مجمل والمجمل ما ازدحمت فيه المعاني واشتبه المراد اشتباهاً لا يدرك بنفس العباد بل بالرجوع إلى الاستفسار ، ثم الطلب ثم التأمل وهو ضد المفسر، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة يعني لم يفسره وتوقف فيه حيث قال: لا أدري ما الدهر، وهذا من جلالة قدره وكمال ،ورعه فإنه توقف فيما اختلف أرباب اللغة في تقديره؛ لأن اللغات لا تدرك قياساً، والعرف لم يعرف استمراره للاختلاف في الاستعمال، وقيل: إنما قال: "لا أدري" تأدباً وحفظاً للسانه عن التحدث في الدهر، فإنه جاء في الخبر "لا تسبوا الدهر، فإن الله هو الدهر" أي خالق الدهر. وكذا توقف أبو حنيفة في مسائل هذه إحداها، والثانية: أن الملائكة أفضل أم الأنبياء؟ والثالثة: أين محل أطفال المشركين، والرابعة حكم الخنثى المشكل، والخامسة وقت الختان، والسادسة: الكلب متى يصير معلماً، السابعة: البقرة الجلالة متى يطيب لحمه الثامنة سور الحمار التاسعة حكم نقش جدار المسجد للواقف، وفيه تنبيه لكل مفت أن لا يستنكف من التوقف فيما لا وقوف له عليه؛ إذ المجازفة افتراء على الله تعالى بتحريم الحلال وضده، وقالا الدهر المنكر على ستة أشهر ؛ لأنه يستعمل فيها، يقال: ما رأيته منذ دهر ومنذ حين بمعنى واحد وهو الصحيح، فإنه إذا لم يرو عن الإمام شيء في مسألة الإفتاء بقولهما، وجب قالت الثلاثة وبه أن التعريف والتنكير سواء عند أبي حنيفة. (فتح، عيني) والأيام إلخ: أي من حلف لا يكلم فلاناً الأيام أو أياماً كثيرة أو الشهور أو السنين تقع يمينه على عشرة من كل صنف عند أبي حنيفة، فيقع على عشرة أيام، وعشرة أشهر في الثالث، وعشر سنين في الرابع؛ لأن أقصى ما ينتهي إليه اسم الأيام عشرة، يقال: "ثلاثة أيام" إلى "عشرة أيام، وبعده ينكر مفرداً، يقال: أحد عشر يوماً، وكذلك الشهور والسنون وقالا السنون والدهور على العمر ، والشهور على السنة، والأيام وأيام كثيرة على السبعة؛ لأن العهد في الأيام أنها تدور على سبعة، وفي الشهور على اثني عشر، فتنصرف إليه، وفي غيرهما لم يوجد، فيستغرق العمر، وله أن أكثر ما يطلق عليه اسم الجمع عشرة، وأقله ثلاثة، فإذا دخلت عليه آلة التعريف استغرق الجميع، وهو العشرة، وقالت الثلاثة في الشهور والسنين تنصرف اليمين إلى العمر. (فتح عيني) عشرة: خبر المبتدأ أي من كل نوع، فلو قال: لا أكلمه الأيام أو أياماً كثيرة يقع على عشرة أيام، وكذا البواقي. (عيني) ومنكرها ثلاثة: أي منكر الأيام والشهور والسنين ثلاثة من كل صنف، فلو قال: "والله لا أكلم فلاناً أياماً" فهو على ثلاثة أيام، ولو قال: "شهوراً" فهو على ثلاثة أشهر، ولو قال: "سنين" فهو على ثلاث سنين بالإجماع؛ لأنه لجمع منكر، وأقل الجمع ثلاثة ما لم يوصف بالكثرة، فيتناول الأقل للتيقن به رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٢/١] (فتح)
Book of Oaths. 64. Chapter on Oath regarding Eating and Drinking. Life-term, and indefinite time is ambiguous. And days, many days, months, and years are ten, and their indefinite are three. Life-term: predicate. That is, the indefinite of time is subject with its conjuncts. And times and periods: that is, the indefinite of days, months, and years. The subject, that is, so it is the life-term. So in the two mentioned examples, the oath falls on the life-term. ('Inayah) And indefinite time is ambiguous: [Imam said: I do not know, and they said: it is six months and that is given fatwa. (Tahawi)] subject and predicate, the allowance being that the subject is conjuncted. He intended that the indefinite of time is ambiguous, and the ambiguous is what meanings crowd in it and the intended is confused in a way not perceived by the servants themselves but by returning to inquiry, then seeking, then reflection; and it is opposite of the explained. This according to Abu Hanifa means he did not explain it and suspended it, saying: I do not know what time is. This is from his high rank and perfect piety, for he suspended what the linguists differed in estimating; because languages are not known by analogy, and custom did not continue due to difference in usage. And it was said: He only said 'I do not know' as politeness and guarding his tongue from speaking about time, for it came in the hadith: 'Do not curse time, for Allah is time,' meaning the Creator of time. Likewise Abu Hanifa suspended in issues: this is one of them, the second: are angels better or prophets? third: where are the children of polytheists? fourth: ruling of the ambiguous hermaphrodite, fifth: time of circumcision, sixth: when does a dog become trained?, seventh: when does the jalalah cow's meat become permissible, eighth: the donkey's Surah, ninth: ruling of engraving the mosque wall for the endower. In it is a warning for every mufti not to be ashamed of suspending in what he has no knowledge of; for rashness is slander against Allah Almighty by making lawful unlawful and vice versa. And the two said: the indefinite time is six months, because it is used therein; it is said: 'I have not seen him since a time' and 'since a while' meaning the same, and that is correct. For if nothing is transmitted from the Imam in an issue, then in fatwa it is by their saying, and it is incumbent, the three said, and the definition and indefiniteness are equal according to Abu Hanifa. (Fath, 'Inayah) And the days etc.: that is, whoever swears he will not speak to so-and-so the days or many days or the months or the years, his oath falls on ten of each kind according to Abu Hanifa. So it falls on ten days, ten months in the third, and ten years in the fourth; because the maximum that the name 'days' reaches is ten; it is said: 'three days' up to 'ten days', and after that it is expressed in the singular indefinite: 'eleven days'. And similarly months and years. And the two said: years and periods (definite) are for life, months for a year, and days and many days for seven, because the norm for days is that they revolve on seven, and months on twelve, so the oath turns to that. And as for others, it is not found, so it covers life. And for him (Abu Hanifa) that the most that the plural noun is applied to is ten, and its least is three. So when the article of definition enters it, it covers all, which is ten. And the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) said in months and years that the oath turns to life. (Fath, 'Inayah) Ten: predicate of the subject, i.e., of each type. So if he said: 'I will not speak to him the days' or 'many days', it falls on ten days, and likewise the rest. ('Inayah) And their indefinite are three: i.e., the indefinite of days, months, and years are three of each type. So if he said: 'By Allah, I will not speak to so-and-so days,' it is on three days; if he said: 'months,' it is on three months; if he said: 'years,' it is on three years, by consensus, because it is an indefinite plural, and the least of a plural is three as long as it is not described by abundance, so it applies to the least for certainty. (Fath)
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Ayman (Book of Oaths) in Kanz al-Daqa'iq, with commentary from 'Inayah and Fath, addresses oaths involving time expressions. The matn states: for oaths involving 'umr (life-term) and dahr (indefinite time), the indefinite time (dahr) is ambiguous (mujmal). For definite plurals like 'al-ayyam' (the days), 'ayyam kathirah' (many days), 'al-shuhur' (the months), 'al-sinin' (the years), the number is ten. For indefinite plurals like 'ayyaman' (days), 'shuhuran' (months), 'sinin' (years), the number is three. The commentary explains each part. Regarding dahr, Abu Hanifa suspended judgment, saying 'I do not know', due to linguistic disagreement and out of piety, avoiding speaking about time as per the hadith 'Do not curse time, for Allah is time.' The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) ruled it as six months, and that is the position given fatwa. The commentary also lists seven other issues where Abu Hanifa similarly suspended judgment. Regarding definite plurals, Abu Hanifa specifies ten as the maximum for the plural form, while the two specify seven for days, twelve for months, and for years/periods, life. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position for indefinite dahr is six months (Sahibayn), and for definite plurals, Abu Hanifa's view of ten is followed. The passage concludes with the consensus that indefinite plurals refer to a minimum of three.

Key Terms
دهر (dahr): Indefinite time; in the context of oaths, it is ambiguous (mujmal) and Abu Hanifa suspended judgment on its extent. The Sahibayn defined it as six months.
مجمل (mujmal): Ambiguous; a word whose meaning is not clear without clarification. Opposite of مفسر (mufassar).
منكر (munkar): Indefinite (nakirah); in this passage, refers to indefinite plurals (e.g., أياما, شهورا, سنين) which take the minimum of three.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, how many months does an indefinite time oath (dahr munkar) cover?
A) It is ambiguous and no ruling is given.
B) Six months.
C) One year.
D) A lifetime.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths on time, indefinite dahr is ambiguous but fatwa is on six months; definite plurals have an ikhtilaf (Abu Hanifa: 10, Sahibayn: custom-based); indefinite plurals are uniformly three.

Conditions for Manumission under 'First Slave I Own'
Ruling on 'first slave I own is free' with multiple ownership scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions of fardiyya (individuality) and sabq (precedence) for the oath 'first slave I own is free' and apply them to scenarios of simultaneous and sequential ownership.

Core Idea
'First' requires both individuality and precedence; two simultaneous owners are neither, so no slave is freed.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on the common oath 'The first slave I own is free.' The key is understanding that the word 'first' (awwal) implies a single, preceding entity. The text walks through ownership scenarios to clarify when the oath is fulfilled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition in the oath
The word 'first' (awwal) requires a single, preceding individual (fard sābiq). No other slave should share this description.
2
Apply to single ownership
If one slave is owned alone, he is freed because he is an individual and the first owned.
3
Apply to simultaneous ownership
If two slaves are bought together or come into ownership simultaneously, neither is an individual (fard), so the condition fails.
The two together are not 'first' because they are not a single entity.
4
Apply to subsequent purchase
If a third slave is bought after two together, he is not preceding, so he is not freed.
The third is an individual but not preceding.
5
Analyze the effect of adding 'alone'
If the swearer adds 'alone' (waḥdahu), solitude in the purchase act is required. So if two are bought together and later one alone, the later alone is freed.
'Alone' modifies the verb, not the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فملك عبداً عتق أي من قال: أول" عبد أملكه فهو حر فملك عبداً واحداً، عتق هذا المملوك؛ لأن الأول اسم لفرد سابق لا يشاركه غيره في اسمه ومعناه، وقد وجد فيه هذا المعنى، فيعتق، وقوله: "ولو ملك عبدين معاً" أي اشتراهما في عقد واحد أو جاءا في ملكه مجتمعين ثم ملك آخر لا يعتق واحد منهم؛ لأن الأولين وإن كانا سابقين لكنهما ليسا بفردين، والعبد الثالث وإن كان فرداً إلا أنه غير سابق وقد كان الشرط كون الأول فرداً سابقاً فلم يوجد (مستخلص، فتح واحد منهم: أي من العبيد الثلاثة؛ لعدم وجود الشرط وهو الفردية في العبدين ولا في ما اشتراه بعدهما لعدم السبق. (عيني) ولو زاد إلخ: أي لو زاد الحالف على كلامه الأول لفظة "وحده" بأن قال: أول" عبد أملكه وحده فهو حر"، فاشتری عبدین معاً، ثم اشترى آخر بعدهما عتق الثالث؛ لأنه يراد به الانفراد في حالة الشراء؛ لأن وحده للحال وحده عتق الثالث، ولو قال آخر عبدٍ أملكه فهو حرّ، فملك عبداً فعبداً فمات عتق الآخر = أي رجل أي الحالف يقال: جاء زيد وحده أي منفرداً، فيشترط انفراده في حالة الشراء، ولم يسبقه أحد بهذه الصفة فكان أولاً، ولو قال: واحداً موضع "وحده" لا يعتق الثالث؛ لأن وحده يقتضي الانفراد في الفعل المقرون به ولا يقتضي الانفراد في الذات، و"واحداً" يقتضي الانفراد في الذات، يظهر ذلك في قوله: "ما في الدار رجل واحد"، و"ما في الدار رجل وحده"، فإنه إذا كان فيها رجلان كان الأول كاذباً والثاني صادقاً. : ولو قال: أول عبد أملكه فهو حر، فملك عبداً ونصف عبد عتق العبد الكامل؛ لأن نصف العبد ليس بعبد، فلم يشاركه في اسمه، فلا تنقطع عنه وصف الأولية والفردية كما لو ملك معه ثوباً أو نحوه، بخلاف ما إذا قال: أول كر أملكه فهو هدي، فملك كراً ونصف كر حيث لا يلزمه شيء؛ لأن النصف يزاحم الكل في المكيلات والموزونات؛ لأنه بالضم يصير شيئاً واحداً بخلاف الثياب والعبيد. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٣/١]
So if he owns a slave, he becomes free: i.e., one who says: 'The first slave I own is free' and he owns one slave, that slave is freed because 'first' is a name for a preceding individual that no other shares in its name and meaning, and this meaning is found in it, so it is freed. His statement: 'And if he owns two slaves together' i.e., he bought them in one contract or they came into his ownership simultaneously, then he owns another, none of them is freed because the first two, though preceding, are not individuals, and the third slave, though an individual, is not preceding, and the condition was for it to be an individual and preceding, so it is not found. (Mustakhlas, Fath) 'None of them is freed' i.e., from the three slaves, because the condition—individuality—is absent in the two slaves, and in what he bought after them due to lack of precedence. (Ayni) 'And if he added, etc.' i.e., if the swearer added to his first statement the word 'alone' (waḥdahu) by saying: 'The first slave I own alone is free,' and he bought two slaves together, then bought another after them, the third is freed because by it is intended solitude in the state of purchase, because 'alone' is for the state. So 'alone' frees the third. And if he said: 'The last slave I own is free,' and he owns a slave then a slave and then dies, the last is freed. = a man, i.e., the swearer. It is said: 'Zayd came alone' i.e., by himself, so his solitude in the state of purchase is stipulated, and no one preceded him in this description, so he is first. And if he said: 'one' (wāḥidan) instead of 'alone' (waḥdahu), the third is not freed because 'alone' entails solitude in the verb it is attached to and does not entail solitude in the essence, while 'one' entails solitude in the essence. This is shown in his saying: 'There is not in the house one man' and 'There is not in the house a man alone'—if there are two men in it, the first is false and the second is true. And if he said: 'The first slave I own is free,' and he owns a slave and half a slave, the complete slave is freed because half a slave is not a slave, so it does not share with him in its name, so the description of firstness and individuality is not cut off from him, just as if he owned with him a garment or the like. Contrary to if he said: 'The first kurr I own is a gift,' and he owns a kurr and half a kurr, where nothing is due because the half competes with the whole in things that are measured or weighed, because by combining they become one thing, unlike clothes and slaves. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:363]
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement clarifying the conditions for manumission under an oath like 'The first slave I own is free.' It explains that the word 'first' (awwal) requires a single, preceding individual. If one slave is owned alone, he is freed. If two slaves are owned simultaneously (e.g., bought together), neither is the first because the condition of 'individual' (fard) is not met. If a third is purchased later, he is not first because he is not preceding. If the swearer adds the word 'alone' (waḥdahu), then the condition becomes solitude at the time of purchase, so if he buys two together and later another alone, the third is freed. The distinction between 'alone' (waḥdahu) and 'one' (wāḥid) is explained: 'alone' refers to solitude in the act (e.g., buying alone), while 'one' refers to solitude in essence (e.g., being a single entity). The example of 'no man in the house' and 'no man alone' illustrates this. Finally, if one owns a slave and half a slave, the complete slave is freed because half is not a slave, so it does not compete. This contrasts with measurable items like grain (kurr), where half combines with the whole to form one unit, so the oath is not fulfilled.

Key Terms
أول (awwal): A term for a singular, preceding individual that shares its name and meaning with no other.
فرد (fard): An individual, single entity; a slave is a fard; half a slave is not.
وحده (waḥdahu): An adverb meaning 'alone', indicating solitude in the action (e.g., buying alone).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says: 'The first slave I own is free.' He buys two slaves together in one contract. Later, he buys a third slave. Which slaves are freed?
A) The first two are freed equally.
B) The third is freed because he is the first single acquisition.
C) None are freed because the condition of a single preceding individual was not met.
D) All three are freed.

Key Takeaway
For an oath 'first slave I own is free' to be fulfilled, the slave must be a single, preceding individual.

Distinguishing 'Alone' vs 'One' and the Half-Slave Exception
Rulings on 'first slave I own' with 'alone' vs 'one' and half-slave exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the legal effects of 'alone' (waḥdahu) and 'one' (wāḥid) in oaths, and explain why half a slave does not compete with a whole slave in fulfilling an oath.

Core Idea
'Alone' modifies the act – solitude in purchase – while 'one' modifies the essence; half a slave is not a slave, so it does not compete.

This lesson delves into two nuanced aspects of the 'first slave' oath. First, the subtle difference between saying 'first slave I own alone' versus 'first slave I own one' leads to different rulings. Second, the case of owning a slave and half a slave shows that half a slave is not considered a slave for the oath, unlike measurable goods where halves combine.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction between 'alone' and 'one'
'Alone' (waḥdahu) pertains to the action – the slave must be purchased in a solitary transaction. 'One' (wāḥid) pertains to the essence – the slave must be a single entity at the time of acquisition.
Example: 'no man in the house one' vs 'no man alone in the house' shows the difference.
2
Apply to the oath with 'alone'
If the swearer says 'first slave I own alone', then buying two together and later one alone: the later alone is freed because it was acquired alone.
3
Apply to the oath with 'one'
If the swearer says 'first slave I own one', then buying two together and later one alone: none is freed because the first two were not a single entity, and the later one is not first.
The third is not the first single entity.
4
Analyze the half-slave scenario
If one owns a slave and half a slave, the whole slave is freed because half a slave is not a slave (not a fard). It does not compete in the name 'slave'.
5
Contrast with measurable goods
For goods like kurr (a measure of grain), half a kurr does compete because when combined with a whole, they form a single measurable unit. Thus the oath 'first kurr I own is a gift' is not fulfilled if half is owned first.
The ruling differs because slaves and clothes are not measurable by weight or volume.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فملك عبداً عتق أي من قال: أول" عبد أملكه فهو حر فملك عبداً واحداً، عتق هذا المملوك؛ لأن الأول اسم لفرد سابق لا يشاركه غيره في اسمه ومعناه، وقد وجد فيه هذا المعنى، فيعتق، وقوله: "ولو ملك عبدين معاً" أي اشتراهما في عقد واحد أو جاءا في ملكه مجتمعين ثم ملك آخر لا يعتق واحد منهم؛ لأن الأولين وإن كانا سابقين لكنهما ليسا بفردين، والعبد الثالث وإن كان فرداً إلا أنه غير سابق وقد كان الشرط كون الأول فرداً سابقاً فلم يوجد (مستخلص، فتح واحد منهم: أي من العبيد الثلاثة؛ لعدم وجود الشرط وهو الفردية في العبدين ولا في ما اشتراه بعدهما لعدم السبق. (عيني) ولو زاد إلخ: أي لو زاد الحالف على كلامه الأول لفظة "وحده" بأن قال: أول" عبد أملكه وحده فهو حر"، فاشتری عبدین معاً، ثم اشترى آخر بعدهما عتق الثالث؛ لأنه يراد به الانفراد في حالة الشراء؛ لأن وحده للحال وحده عتق الثالث، ولو قال آخر عبدٍ أملكه فهو حرّ، فملك عبداً فعبداً فمات عتق الآخر = أي رجل أي الحالف يقال: جاء زيد وحده أي منفرداً، فيشترط انفراده في حالة الشراء، ولم يسبقه أحد بهذه الصفة فكان أولاً، ولو قال: واحداً موضع "وحده" لا يعتق الثالث؛ لأن وحده يقتضي الانفراد في الفعل المقرون به ولا يقتضي الانفراد في الذات، و"واحداً" يقتضي الانفراد في الذات، يظهر ذلك في قوله: "ما في الدار رجل واحد"، و"ما في الدار رجل وحده"، فإنه إذا كان فيها رجلان كان الأول كاذباً والثاني صادقاً. : ولو قال: أول عبد أملكه فهو حر، فملك عبداً ونصف عبد عتق العبد الكامل؛ لأن نصف العبد ليس بعبد، فلم يشاركه في اسمه، فلا تنقطع عنه وصف الأولية والفردية كما لو ملك معه ثوباً أو نحوه، بخلاف ما إذا قال: أول كر أملكه فهو هدي، فملك كراً ونصف كر حيث لا يلزمه شيء؛ لأن النصف يزاحم الكل في المكيلات والموزونات؛ لأنه بالضم يصير شيئاً واحداً بخلاف الثياب والعبيد. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٣/١]
So if he owns a slave, he becomes free: i.e., one who says: 'The first slave I own is free' and he owns one slave, that slave is freed because 'first' is a name for a preceding individual that no other shares in its name and meaning, and this meaning is found in it, so it is freed. His statement: 'And if he owns two slaves together' i.e., he bought them in one contract or they came into his ownership simultaneously, then he owns another, none of them is freed because the first two, though preceding, are not individuals, and the third slave, though an individual, is not preceding, and the condition was for it to be an individual and preceding, so it is not found. (Mustakhlas, Fath) 'None of them is freed' i.e., from the three slaves, because the condition—individuality—is absent in the two slaves, and in what he bought after them due to lack of precedence. (Ayni) 'And if he added, etc.' i.e., if the swearer added to his first statement the word 'alone' (waḥdahu) by saying: 'The first slave I own alone is free,' and he bought two slaves together, then bought another after them, the third is freed because by it is intended solitude in the state of purchase, because 'alone' is for the state. So 'alone' frees the third. And if he said: 'The last slave I own is free,' and he owns a slave then a slave and then dies, the last is freed. = a man, i.e., the swearer. It is said: 'Zayd came alone' i.e., by himself, so his solitude in the state of purchase is stipulated, and no one preceded him in this description, so he is first. And if he said: 'one' (wāḥidan) instead of 'alone' (waḥdahu), the third is not freed because 'alone' entails solitude in the verb it is attached to and does not entail solitude in the essence, while 'one' entails solitude in the essence. This is shown in his saying: 'There is not in the house one man' and 'There is not in the house a man alone'—if there are two men in it, the first is false and the second is true. And if he said: 'The first slave I own is free,' and he owns a slave and half a slave, the complete slave is freed because half a slave is not a slave, so it does not share with him in its name, so the description of firstness and individuality is not cut off from him, just as if he owned with him a garment or the like. Contrary to if he said: 'The first kurr I own is a gift,' and he owns a kurr and half a kurr, where nothing is due because the half competes with the whole in things that are measured or weighed, because by combining they become one thing, unlike clothes and slaves. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:363]
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement clarifying the conditions for manumission under an oath like 'The first slave I own is free.' It explains that the word 'first' (awwal) requires a single, preceding individual. If one slave is owned alone, he is freed. If two slaves are owned simultaneously (e.g., bought together), neither is the first because the condition of 'individual' (fard) is not met. If a third is purchased later, he is not first because he is not preceding. If the swearer adds the word 'alone' (waḥdahu), then the condition becomes solitude at the time of purchase, so if he buys two together and later another alone, the third is freed. The distinction between 'alone' (waḥdahu) and 'one' (wāḥid) is explained: 'alone' refers to solitude in the act (e.g., buying alone), while 'one' refers to solitude in essence (e.g., being a single entity). The example of 'no man in the house' and 'no man alone' illustrates this. Finally, if one owns a slave and half a slave, the complete slave is freed because half is not a slave, so it does not compete. This contrasts with measurable items like grain (kurr), where half combines with the whole to form one unit, so the oath is not fulfilled.

Key Terms
أول (awwal): A term for a singular, preceding individual that shares its name and meaning with no other.
فرد (fard): An individual, single entity; a slave is a fard; half a slave is not.
وحده (waḥdahu): An adverb meaning 'alone', indicating solitude in the action (e.g., buying alone).
واحد (wāḥid): An adjective meaning 'one', indicating singleness in essence.
كر (kurr): A measure for grain, approximately 125 liters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says 'First slave I own alone is free.' He buys two slaves together, then buys a third alone. What is the ruling?
A) The first two are freed.
B) The third is freed.
C) None are freed.
D) All three are freed.

Key Takeaway
The word 'alone' in an oath conditions the manner of acquisition, while 'one' conditions the nature of the entity; half a slave is not a slave for the purpose of oaths.

Timing of Manumission in the 'Last Slave' Oath
Intra-Hanafi dispute on timing of emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on the timing of emancipation in the oath 'The last slave I own is free' and state the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) for estate distribution.

Core Idea
The timing of emancipation in a 'last slave' oath determines whether freedom comes from all wealth or only one-third.

In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an oath: 'The last slave I own is free.' All agree that if the swearer buys two slaves and dies, the second slave is freed. But when did freedom begin? Abu Hanifa says from the time of purchase, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say only at death. This disagreement has major implications for how the slave's value is deducted from the estate—whether from the entire estate or only from the one-third bequest limit.

Guided Path
1
State the agreed ruling
Identify what all Hanafi imams agree on: the second slave is definitely freed upon death if the swearer owned two slaves.
The dispute is not about validity but about timing.
2
Identify the dispute: timing of freedom
Abu Hanifa holds that the slave is freed retroactively from the moment of purchase. The Sahibayn hold that freedom begins only at death.
This is the core issue.
3
Explain Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that the attribute of 'lastness' is already present at the time of purchase because no other slave was bought after. Death merely confirms it, so freedom relates back to purchase.
Key phrase: 'death is a definer, not a producer'.
4
Explain the Sahibayn's reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that lastness is only certain at death because the swearer could have bought another slave. Until death, the possibility remains, so emancipation occurs at death.
They emphasize uncertainty before death.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah)
If purchased in health: Abu Hanifa frees from all property (full estate); Sahibayn frees from one-third. If purchased in illness: both treat from one-third, but the timing affects whether the slave is considered a bequest or an inheritance.
This affects the shares of other heirs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمات عتق الآخر: أي لو قال آخر عبد أملكه فهو حر، فاشترى عبداً ثم اشترى عبداً آخر فمات الحالف، عتق العبد الآخر اتفاقاً؛ لأن الآخر اسم لفرد لاحق لا يشاركه غيره من جنسه والعبد الآخر كذلك فيعتق، لكن الاختلاف في ابتداء عتقه، فقوله: "مذ "ملك" أي عتق من حين ملكه الحالف يعني من وقت الشراء عند أبي حنيفة حتى يعتبر عتقه من جميع المال إن كان اشتراه في صحته وعندهما يعتق مقتصراً على حالة الموت، فيعتبر من الثلث سواء كان شراؤه في الصحة أو المرض؛ لأن الآخرية تثبت بعدم شراء غيره بعده. وإنما يثبت العدم عند الموت، فيقتصر العتق على وقت الموت؛ ولأن الآخرية تثبت للثاني كما اشتراه، إلا أن هذه الصفة بمعرض الزوال؛ لاحتمال شراء غيره، فإذا مات لم يوجد ما يبطل صفة الآخرية، فكان بالموت معرفاً لوجود شرط الآخرية لا موجداً له، فتبين أنه كان آخراً عند الشراء، فعتق من ذلك الوقت، وعلى هذا الخلاف ما إذا قال: آخر امرأة أتزوجها فهي طالق ثلاثاً، يقع عند الموت عندهما، وترث بحكم أنه فار، ولها مهر واحد، وعليها العدة لأبعد الأجلين من عدة الطلاق والوفاة، وإن كان الطلاق رجعياً بأن لم يقل: "ثلاثاً" فعليها عدة الوفاة وتحد وعنده: يقع من حين تزوجها، فإن كان دخل بها فلها مهر ونصف مهر بالدخول بشبهة ونصف مهر بالطلاق قبل الدخول، وعدتها بالحيض بلا حداد، ولا ترث منه [رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٣/١] (مستخلص) واعلم أنه وقع في نسخة شرح ملا مسكين عبارة المتن :هكذا ولو قال: "آخر عبد ملكه فهو حر فملك عبداً لم يعتق، فلو اشترى عبداً ثم اشترى عبداً فمات" إلخ والمعنى أن الحالف إذا ملك عبداً فقط ومات بعد ذلك لم يعتق هذا العبد؛ لأن الآخر اسم لفرد لاحق، واللاحق من يكون له سابق ولا سابق لهذا العبد، فلا يكون لاحقاً، فلا يعتق. (محشي)
So if he dies, the other is freed: i.e., if he said: 'The last slave I own is free,' and he bought a slave then bought another slave and then the swearer died, the last slave is freed by agreement; because 'last' (ākhir) is a name for a subsequent individual which no other of its type shares, and the last slave is like that, so it is freed. However, the difference is in the beginning of his freedom. His statement: 'since he owned' (mudh malaka) means he is freed from the time the swearer owned him, i.e., from the time of purchase according to Abu Hanifa, so his freedom is considered from all the property if he bought it in health. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he is freed only at the time of death, so it is considered from the one-third, whether his purchase was in health or illness; because the attribute of being last is established by the non-purchase of another after him, and this non-existence is only established at death, so the emancipation is confined to the time of death. Also, because lastness is established for the second at the time of purchase, but this attribute is subject to removal due to the possibility of buying another; when he dies, nothing exists to invalidate the attribute of lastness, so death is a definer of the condition of lastness, not its producer, so it becomes clear that he was last at the time of purchase, so he is freed from that time. On this difference is based if he said: 'The last woman I marry is divorced thrice'—it occurs at death according to them, and she inherits on the basis that he is a free man, and she gets one dower, and her waiting period is the longer of the two periods from divorce and death; and if the divorce was revocable (by not saying 'thrice'), then she performs idda of death and observes mourning. According to him (Abu Hanifa), it occurs from the time he married her, and if he had consummated, she gets one and a half dowers: half for consummation under doubt and half for divorce before consummation, and her idda is by periods without mourning, and she does not inherit from him. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1:363] (Mustakhlas) And know that in the copy of the commentary by Mulla Miskin, the text is: 'And if he said: 'The last slave I own is free' and he owns a slave, it is not freed; but if he bought a slave then bought another slave then died,' etc. The meaning is that if the swearer owns only one slave and dies thereafter, this slave is not freed; because 'last' is a name for a subsequent individual, and the subsequent one must have a predecessor, and this slave has no predecessor, so he is not subsequent, so he is not freed. (Hashiya)
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an oath 'The last slave I own is free.' There is agreement that if the swearer buys a slave then another and dies, the second slave is freed. The dispute is about the moment of emancipation: Abu Hanifa holds that the slave is freed from the moment of purchase, so if purchased in health, it is considered from all property (the full estate). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that the slave is freed only at death, so it is considered from the one-third of the estate, regardless of when purchased. Their reasoning: the attribute of 'lastness' is only confirmed at death because until then, he could have bought another slave; death defines it but does not produce it. Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the lastness was actually present at the time of purchase (since no other was bought after), so freedom starts then. The text then applies this dispute to a similar oath about marrying the last woman: if the swearer says 'The last woman I marry is divorced thrice,' the divorce occurs at death according to the Sahibayn (so she inherits, etc.), but at the time of marriage according to Abu Hanifa (so she does not inherit, etc.). The final note clarifies that if only one slave is owned at death, he is not freed because there is no predecessor to make him 'last'.

Key Terms
آخر (ākhir): The last, a subsequent individual that has a predecessor; here, it refers to the final slave owned at death.
مذ ملك (mudh malaka): An expression meaning 'from the time he owned' indicating retroactive emancipation from purchase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says: 'The last slave I own is free.' He buys two slaves, then dies. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, from what part of the estate is the second slave freed?
A) From the entire estate because ownership was in health.
B) From the one-third of the estate only.
C) From the half of the estate.
D) The slave is not freed because the condition is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on the timing of emancipation in a 'last slave' oath illustrates how a difference in the interpretation of a condition (lastness) leads to different practical rulings on estate distribution.

The Meaning of 'Wahdahu' in Oaths of Manumission
Explaining the legal implication of the word 'alone' in conditional manumission oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal implications of 'wahdahu' and 'wahidan' in Hanafi oath contexts and correctly determine the freed slave in conditional manumission scenarios.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, 'wahdahu' qualifies the action, not the essence—a distinction that changes the outcome of manumission oaths.

This lesson examines a subtle but crucial linguistic distinction in Hanafi jurisprudence: the difference between 'wahdahu' (alone) and 'wahidan' (one). In oaths of manumission, this distinction determines which slave is freed. By parsing the grammatical function, you will learn to apply this rule to concrete cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the operative word in the oath
Look for 'wahdahu' or 'wahidan' in the condition. The word used determines the legal outcome.
2
Determine whether the word modifies the action or the essence
'Wahdahu' is an adverb that modifies the verb (action), indicating the action is done alone. 'Wahidan' is an adjective modifying the noun (essence), indicating the entity is single.
Example: 'Zayd came alone' vs. 'Zayd is one man'.
3
Apply to the manumission scenario
In 'the first slave I own alone is free', 'alone' refers to the act of ownership: the slave must be purchased in a solitary transaction. If three slaves are bought successively, only the third qualifies because at his purchase, the owner already owned others? Actually, the passage states the third is freed.
The key is that 'wahdahu' requires solitude in the purchase action, not in the slave's essence.
4
Test with the house example
Consider 'There is not in the house a man alone' vs. 'There is not in the house one man'. With two men, the first statement is true (no one is alone), the second is false (there are two). This illustrates the difference.
This linguistic test helps solidify the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحده عتق الثالث، ولو قال آخر عبدٍ أملكه فهو حرّ، فملك عبداً فعبداً فمات عتق الآخر = أي رجل أي الحالف يقال: جاء زيد وحده أي منفرداً، فيشترط انفراده في حالة الشراء، ولم يسبقه أحد بهذه الصفة فكان أولاً، ولو قال: واحداً موضع "وحده" لا يعتق الثالث؛ لأن وحده يقتضي الانفراد في الفعل المقرون به ولا يقتضي الانفراد في الذات، و"واحداً" يقتضي الانفراد في الذات، يظهر ذلك في قوله: "ما في الدار رجل واحد"، و"ما في الدار رجل وحده"، فإنه إذا كان فيها رجلان كان الأول كاذباً والثاني صادقاً.
His saying: 'alone – he frees the third.' And if he said: 'The last slave I own is free,' then he owned a slave, then a slave, then died, the last is freed. Explanation: 'any man' means the swearer. It is said: 'Zayd came alone' meaning by himself. So his being alone at the time of purchase is a condition, and no one preceded him with this description so he was first. And if he said 'one' instead of 'alone', the third is not freed because 'alone' necessitates solitude in the action associated with it, not solitude in the essence; while 'one' necessitates solitude in the essence. That is clear in his saying 'There is not in the house one man' vs. 'There is not in the house a man alone' – if there are two men, the first is false and the second is true.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling. It explains the legal nuance of the word 'wahdahu' (alone) in oaths of manumission. When a person swears 'the first slave I own alone is free' and then buys three slaves successively, only the third is freed because the condition of solitude at the time of purchase is met for that slave – no other slave was owned with him. The author distinguishes 'wahdahu' from 'wahidan': 'wahdahu' describes the action (ownership) as solitary, while 'wahidan' describes the essence (the slave) as single. So if he said 'one slave' instead, the third would not be freed because the word 'one' refers to the slave's essence, and the first slave he owned was also one. The example with the house shows the difference: 'There is not in the house one man' is false if there are two, but 'There is not in the house a man alone' is true if there are two because they are together.

Key Terms
وحده (wahdahu): Meaning 'alone', it qualifies the action of the verb, indicating the subject performed it without others.
واحداً (wāḥidan): Meaning 'one', it qualifies the noun itself, indicating numerical singularity of the entity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The first slave he bought
B) The second slave he bought
C) The third slave he bought
D) All three are freed

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between 'wahdahu' (altering the action) and 'wahidan' (altering the essence), a difference that determines the outcome in conditional manumission oaths.

Fraction of a Slave and Oaths of First Ownership
Ruling on oaths involving first owned slave when a fraction is owned

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when an oath on the first owned slave is made and a fraction of a slave is also owned, and distinguish it from the case of measured goods.

Core Idea
Half a slave is not a slave for oaths; the first whole slave is freed.

In Hanafi fiqh, oaths often depend on the precise meaning of the terms used. When someone swears to free the first slave they own, what happens if they acquire a whole slave and a half-share in another? This passage explores that question and draws a sharp contrast with oaths involving measured goods like grain. Understanding this distinction is key to applying the correct ruling in similar cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two oaths and their objects
The passage presents two oath scenarios: one regarding a slave (discrete being) and one regarding a kurr (continuous measure). Note the difference in objects.
2
Understand the ruling for the slave case
When a whole slave and half a slave are owned, only the whole slave is freed. The rationale: half a slave is not legally a 'slave' for the oath, so it does not compete for the description 'first.'
The half is considered non-existent in this context.
3
Understand the ruling for the kurr case
When a full kurr and half a kurr are owned, nothing is due. Rationale: in measured items, fractions combine with the whole to form a single quantity, so there is no distinct 'first kurr.'
4
Compare the underlying reasoning
Why do the two cases differ? Because slaves are discrete indivisible entities, while measured/weighed items are continuous and their parts merge. This is the key legal distinction (al-illah).
The analogy (qiyas) between the two cases highlights this distinction.
5
Apply the principle to new scenarios
Now you can determine the ruling for oaths on other discrete items (e.g., camels, cars) versus continuous items (e.g., liters of oil).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال: أول عبد أملكه فهو حر، فملك عبداً ونصف عبد عتق العبد الكامل؛ لأن نصف العبد ليس بعبد، فلم يشاركه في اسمه، فلا تنقطع عنه وصف الأولية والفردية كما لو ملك معه ثوباً أو نحوه، بخلاف ما إذا قال: أول كر أملكه فهو هدي، فملك كراً ونصف كر حيث لا يلزمه شيء؛ لأن النصف يزاحم الكل في المكيلات والموزونات؛ لأنه بالضم يصير شيئاً واحداً بخلاف الثياب والعبيد.
And if he said: 'The first slave I own is free,' then he owned a slave and half a slave, the complete slave is freed because half a slave is not a slave, so it does not share its name with him, and the description of being first and single is not cut off from him, just as if he owned with him a garment or the like. Unlike if he said: 'The first kurr (measure) I own is a sacrifice,' then he owned a kurr and half a kurr, where nothing is incumbent upon him because half competes with the whole in measured and weighed items, since by addition they become one thing, unlike garments and slaves.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It addresses a scenario where a person swears 'the first slave I own is free' and then acquires a whole slave and half a slave. The ruling is that only the whole slave is freed. The reason is that half a slave is not legally considered a 'slave' – a slave must be a complete human being. Therefore, the half does not compete for the description 'first.' This is contrasted with food items measured by volume or weight (like kurr): if someone swears 'the first kurr I own is a sacrifice,' and then owns a full kurr and half a kurr, nothing is due because the half combines with the whole to form a single quantity, so the first is not solely the whole. This analogy shows the difference between discrete items (slaves, garments) where parts do not combine into a single unit, and continuous quantities (grains, liquids) where parts do combine.

Key Terms
نصف عبد (niṣf 'abd): Half a slave – a fraction of a human being, which is not legally considered a 'slave' for the purpose of this oath.
كر (kurr): A measure of volume or weight, typically used for grains.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears 'The first slave I own is free.' He then buys a complete slave and a half-share in another slave. Which slave is freed?
A) The complete slave
B) The half-share
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Half a slave is not a slave for oath purposes; the first whole slave is freed, contrasting with measured goods where fractions combine.

Glad Tidings (Bisharah) in Oaths of Manumission
Ruling on manumission conditioned on glad tidings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for an oath of manumission conditioned on glad tidings and distinguish between sequential and simultaneous bearers of the news.

Core Idea
Bisharah (glad tidings) is a unique event; only the first informant triggers manumission unless all inform simultaneously.

This lesson examines a specific type of oath where a master promises freedom to any slave who brings him glad tidings about a certain matter. The key is understanding what qualifies as 'bisharah' and how it applies when multiple slaves bring the same news. We will see the ruling for sequential versus simultaneous glad tidings and the distinction between judicial and religious validity when the master claims he intended only one.

Guided Path
1
Define bisharah
Understand that bisharah is pleasing news previously unknown to the recipient. Distinguish it from mere khabar.
This definition is critical for the entire ruling.
2
Apply the temporal condition
If slaves give tidings separately, only the first is freed. If they give tidings together, all are freed. Explain why.
The simultaneity matters because the news becomes known at once.
3
Cite the Quranic proof
Refer to Surah al-Dhariyat (51:28) where angels collectively give glad tidings to Ibrahim, proving that a group can bear bisharah.
This supports the ruling for simultaneous bearers.
4
Analyze the master's claim of intent
If the master says 'I meant one,' his claim is not accepted in court (qada') but is accepted between him and Allah (din). He may then free only one and keep the rest.
This shows the distinction between judicial and religious validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مذ ملك، كلّ عبدٍ بشرني بكذا فهو حقٌّ، فبشره ثلاثة متفرقون عتق الأول، وإن بشروه معا عتقوا، وبين. عتقوا لأن البشارة تحققت من الكل على المعنى الذي سبق، وقد قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَبَشِّرُوهُ بِغُلامٍ عَلِيمٍ﴾ (الذريات:28)، فدل على أنه يتحقق البشارة من الجماعة، ولو قال: عنيت واحداً لم يدين في القضاء، بل فيما بينه الله تعالى، فيختار منهم من شاء فيمضي عتقه ويمسك البقية.
From the time he owned: 'Every slave who gives me glad tidings of such-and-such is free.' Then three gave him glad tidings separately: the first is freed. If they gave him glad tidings together, all are freed. And between: they are freed because the glad tidings was realized from all on the meaning that preceded. And Allah Almighty said: 'And give him glad tidings of a knowing boy' (51:28), which indicates that glad tidings is realized from a group. And if he said 'I meant one', he is not believed in court, but between him and Allah, he may choose whomever he wishes, effect his manumission, and keep the rest.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling for an oath like 'Every slave who gives me glad tidings of such-and-such is free.' The key definition: 'bisharah' (glad tidings) is news that is pleasing and that the recipient did not previously know. If three slaves give the news one after another, only the first is freed because the glad tidings was already known from him; the others simply repeat. However, if they give the news simultaneously, all are freed because each contributed to the tidings at the same time. The proof is from the Quran (51:28) where the angels collectively gave glad tidings to Ibrahim, showing that a group can be the bearer of glad tidings. If the master later says 'I only meant one of them,' his claim is not accepted in court (qada’) because the condition was general; but between him and Allah, he may honor his intention and free only one. This distinction between judicial and religious validity is important in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
بشارة (bishārah): Glad tidings – news that is pleasing and previously unknown to the recipient.
يدين (yudayyanu): He is believed (religiously) – one's inner intention is accepted between him and Allah, though not enforceable in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master says 'Every slave who gives me glad tidings of Zayd's arrival is free,' and three slaves separately inform him of Zayd's arrival, what is the ruling?
A) All three are freed
B) Only the first is freed
C) Only the last is freed
D) None are freed

Key Takeaway
Bisharah is defined as pleasing news unknown to the hearer; in oaths of manumission, only the first bearer of such news triggers freedom unless all bear simultaneously.

Purchasing a Relative for Expiation and Sworn Manumission
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute on buying a relative for expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on purchasing a relative for expiation, identify the opposing view, and distinguish it from the case of sworn manumission.

Core Idea
Buying your enslaved father for expiation is valid, but buying a slave you swore to free does not count.

This lesson examines a nuanced dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i (along with Zufar) regarding whether purchasing and freeing one's enslaved father can fulfill an expiation obligation. The ruling hinges on how we define the 'cause' of manumission and when intention must accompany it. We will also compare this with the related case of a man who swears to free a specific slave and later buys him intending expiation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text presents two distinct cases: buying father for expiation versus buying a slave one swore to free. The ruling differs in each.
The first is valid; the second is not.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for the first scenario
Hanafis rule that purchasing one's father (who is a slave) for the purpose of expiation is valid and suffices for the kaffarah.
3
Analyze the opposing proof
Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue that the cause of manumission here is kinship, not the intention of expiation, so the intention does not coincide with the cause, making it invalid.
They require intention to accompany the cause ('illat al-'itq).
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response and proof
Hanafis cite the hadith: 'A child does not fulfill for his father unless he finds him a slave and buys and frees him.' They argue that the purchase itself is the act of manumission, so intention accompanies the purchase. This hadith is explicit evidence.
The hadith is narrated by all except al-Bukhari.
5
Contrast with the second scenario
If a man swears 'If I buy you, you are free' regarding another's slave, and later buys him intending expiation, it does not suffice because the cause of manumission is the oath, and the intention for expiation must accompany the oath, not the purchase.
The slave is freed by the oath, but the expiation is not fulfilled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح شراء أبيه للكفارة لا شراء من حلف بعتقه، للكفارة بأن كانت عليه كفارة صوم مثلا فاشترى أباه العبد لذلك. أي كفارة يمينه، ومثلها كفارة الظهار والصوم والقتل كان الأليق بهذه المسألة. مع ما بعدها فصل الكفارة، وأشار بالشراء إلى أنه لا يجزئه عنها بالإرث؛ لأنه يثبت فيه الملك بلا اختياره فلا تتصور النية فيه. وصورة المسألة أنه كانت عليه كفارة يمين أو صوم أو غيرهما، فاشترى أباه العبد لذلك، يصح ويجزئ عن الكفارة. وقال زفر والشافعي: لا يجوز، والأصل فيه أن النية إذا قارنت علة العتق ورق المعتق كامل، صح التكفير، وإلا فلا، فعندهما علة العتق القرابة، فالنية لم تتصل بها فلا يصح، وعندنا شراء القريب إعتاق، قال عليه: "لا يجزئ ولد والده إلا أن يجده مملوكاً فيشتريه فيعتقه"، رواه الجماعة غير البخاري، أي يعتقد بذلك الشراء؛ لأنه لا يحتاج للعتق إلى شيء آخر، وقد اقترنت النية به، فوجب القول بجوازه. لا شراء إلخ بأن قال لعبد غيره: إن اشتريتك فأنت حر، فاشتراه ناوياً به العتق عن كفارته، فإنه لا يجزئه عن الكفارة؛ لأن هذه النية يشترط قرانها لعلة العتق وهي اليمين والفرض أنه لم ينو عند التكلم بل عند مباشرة الشراء، فيعتق ولا يجزئ عن الكفارة.
And the purchase of his father for expiation is valid, but not the purchase of one whom he swore to free. For expiation: for example, if he owes a kaffarah of fasting, he buys his father who is a slave for that purpose. That is, the expiation of his oath, and similarly the expiation of zihar, fasting, and killing – this is more appropriate for this issue. Along with what comes after, the chapter on expiation. He indicated by 'purchase' that it does not suffice by inheritance because ownership is established without his choice, so intention cannot be conceived in it. The scenario: he owes expiation for an oath, fasting, or other, and buys his father who is a slave for that purpose – it is valid and suffices for the expiation. Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: it is not permissible. The principle is that if intention accompanies the cause of manumission and the freed slave is a complete slave, the expiation is valid; otherwise not. According to them, the cause of manumission is kinship, so intention did not accompany it; thus it is not valid. According to us, buying a relative is manumission. The Prophet ﷺ said: 'A child does not fulfill (the duty) for his father unless he finds him as a slave and buys him and frees him,' narrated by all except al-Bukhari. That is, he achieves it by that purchase because he needs no other act for manumission, and intention has accompanied it, so we must rule its permissibility. 'Not the purchase of one whom he swore to free': that is, if he said to another's slave, 'If I buy you, you are free,' and he buys him intending expiation, it does not suffice because this intention must accompany the cause of manumission, which is the oath, and it is assumed he did not intend at the utterance but at the purchase, so he is freed but it does not count for expiation.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and his followers) and Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Zufar (a Hanafi who sometimes disagreed). The issue: can a person who owes an expiation (kaffarah) – such as for a broken oath, zihar, intentional killing, or missed fast – buy his own father (who is a slave) and free him, and does that count as the expiation? The Hanafi ruling: yes, it is valid. The proof is a hadith where the Prophet ﷺ said a child cannot repay his father except by finding him enslaved, buying and freeing him. This indicates that the purchase and subsequent freeing is considered a valid manumission for expiation. The opponents (Zufar and al-Shafi'i) argue that the 'cause' ('illah) of manumission here is kinship, not the expiation intention, so the intention does not coincide with the cause. But the Hanafis respond that the purchase itself is the manumission (the Prophet called it 'i'taqahu' – 'frees him'), so the intention is present at the moment of purchase. A second issue: if a person swore 'If I buy you, you are free' regarding another's slave, and then buys him with the intention of expiation, it does not count because the cause of manumission is the oath (made at the time of utterance), and the intention for expiation came only at the purchase, not at the oath. Therefore the slave is freed by the oath, but the expiation is not fulfilled.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation – a religious obligation to compensate for certain sins or omissions (e.g., broken oath, zihar).
علة العتق ('illat al-'itq): The effective cause of manumission – in this context, whether it is the oath or the kinship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears an oath and then buys his father who is a slave, intending this purchase and manumission to count as expiation. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) It is valid and suffices
B) It is invalid
C) Valid only if he had no other means
D) Valid only if he says 'I intend expiation' at the oath

Key Takeaway
Buying and freeing one's enslaved father fulfills expiation in the Hanafi school, provided the intention is present at the purchase; this is supported by prophetic hadith.

Oath on Receiving Good News (Bushra)
Ruling on oath rewarding a slave for good news

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an oath tied to receiving good news (bushra) and distinguish it from a simple news (khabar) oath.

Core Idea
Bushra requires the recipient's ignorance; khabar does not — a single condition changes the entire outcome.

In this lesson, we examine a passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq that rules on an oath where a master promises freedom to any slave who brings him good news. The key distinction is between 'bushra' (good news) and 'khabar' (news). We will see how the Hanafi school defines bushra and how that definition determines whether one slave or multiple slaves are freed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its condition
The master says: 'Every slave who gives me good news (bushra) of such-and-such is free.' Note the term bushra.
2
Define bushra legally
The commentary defines bushra as joyful news of which the recipient has no prior knowledge, and it must be true. This is contrasted with khabar (news), where ignorance is not required.
3
Apply to separate notifications
If three slaves give news one after another, only the first is freed because the good news is fully realized from him; the others' news is redundant since the recipient now knows.
4
Apply to simultaneous notifications
If they give news together, all are freed because the good news is realized from all at the same moment; the Quranic verse (51:28) supports that bushra can come from a group.
5
Address the master's claim of intent
If the master says he intended only one, he is not believed in court but may choose privately between himself and Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين في الطلاق والعتاق مذ ملك، كلّ عبدٍ بشرني بكذا فهو حقٌّ، فبشره ثلاثة متفرقون عتق الأول، وإن بشروه معا عتقوا، مذ ملك: أي من حين ملك وهو وقت الشراء، حتى اعتبر من جميع المال لو اشتراه في الصحة. (عيني) بكذا: أي بقدوم زيد مثلاً السفر أو بعافية مريضه. (عيني) عتق الأول : إذا قال: كل عبد بشرني بكذا فهو حر، فبشره ثلاثة، واحد بعد واحد عتق المبشر الأول؛ لتحقق البشارة منه دون الباقين؛ لأن البشارة عرفاً اسم لخبر سار ليس للمبشر به علم، ويشترط كون الخبر صادقاً وقد حصل ذلك بالأول، بخلاف الخبر حيث لا يشترط عدم العلم به حتى لو قال: من أخبرني بقدوم فلان فهو حر، فأخبره ثلاثة متفرقون عتقوا، بشرط أن يكون الخبر صادقاً، والبشارة تكون بكتابة ورسالة كما يكون الخبر بهما، وروي أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قال لابن مسعود له وهو يقرأ القرآن ومعه أبو بكر، فقال عليه الصلاة والسلام: "من أراد أن يقرأ القرآن فليقرأ بقراءة ابن أم عبد"، فأخبره بذلك أبو بكر ثم عمر، فكان ابن مسعود يقول: بشرني أبو بكر، وأخبرني عمر. (عيني فتح) وإن بشروه معا: أي إن بشر العبيد الثلاثة المولى بذلك. (عيني) عتقوا لأن البشارة تحققت من الكل على المعنى الذي سبق، وقد قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَبَشِّرُوهُ بِغُلَامٍ عَلِيمٍ﴾ (الذريات:28)، فدل على أنه يتحقق البشارة من الجماعة، ولو قال: عنيت واحداً لم يدين في القضاء، بل فيما بينه الله تعالى، فيختار منهم من شاء فيمضي عتقه ويمسك البقية. (مستخلص، فتح)
Chapter of the oath in divorce and manumission. From the moment he owns, every slave who gives me good news of such-and-such is free; if three give him good news separately, the first is freed; and if they give him good news together, they are all freed. [Commentary:] “From the moment he owns”: i.e. from the time he owns, which is the time of purchase, so it is considered from all his property if he bought it in health. “Of such-and-such”: e.g. the arrival of Zayd from travel or the recovery of his sick. “The first is freed”: if he says: every slave who gives me good news of such-and-such is free, and three give him news one after another, the first giver of news is freed because the good news is verified from him and not from the others; because “good news” (bushra) in custom is the name for joyful news of which the one being told has no knowledge, and it is conditioned that the news be true, and that was obtained from the first. This is unlike “news” (khabar) where lack of knowledge is not a condition; so if he says: whoever tells me of the arrival of so-and-so is free, and three tell him separately, they are all freed on condition that the news be true. Good news can be by writing and message just as news can be by them. It is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to Ibn Mas‘ud as he was reciting the Quran with Abu Bakr, and he said: “Whoever wants to recite the Quran should recite according to the recitation of Ibn Umm ‘Abd.” Abu Bakr informed him of that, then ‘Umar, and Ibn Mas‘ud used to say: Abu Bakr gave me good news, and ‘Umar informed me. “And if they give him good news together”: i.e. if the three slaves give the master that good news together. They are all freed because the good news is verified from all according to the meaning previously mentioned. Allah Almighty said: “And they gave him good news of a forbearing boy” (Quran 51:28), so it indicates that good news is verified from a group. And if he says: I meant one, he is not believed in court, but only between him and Allah Almighty, so he may choose among them whom he wills and carry out his manumission and keep the rest.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It deals with an oath where a master says: 'Every slave who gives me good news (bushra) of such-and-such is free.' The ruling distinguishes between separate and simultaneous notification. If three slaves give news separately, only the first is freed because the good news is fully realized with the first truthful informant. If they give news together, all are freed because the good news is realized from all simultaneously. The commentary defines bushra as joyful news unknown to the recipient, unlike mere khabar (news) which does not require ignorance. The proof is from the Quranic verse where angels gave good news to Ibrahim. The report about Ibn Mas'ud illustrates the distinction between bushra and khabar. The ruling also addresses judicial vs. inner truth: if the master claims he intended only one, he is not believed in court but may choose in private between him and Allah.

Key Terms
بشارة (bushra): Joyful news of which the recipient has no prior knowledge; conditioned on truthfulness.
يمين (yamin): An oath that triggers a legal consequence (here, freeing a slave) upon fulfillment of a condition.
عتق (itq): Manumission; freeing a slave, which is the consequence of the oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a master says 'Every slave who gives me good news (bushra) of such-and-such is free,' and three slaves give him the news one after another, what is the ruling?
A) All three are freed.
B) Only the first is freed.
C) Only the last is freed.
D) None are freed.

Key Takeaway
Bushra (good news) is legally distinct from khabar (news) by requiring the recipient's ignorance, which determines how many slaves are freed under a conditional oath.
