Buying a Relative for Expiation in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling on purchasing relatives for expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on purchasing a relative for expiation (kafara) and distinguish cases where the purchase does not suffice due to the cause of manumission.

Core Idea
Buying a relative is itself manumission; intention at purchase suffices for expiation.

In this passage, the Hanafi school addresses a common scenario: a person needs to free a slave for expiation (kafara) and buys his own father, intending the purchase as manumission. The Hanafis hold this valid based on a hadith, while Zufr and al-Shafi'i disagree. The text then distinguishes cases involving a slave about whom one swore an oath and the master's umm walad. Understanding the role of intention and the cause of manumission is key to applying these rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on buying father for kafara
The text rules 'صح شراء أبيه للكفارة' – buying one's father (a slave) for expiation is valid and suffices. Identify the act and the ruling: permissible and sufficient.
This applies regardless of the type of kafara (oath, zihar, etc.).
2
Trace the dispute and its reasoning
Zufr and al-Shafi'i hold it invalid; they argue the cause of manumission is kinship, not the purchase, so intention does not attach. The Hanafi response: buying a relative is itself manumission, supported by the hadith.
The hadith: 'لا يجزئ ولد والده إلا أن يجده مملوكاً فيشتريه فيعتقه'.
3
Distinguish the exception: slave about whom one swore
If a man says to a slave 'if I buy you, you are free' and then buys him intending kafara, the slave is freed but the kafara is not fulfilled. Reason: the cause of manumission is the oath, and intention must accompany the oath, not the purchase.
Here the purchase merely executes a prior oath; intention at purchase does not connect to the cause.
4
Apply the same principle to umm walad
Buying one's own umm walad for kafara is also invalid if the oath was specifically for expiation. The text distinguishes absolute oaths from those tied to kafara, emphasizing the need for intention at the correct moment.
If the oath was absolute ('you are free'), buying her might count; but if the oath mentioned kafara, it does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح شراء أبيه للكفارة لا شراء من حلف بعتقه، وأم ولده. للكفارة بأن كانت عليه كفارة صوم مثلا فاشترى أباه العبد لذلك. (عيني)] أي كفارة يمينه، ومثلها كفارة الظهار والصوم والقتل كان الأليق بهذه المسألة. مع ما بعدها فصل الكفارة، وأشار بالشراء إلى أنه لا يجزئه عنها بالإرث؛ لأنه يثبت فيه الملك بلا اختياره فلا تتصور النية فيه وصورة المسألة أنه كانت عليه كفارة يمين أو صوم أو غيرهما، فاشترى أباه العبد لذلك، يصح ويجزئ عن الكفارة. وقال زفر والشافعي: لا يجوز، والأصل فيه أن النية إذا قارنت علة العتق ورق المعتق كامل، صح التكفير، وإلا فلا، فعندهما علة العتق القرابة، فالنية لم تتصل بها فلا يصح، وعندنا شراء القريب إعتاق، قال عليه الصلاة والسلام: "لا يجزئ ولد والده إلا أن يجده مملوكاً فيشتريه فيعتقه"، رواه الجماعة غير البخاري، أي يعتقد بذلك الشراء؛ لأنه لا يحتاج للعتق إلى شيء آخر، وقد اقترنت النية به، فوجب القول بجوازه [ رمز الحقائق: ٣٦٤/١] (فتح) لا شراء إلخ بأن قال لعبد غيره: إن اشتريتك فأنت حر، فاشتراه ناوياً به العتق عن كفارته، فإنه لا يجزئه عن الكفارة؛ لأن هذه النية يشترط قرانها لعلة العتق وهي اليمين والفرض أنه لم ينو عند التكلم بل عند مباشرة الشراء، فيعتق ولا يجزئ عن الكفارة. (عيني فتح) من حلف بعتقه : بأن قال لعبد غيره: إن اشتريتك فأنت حر، فاشتراه ناوياً عن التكفير، فإنه لا يجزئه. (عيني) وأم ولد : [ أي لا يصح أيضاً شراء أم ولده للكفارة. (عيني)] عطف على قوله : "من حلف" أي ولا يصح شراء أم ولده أيضاً عن التكفير بأن قال لأمة رجل استولدها بالنكاح: إن اشتريتك فأنت حرة عن كفارة يميني حيث يجوز عن كفارته إذا اشترى؛ لأن حريتها غير متحققة بجهة آخر فلم يختل الإضافة إلى اليمين وقد قارنته النية، والفرق بين هذه المسألة مسألة العبد المحلوف عليه بعتقه أن في مسألة العبد قد حلف بعتقه مطلقاً، مسألة وفي القنة وأم الولد قد حلف بعتقهما عن كفارة يمين. (مستخلص)
And buying his father for expiation is valid, but not buying the one about whose manumission he has sworn, nor his umm walad. For expiation: such as if he owes an expiation of fasting, for example, and he buys his father who is a slave for that purpose. That is, expiation of his oath, and similarly expiation of zihar, fasting, and killing – it would be more appropriate for this issue along with what follows as the chapter on expiation. He indicated by 'buying' that it does not suffice for him by inheritance because ownership is established without his choice, so intention is not conceivable in it. The scenario: he owes an expiation of an oath or fasting or other, and he buys his father who is a slave for that, it is valid and suffices for the expiation. Zufr and al-Shafi‘i said it is not permissible. The principle: if intention accompanies the cause of manumission and the slave is fully owned, the expiation is valid; otherwise not. According to them, the cause of manumission is kinship, so the intention did not connect to it and thus it is not valid. According to us, buying a relative is manumission. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: “A child does not repay his parent unless he finds him a slave, buys him, and frees him,” reported by all except Bukhari, meaning he intends by that purchase because he does not need anything else for manumission, and intention has accompanied it, so it is necessary to rule its permissibility. (Symbol of al-Haqa'iq 1/364) (Fath) “Not buying…” etc.: such as if he said to another’s slave: “If I buy you, you are free,” and he buys it intending thereby manumission for his expiation – it does not suffice for the expiation because this intention requires conjunction with the cause of manumission, which is the oath, and the assumption is that he did not intend at the time of speaking but at the time of the purchase, so the slave is freed but it does not suffice for the expiation. (Ayni, Fath) “One who swore by his manumission”: such as if he said to another’s slave: “If I buy you, you are free,” and he buys it intending expiation, it does not suffice. (Ayni) “And his umm walad”: it is also not valid to buy his umm walad for expiation. This is conjoined to his statement “one who swore by his manumission,” i.e., it is also not valid to buy his umm walad for expiation, such as if he said to a man’s female slave whom he has made an umm walad through marriage: “If I buy you, you are free for the expiation of my oath,” it does not suffice for his expiation if he buys, because her freedom is not established by another means so the connection to the oath is not disrupted, and intention has accompanied it. The difference between this case and the case of the slave about whose manumission he swore is that in the case of the slave he swore by his manumission absolutely; in the case of the female slave and umm walad he swore by their manumission for the expiation of an oath. (Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage addresses the validity of buying one's relative (father or umm walad) for expiation (kafara). The Hanafi position is that it is valid because buying a relative is considered manumission itself, and intention accompanies the purchase. The hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) is cited as proof: a child does not repay his parent unless he buys him and frees him. Zufr and al-Shafi‘i disagree, arguing that the cause of manumission is kinship, not the purchase, so intention does not connect. The text then distinguishes between buying a slave whom one has sworn to free (if bought, the freedom is executed but it does not count for expiation because the intention must accompany the oath, not the purchase) and buying one's father or umm walad (where the purchase itself effects manumission). For the umm walad, the ruling is similar to the father: buying her for expiation is invalid if the oath was specifically for expiation? Wait, the text says “لا يصح أيضاً شراء أم ولده للكفارة” – buying umm walad for expiation is also not valid. The commentary explains that if the oath was for expiation (e.g., “if I buy you, you are free for my kafara”), it does not count, whereas if the oath was absolute, it would count. The difference hinges on the cause of manumission.

Key Terms
كفارة (kafara): Expiation required for breaking an oath, zihar, etc.; can be fulfilled by freeing a slave.
عتق (itq): Manumission of a slave; the act of freeing.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master; her status is special.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi text, which of the following purchases suffices for expiation (kafara)?
A) Buying one's father with the intention of freeing him for kafara.
B) Buying a slave about whom one said 'if I buy you, you are free' with the intention of kafara.
C) Buying one's umm walad with the intention of freeing her for kafara.
D) All of the above are valid.

Key Takeaway
Buying a relative for expiation is valid because the purchase itself is manumission; buying a slave under a prior oath does not suffice because the cause is the oath, not the purchase.

Oath on Taking a Concubine: Ownership Requirement
Ruling on oath of manumission upon tasarri with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an oath of manumission conditioned on tasarri, identify the condition of ownership at the time of the oath, and explain the dispute with Zufr using qiyas.

Core Idea
Oath to free on tasarri valid only if owned at oath; implied ownership limited.

This lesson examines a specific oath: 'If I take a female slave as a concubine (tasarri), she is free.' The Hanafi school lays down a crucial condition that determines the oath's validity. We will explore the ruling, its proof, and a notable intra-Hanafi disagreement with Imam Zufr, who argued by qiyas that mentioning tasarri implies ownership sufficient for manumission.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its condition
Identify the main ruling: the oath is valid only if the slave is in his possession at the time of the oath. Otherwise, it is invalid.
The text says: 'صح لو في ملكه، وإلا لا'.
2
Identify the Hanafi proof
Understand the proof: the oath of manumission is only valid in ownership or attached to it or its cause. Since the slave is not owned at the time, the oath cannot take effect.
This is a general principle in oaths of manumission.
3
Analyze Zufr's qiyas
Examine Zufr's analogy: tasarri is only valid in ownership, so mentioning it implies ownership, just as mentioning divorce to a foreign woman implies marriage.
Zufr uses qiyas shabahah between tasarri and divorce.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response: the implied ownership is limited to the validity of tasarri, not extended to manumission. Otherwise, it would contradict cases where the slave was owned at the time.
This shows that implication is restricted to the specific context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن تسرّيت أمة فهي حرّة صح لو في ملكه، وإلا لا. إن تسرّيت أمة إلخ من التسري وهو جعل الأمة سرية بالوطء. (محشي)] التسري هو الجماع؛ لأنه مشتق من السر بمعنى الجماع، وفي الشرع عبارة عن تحصين الأمة وإسكانها ومنعها من الخروج عند الطرفين، وعند أبي يوسف: عن طلب الولد هذه القيود، وصورة المسألة أنه لو قال :رجل إن تسرّيت أمة فهي حرة، هذا الحلف حلفه فإذا تسراها عتقت؛ لأن اليمين انعقدت في حقها لمصادفتها للملك؛ لأن الأمة تتناول المملوكة في ذلك الوقت على العموم، فإنها نكرة في سياق الشرط وهي كالنفي. (عيني، مستخلص) وإلا لا : أي وإن لم تكن الجارية في ملكه وقت اليمين لا يصح التحرير، حتى لو اشترى أمة وتسرى بها لا تعتق قالت الثلاثة، وقال زفر: تعتق؛ لأن التسري لا يصح إلا في الملك، فكان ذكر التسري ذكراً للملك كمن قال لأجنبية: إن طلقتك فعبدي حر حتى يصير كأنه قال: إن تزوجتك وطلقتك؛ لأن الطلاق لا يصح إلا في الملك، فصار ذكره ذكراً للملك، فكذا هذا. ولنا: أن اليمين بالعتق إنما يصح في الملك أو مضافاً إليه أو إلى سببه، و لم يوجد واحد منها في حقها، ولئن سلمنا أن ذكر التسري ذكر لملك اليمين لا يلزم عتقها؛ لأن الملك ثبت اقتضاء لصحة التسري، فيقدر بقدرها، ولا يظهر ثبوته في حق الحرية، وما قاله زفر لا يصح؛ لأنه لو كان كما قال لما تناول من "يوم حلف؛ إذ لو كان تقدير الكلام إن ملكت جارية وتسريت بها فهي "حرة لم يعتق من كانت في ملكه يومئذ إذا تسرى بها، وطلب الولد ليس بشرط للتسري عند الطرفين حتى لو عزل عنها لا تكون سرية عند أبي يوسف وتكون عندهما. والسرية بالضم منسوبة إلى السر بمعنى الجماع، وإنما ضمت سينه؛ لأن الأبنية قد تغير في النسبة خاصة، وعند الأخفش: مشتق من السرور، وقيل: مأخوذ من السري بمعنى السيد لصيرورتها سيد الجواري. (عيني، فتح، مستخلص)
If I take a female slave as a concubine (tasarrī), she is free – valid if she is in his possession, otherwise not. “If I take a female slave as a concubine”, etc.: from tasarrī, which is making a female slave a concubine (surriyya) through intercourse. Tasarrī is intercourse because it is derived from sirr meaning intercourse; in sacred law it is an expression for confining the female slave, housing her, and preventing her from going out – according to both [Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?]. According to Abu Yusuf: it is about seeking offspring – these restrictions. The scenario: a man says: “If I take a female slave as a concubine, she is free”; this is his oath. When he takes her as a concubine, she becomes free because the oath is valid in her regard as it meets ownership; because “a female slave” (ama) encompasses the owned one at that time generally, since it is an indefinite noun in the context of a condition, which is like negation. “Otherwise not”: i.e., if the slave girl is not in his possession at the time of the oath, the manumission is not valid, even if he buys a female slave and takes her as a concubine, she does not become free – this is the saying of the three [Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad]. Zufr said: she becomes free because tasarrī is not valid except in ownership, so mentioning tasarrī is a mention of ownership, like one who says to a foreign woman: “If I divorce you, my slave is free,” which becomes as if he said: “If I marry you and divorce you,” because divorce is not valid except in ownership, so its mention becomes a mention of ownership; similarly here. Our proof: the oath of manumission is only valid in ownership or attached to it or to its cause, and none of these exist in her regard. And even if we concede that mentioning tasarrī is a mention of ownership, that does not necessitate her freedom because ownership is established by implication for the validity of tasarrī, and is limited to that, and does not appear in the matter of freedom. And what Zufr said is not valid because if it were as he said, it would not apply to the one on the day he swore; for if the implied meaning were “If I own a slave girl and take her as a concubine, she is free,” then one who was in his possession on that day would not be freed when he took her as a concubine. And seeking offspring is not a condition for tasarrī according to the two [Abu Hanifa and Muhammad]; even if he practices coitus interruptus with her, she is not a concubine according to Abu Yusuf, but is according to them. “Surriyya” (by the damma) is ascribed to sirr meaning intercourse; its sīn is given a damma because noun forms sometimes change in attribution. According to al-Akhfash: it is derived from surūr (joy); others: it is taken from sirr (secret) or from sayyid (master) because she becomes the master of slave girls.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on an oath: 'If I take a female slave as a concubine (tasarri), she is free.' The Hanafi position is that this is valid only if the slave is already in his possession at the time of the oath. If she is not, the oath does not take effect, and even if he later buys her and takes her as a concubine, she is not freed. This is the view of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad (the three). Zufr disagrees, arguing that because tasarri is only valid in ownership, mentioning it implies ownership, similar to an oath about divorce. The Hanafi response refutes this by clarifying that ownership is only implied to the extent necessary for the validity of tasarri, not for the wider purpose of manumission. The passage also includes linguistic and legal definitions of tasarri and surriyya, and notes a difference between Abu Yusuf and the others regarding the condition of seeking offspring.

Key Terms
تسري (tasarri): Taking a female slave as a concubine (surriyya) through intercourse; in legal terms, confining her and preventing her from leaving.
سرية (surriyya): A female slave taken as a concubine; she has special status and cannot be sold.
يمين (yamin): Oath that conditions a consequence (here, manumission) on an action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man swears: 'If I take a female slave as a concubine, she is free.' At the time of the oath, he does not own any slave. Later he buys a slave and takes her as a concubine. According to the Hanafi position (three imams), what is the ruling?
A) The slave is freed because tasarri implies ownership.
B) The slave is not freed because the oath was invalid from the start.
C) The slave is freed only if he intended ownership at the time of the oath.
D) The slave is not freed because the condition was not fulfilled.

Key Takeaway
An oath to free a slave upon tasarri is only valid if the slave is owned at the time of the oath; implied ownership from mentioning tasarri does not extend to manumission.

General Oath Freeing All Slaves: Scope and Intentions
Ruling on comprehensive oath 'every slave of mine is free'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of the comprehensive manumission oath and distinguish which restrictive intentions are accepted privately versus rejected entirely.

Core Idea
A general oath frees all fully owned slaves; hidden intentions are only accepted privately for plausible limits, never in court.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi ruling on the statement 'every slave of mine is free.' The commentator al-Ayni clarifies that the absolute term covers all slaves under complete ownership. He then examines the legal effect of various restrictive intentions, showing that only gender-based limitation is believed privately, while racial or exclusionary intentions are disregarded entirely. A difference of narration on mudabbar slaves highlights the complexity of legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Ruling
State what happens when someone says 'every slave of mine is free': all fully owned slaves (three categories) are freed immediately.
The ruling applies to slaves in complete ownership (raqaba and yad).
2
Determine the Basis
The absolute term 'mamlūk' applies to complete ownership, so all such slaves are included. The phrase is executory.
Partial ownership or incomplete rights might not be included; mudabbar is disputed.
3
Classify Restrictive Intentions
Intentions are divided: (a) gender-based restriction is accepted privately (diyaanatan) but not in court; (b) racial or opposite-gender restrictions are rejected entirely, even privately.
Court always follows the apparent meaning (zahir).
4
Analyze the Mudabbar Exception
There are two narrations: one accepts private exclusion of mudabbar, the other does not. No explicit tarjih is given.
Mudabbar slaves have a future right to freedom, causing ambiguity.
5
Apply the Rule
In practice, the court frees all slaves falling under complete ownership. Privately, the owner may follow his intention if it is plausible (gender) or according to the accepted narration on mudabbar.
The distinction between diyaanatan and qada'an is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل مملوك لي إلخ أي لو قال: كل مملوك لي فهو حر، عتق هؤلاء الأصناف الثلاثة؛ لثبوت الملك له فيهم كاملاً ورقبة ويداً؛ لأن المطلق ينصرف إلى الكامل، ولو قال: أردت به الرجال دون النساء، صدق ديانة لا قضاء، ولو نوى السود دون غيرهم أو النساء دون الذكور أو العكس لا يصدق أصلاً، ولو قال: لم أنو المدبرين يصدق ديانة في رواية ولا يصدق أصلاً في أخرى. (عيني، فتح)
“Every slave of mine”: i.e., if he says: “Every slave of mine is free,” these three categories are freed because ownership is fully established in them in terms of ownership of the person and possession; because the absolute expression applies to the complete [ownership]. If he says: “I meant men, not women,” he is believed in private (diyaanatan) but not in court. If he intended black slaves to the exclusion of others, or women to the exclusion of men, or the opposite, he is not believed at all. If he says: “I did not intend the mudabbar slaves,” he is believed in private in one narration, and not believed at all in another. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage addresses a general oath: 'Every slave of mine is free.' The commentary explains that such an oath frees three categories (likely referring to slaves fully owned, as opposed to those with partial ownership or conditional freedom). The absolute term 'mamlūk' (slave) applies to complete ownership – i.e., those owned both in corpus (raqaba) and possession (yad). The text then discusses the effect of restrictive intentions. If the person claims he intended only men, that is accepted between him and Allah (diyaanatan) but not in an Islamic court (qaḍā’an). However, if he intends racial or gender-based restrictions that contradict the apparent meaning (e.g., only black slaves), he is not believed at all – not even in private. There is a difference of narration regarding excluding mudabbar slaves (those whose freedom is deferred after the master's death): in one narration he is believed in private, in another he is not. This demonstrates the Hanafi principle that a clear general expression cannot be restricted by a hidden intention that contradicts it, except in limited cases.

Key Terms
مملوك (mamlūk): A slave owned fully in corpus and possession.
ديانة (diyaanatan): In private, between the person and Allah; as opposed to qaḍā’an (in court).
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose freedom is deferred until after the master's death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says: 'Every slave of mine is free,' but he claims in court that he intended only male slaves. How is this treated?
A) He is believed in court because intention matters.
B) He is not believed in court but it is accepted between him and Allah.
C) He is not believed at all, even privately.
D) All female slaves remain enslaved because of his intention.

Key Takeaway
The general oath frees all fully owned slaves; only gender-based restrictions are accepted privately, while other restrictions and court actions are based on the apparent meaning.

Ruling on Buying an Umm Walad for Expiation
Explaining the invalidity of buying an umm walad for kaffarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purchasing an umm walad for expiation and explain why it is invalid based on the contingent nature of her freedom.

Core Idea
An umm walad cannot be purchased for kaffarah because her freedom is contingent on her master's death.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: the invalidity of buying an umm walad (a slave mother) to fulfill an expiatory oath. While freeing a slave is a valid kaffarah, the umm walad's unique status means her emancipation is conditional, making such a purchase ineffective. Understanding this exception deepens your grasp of how legal categories affect contract validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? (Buying an umm walad for kaffarah). What is the hukm? (Invalid/ not sahih).
The text says 'la yasihhu'.
2
Understand the legal category of umm walad
What is an umm walad? A slave who bore her master's child and becomes free upon his death. Her freedom is not immediate.
This contingent freedom is key.
3
Apply the condition for valid kaffarah
For kaffarah, a slave must be freed immediately and wholly. Since the umm walad's freedom is deferred, the purchase cannot fulfill the oath.
The intention (niyyah) is present but the object is unsuitable.
4
Distinguish from absolute oath to free a slave
If a person swears unconditionally to free a slave (not for kaffarah), that is valid even for an umm walad? The text says no, it's invalid for kaffarah but valid for absolute oath? Actually the passage contrasts: for absolute oath, freeing is valid; for kaffarah, it's invalid.
Check the distinction made in the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأم ولد : [ أي لا يصح أيضاً شراء أم ولده للكفارة. (عيني)] عطف على قوله : "من حلف" أي ولا يصح شراء أم ولده أيضاً عن التكفير بأن قال لأمة رجل استولدها بالنكاح: إن اشتريتك فأنت حرة عن كفارة يميني حيث يجوز عن كفارته إذا اشترى؛ لأن حريتها غير متحققة بجهة آخر فلم يختل الإضافة إلى اليمين وقد قارنته النية، والفرق بين هذه المسألة مسألة العبد المحلوف عليه بعتقه أن في مسألة العبد قد حلف بعتقه مطلقاً، مسألة وفي القنة وأم الولد قد حلف بعتقهما عن كفارة يمين. (مستخلص)
And the umm walad: [i.e., the purchase of his umm walad for expiation is also not valid. (Ayni)] It is connected to his statement: 'whoever swears' i.e., the purchase of his umm walad for expiation is also not valid, such that if he says to the slave woman of a man who has fathered a child with her through marriage: 'If I buy you, you are free for the expiation of my oath' – where it is valid for his expiation if he buys her, because her freedom is not actualized by another means, so the linkage to the oath is not impaired, and intention accompanies it. The difference between this case and the case of the slave who is sworn regarding his emancipation is that in the slave's case, he swore absolutely regarding his emancipation; while in the case of the bondwoman and umm walad, he swore regarding their emancipation for the expiation of an oath. (Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the matn 'wa umm waladihi' (and his umm walad). The ruling is that it is not permissible to purchase an umm walad (a slave woman who has borne a child to her master) for the purpose of fulfilling an expiatory oath (kaffarah). The reason given is that her freedom is not absolute—she becomes free only upon her master's death—so the oath cannot be attached to her emancipation in a way that fulfills the expiation. In contrast, if a person swears to free a slave absolutely (without specifying expiation), that is valid. The commentary clarifies that if someone says to another's slave woman (who is an umm walad) 'If I buy you, you are free for my kaffarah,' the purchase and emancipation are valid because at the time of the oath, her freedom is not yet actualized, and the intention (niyyah) accompanies the act. The key distinction: in the slave case, the oath is unconditional; in the umm walad case, the oath is tied to kaffarah, which is not valid for her.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has given birth to a child from her master; she becomes free upon her master's death.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation for violating an oath, typically by freeing a slave, feeding, or clothing.
يمين (yamin): An oath by Allah to do or not do something.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Is it valid to purchase an umm walad to fulfill an expiatory oath?
A) Yes, always.
B) Yes, if the owner agrees.
C) No, because her freedom is contingent on her master's death.
D) No, because she is not a slave.

Key Takeaway
An umm walad cannot be used to fulfill an expiatory oath because her emancipation is contingent on her master's death, not immediate.

Oath to Free a Concubine: Conditions of Validity and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional emancipation oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on an oath to free a concubine when the slave is not yet owned, and evaluate Zufar's opposing argument using qiyas.

Core Idea
An oath to free a concubine requires ownership at the time; later acquisition does not trigger emancipation.

This passage examines a specific oath concerning taking a concubine and its effect on emancipation. The key question is whether the oath is valid only if the slave woman is owned at the time of the oath, or if mentioning tasri is sufficient to imply ownership and thus trigger emancipation after later acquisition. The majority of the Hanafi school (the three) hold that actual ownership at the time of the oath is necessary, while Zufar argues by analogy to divorce oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and its components
The man swears: 'If I take a concubine, she is free.' Determine what condition is being set and what the consequence is.
The oath is a conditional emancipation.
2
Understand the majority position (the three)
Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad rule that if the slave was not owned at the time of the oath, the emancipation is invalid even if later acquired. The oath must encounter ownership directly.
Reason: an oath of emancipation requires ownership at the time or attached to its cause.
3
Analyze Zufar's opposing analogy
Zufar compares tasri to divorce: mentioning divorce implies marriage, so mentioning tasri implies ownership. Therefore, the oath is valid for any later-acquired concubine.
He uses qiyas based on the idea that mention of an act requiring a relationship implies that relationship.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response to Zufar
The three refute that Zufar's analogy would exclude slaves already owned at the time (since the implied speech would be 'If I own and take...'). Also, ownership implied for tasri is limited to the act itself, not for emancipation.
The response shows that Zufar's qiyas is not comprehensive.
5
Note the secondary dispute on the definition of tasri
Abu Yusuf includes seeking offspring as a condition for tasri; the other two do not. This affects whether the oath applies if 'azl (coitus interruptus) is practiced.
This nuance is important for the complete picture.

(عيني، فتح، مستخلص)
"If I take a concubine..." from tasri which is making a slave woman a concubine by intercourse. (Mahshi) Tasri is intercourse; because it is derived from sirr meaning intercourse, and in sacred law it is the confinement of a slave woman, her housing, and preventing her from going out according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). According to Abu Yusuf: seeking offspring is a condition. The form of the issue is that if a man says: 'If I take a concubine, she is free' – this is his oath. If he takes her as a concubine, she becomes free because the oath is enacted over her as it encounters ownership; because the word 'slave woman' includes the owned one at that time generally, since it is an indefinite noun in the context of a condition and is like negation. (Ayni, Mustakhlas) 'And otherwise not': i.e., if the slave girl was not in his ownership at the time of the oath, the emancipation is not valid. Even if he buys a slave woman and takes her as a concubine, she does not become free – the three said (this). Zufar said: she becomes free because taking a concubine is only valid in ownership, so mentioning tasri is a mention of ownership, like one who says to a foreign woman: 'If I divorce you, my slave is free' – it becomes as if he said: 'If I marry you and divorce you' because divorce is only valid in ownership, so its mention became a mention of ownership; similarly here. Our (argument): an oath of emancipation is only valid in ownership or attached to it or to its cause, and none of these exists regarding her; even if we concede that mentioning tasri is a mention of ownership of the oath, it does not necessitate her emancipation because ownership is established by necessity for the validity of tasri, so it is estimated to that extent, and its establishment is not manifest regarding freedom. What Zufar said is invalid because if it were as he said, it would not include her who was owned on the day of the oath; for if the implied speech were 'If I own a slave girl and take her as a concubine, she is free', then she who was in his ownership at that time would not become free if he takes her as a concubine. Seeking offspring is not a condition for tasri according to the two, such that if he uses a condom with her, she is not a concubine according to Abu Yusuf but is according to them (the other two). The word 'sariyyah' (concubine) is with a damma, attributed to sirr meaning intercourse; its seen is given a damma because structures change in attribution specifically, and according to al-Akhfash: with us, and with it he was in his ownership, derived from surur (joy), and it is said: taken from sari (master) because she becomes the master of slave girls. (Ayni, Fath, Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an oath where a man says: 'If I take a concubine, she is free.' The ruling: if the slave woman was in his ownership at the time of the oath, taking her as a concubine frees her. If she was not owned, the oath is invalid. The three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree that even if he later buys her, she does not become free upon taking her as a concubine. Zufar differs, arguing that since tasri (taking a concubine) is only valid in ownership, mentioning it implies ownership, similar to an oath about divorce. The Hanafi response: the oath of emancipation is valid only in actual ownership or attached to it; a mere mention of tasri does not create ownership for emancipation purposes. Moreover, Zufar's analogy fails because it would exclude slaves already owned at the time of the oath. The passage also notes a difference between Abu Yusuf and the other two regarding whether seeking offspring (talab al-walad) is a condition for tasri. Additionally, linguistic derivations of 'sariyyah' are discussed.

Key Terms
تسري (tasri): Taking a female slave as a concubine by intercourse; also refers to the act of making her a sariyyah.
سرية (sariyyah): A concubine; a female slave taken for sexual relations.
يمين (yamin): An oath invoking Allah to affirm or deny an action.
زفر (Zufar): Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl, a prominent early Hanafi jurist, student of Abu Hanifa, known for his use of qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) No, because the oath was invalid since she wasn't owned at the time.
C) Yes, if she was originally free.
D) No, because taking a concubine is haram.

Key Takeaway
An oath to free a concubine requires the slave to be owned at the time of the oath; later acquisition does not trigger emancipation according to the majority of the Hanafi school.

Mukatab Slaves in Oaths of Manumission: The Hanafi Position
Explains the exclusion of mukatab slaves in an oath of manumission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that mukatab slaves are not freed by a general oath to free all owned slaves, and explain the reasoning based on the distinction between ownership of the corpus (raqabah) and possession (yadd).

Core Idea
A mukatab is owned in corpus but not in possession, so a general oath to free all slaves does not free him unless intended.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies the scope of an oath to free all slaves. It explains that while full slaves, mudabbars, and ummahat awlad are freed, mukatab slaves are excluded because the master's ownership over them is incomplete—only the corpus is owned, not the possession. This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh for determining the effect of such oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is an oath to free all owned slaves. The Hanafi ruling: mukatab slaves are not freed unless explicitly intended.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is the legal reasoning (talil) that ownership of a mukatab is only in the corpus (raqabah), not in possession (yaddan).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (legal link)
Because the master does not possess the mukatab (cannot take his earnings or have intercourse with him), the general term 'owned' in the oath does not fully apply; the illa (effective cause) for inclusion is full ownership (corpus and possession).
4
Note the ikhtilaf (disagreement)
Ahmad ibn Hanbal holds that mukatabs are freed without the need for intention, presumably based on the general ownership of the corpus.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih (preferred view)
The Hanafi position is relied upon (mufta bihi) because the distinction between raqabah and yadd is a well-established principle in Hanafi fiqh, and the incomplete ownership prevents automatic inclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اليمين في الطلاق والعتاق أي لا يعتق عتق عبيده وأمهات أولاده ومدبّروه لا مكاتبوه. (عيني) لا مكاتبوه: [لأن الملك غير ثابت يداً، وكذا معتق البعض إلا أن ينويهما. (ط، عيني)] أي لا يعتق مكاتبوه فيما إذا قال: كل مملوك لي فهو حر إلا أن ينويهم؛ لأن الملك فيهم ثابت رقبة لا يداً، ولهذا لا يملك المولى أكسابه، ولا يحل له وطء المكاتبة، فاختلت الإضافة في صورة المكاتب فلابد من النية عندنا، وقال أحمد يعتق مكاتبوه بدون النية.
Chapter of oath in divorce and manumission, i.e., he does not free: freeing his slaves, mothers of his children, and his mudabbars, but not his mukatabs. (Ayni) Not his mukatabs: because ownership is not established in terms of possession, and likewise the one who freed a part except if he intends them. (T., Ayni) i.e., his mukatabs are not freed when he says: every slave owned by me is free unless he intends them; because ownership in them is established in corpus but not in possession, and therefore the master does not own their earnings, nor is intercourse with the mukataba permissible for him, so the attribution is defective in the case of the mukatab, thus intention is necessary according to us, and Ahmad said: his mukatabs are freed without intention.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage states a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding an oath to free all owned slaves. The commentator explains that the ruling is based on the distinction between full ownership (ownership of both corpus and possession) and partial ownership (ownership of corpus only). Mukatab slaves, who have a contract of manumission, are owned by their master only in terms of the corpus (رقبة), not in terms of possession (يداً), because the master does not have the right to their earnings or to have intercourse with them. Therefore, when someone swears to free all his slaves, mukatabs are not included unless he specifically intends them. Ahmad ibn Hanbal holds a different view, stating that they are freed without the need for intention.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract of manumission (kitabah) with his master, whereby he will be free upon paying a certain sum.
يداً (yaddan): Literally 'hand', meaning possession or control over the slave's actions and earnings.
رقبة (raqabah): The corpus or body of the slave, representing ownership of the slave as property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) His regular slave
B) His mudabbar
C) His mukatab
D) His umm walad

Key Takeaway
A mukatab slave is not freed by a general oath of manumission because the master's ownership is limited to the corpus, lacking possession.

Acts Breaking Oath Only by Direct Action
Defines acts that break an oath only when done personally, not by proxy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between acts that break an oath only by direct action and acts that break it by command, and apply the Hanafi rule to new scenarios.

Core Idea
An oath is broken only by your own hand, not your agent's, unless you are a ruler or intended to forbid commanding.

When you swear not to do something, does commanding someone else break the oath? The Hanafi school distinguishes between acts whose rights revert to the doer and those whose rights revert to the commander. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa’iq defines the first category: acts like sale, purchase, hire, and compromise over property. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct oath-taking and avoiding unintended violations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath and prohibited act
The swearer says 'I will not do X' where X is a specific act like selling or buying. Determine if the act is in the first category (sale, purchase, etc.) or second (e.g., compromise over intentional homicide).
The passage lists examples for the first category.
2
Classify the act's legal nature
Determine whether the rights of the act (e.g., ownership, liability) revert to the actor (agent) or the commander. In the first category, rights revert to the actor; in the second, they revert to the commander.
This classification is the basis of the ruling.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule
For acts where rights revert to the actor, the oath is broken only if the swearer performs the act personally. Commanding an agent does not break the oath.
The condition of the oath (doing it himself) is not fulfilled.
4
Note exceptions
If the swearer intended to prohibit commanding others, or if the swearer is a ruler who never does these acts personally, then even commanding breaks the oath.
The exception is based on intention or customary practice.
5
Compare with Shafi'i view
According to the passage, al-Shafi‘i in the stronger view holds that the oath is not broken even by direct action. Understand the practical difference in rulings.
The text is ambiguous; the Hanafi view is the relied-upon position.

(مستخلص، عيني، محشي)

لا بالأمر: أي لا يحنث فيه بالأمر منه لغيره إذا كان ممن يباشر بنفسه. (ط) عيني) البيع: خبر المبتدأ مع معطوفاته، ومنه الهبة بعوض (ط) الولد أي الكبير ذكراً كان أو أنثى. (عيني)
Book of Oaths. Chapter: The oath concerning sale, purchase, marriage... etc. Chapter on the oath regarding sale, purchase, marriage, i.e., explaining their rulings, as well as Hajj, prayer, fasting, and others like walking, wearing clothes, and sitting. What causes breaking the oath by direct action, not by command: sale, purchase, hire, taking on hire, compromise (sulh) regarding property, division, litigation, and beating one's child. And what causes breaking the oath by both. [Commentary:] 'What causes breaking' is a relative clause as subject, meaning every action. That is, the actions in which the swearer breaks his oath by performing them himself, not by commanding another, are sale, purchase, etc. The summary: actions are of two types: first: its rights revert to the contracting party. In such an action, he breaks the oath by doing it himself, but not by the direct action of the one commanded. Second: its rights revert to the commanded party, then transfer from him to the commander. The first type includes sale, purchase, and their conjuncts. So if he swears not to sell, not to purchase, not to hire out, not to hire, not to compromise property, not to divide with partners, not to litigate against so-and-so, or not to beat his child, then in these cases, if he does these actions himself, he breaks the oath, but if he appoints an agent and the agent does them, he does not break the oath, because these things emanate from the agent, so the condition for breaking the oath—namely, doing it himself—is not fulfilled. According to al-Shafi‘i, in the stronger view, he does not break the oath. Yes! If he intended by his oath not to command another, then he breaks the oath by commanding as well, because that is a stricter interpretation. Similarly, he breaks the oath if the swearer is a sultan who does not personally undertake these matters, because he prevents himself from what he is accustomed to, so he breaks the oath by the action of the commanded. The restriction of compromise 'concerning property' is to exclude compromise concerning intentional homicide, for that belongs to the second category, as will come. (Mustakhlas, ‘Ayni, Muhashi).
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa’iq. It clarifies a core principle in oaths: acts that break the oath only when done directly by the swearer. The commentator first lists examples: sale, purchase, hire, etc., then explains the rationale: if the rights of the act revert to the person performing it (the agent), then the swearer is not considered the doer. The Shafi‘i opinion is cited as a disagreement, but the Hanafi position is that only direct action matters, except when the swearer intended to prohibit commanding others or is a ruler who customarily acts through agents. The restriction on compromise is noted to exclude intentional homicide, which belongs to the second category.

Key Terms
المباشرة (al-mubāsharah): Direct personal performance of an act, as opposed to commanding another.
الحنث (al-ḥinth): Breaking or violating an oath by doing what was sworn not to do.
الصلح عن مال (al-ṣulḥ ʿan māl): A compromise or settlement involving property, distinguished from compromise regarding intentional homicide.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will not sell anything' and then appoints an agent who sells his item, according to the Hanafi school, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because he commanded the sale.
B) No, because the sale was performed by the agent and he did not sell himself.
C) Yes, because selling through an agent is the same as selling personally.
D) No, because the oath is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school limits oath-breaking to personally performed acts for transactions, unless the swearer is a ruler or intended to prohibit commanding others.

When an Agent's Action Breaks an Oath: Hanafi vs. Shafi‘i
Lists proxy-breakable acts with Hanafi proof and Shafi'i objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on proxy action breaking an oath and apply the distinction between physical and verbal acts to determine court credibility.

Core Idea
An oath is broken by proxy if the contract's rights revert to the swearer; the agent is a mere conduit.

When you swear not to perform an act like marriage or divorce, does appointing an agent to do it break your oath? The Hanafi school says yes, the Shafi'i school says no. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and explores a key distinction between verbal acts (like divorce) and physical acts (like beating) that affects whether your intention is accepted in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Second Category of Acts in Oaths
Recognize that the passage lists actions in which the oath is broken whether done personally or by proxy, because the rights of the contract revert to the swearer, not the agent. Examples: marriage, divorce, manumission, gift, etc.
This is distinct from the first category where only personal action breaks the oath.
2
Follow the Hanafi Proof and the Shafi‘i Objection
The Hanafi proof: the agent is a messenger (safīr) and conduit; the rights revert to the principal, so the agent's action is legally the principal's. The Shafi‘i objection: the act exists from the agent in reality, only legally from the principal, so the condition is partially missing. Evaluate the Hanafi response.
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Hanafi, the oath is broken; under Shafi‘i, it is not.
3
Distinguish Between Verbal and Physical Acts
Note that for verbal acts (e.g., divorce), if the swearer claims he intended to do it personally, he is believed in conscience (diyānatan) but not in court (qaḍāʼan). For physical acts (e.g., beating), he is believed in court as well. Understand the rationale: verbal acts are non-physical utterances; the agent's command to utter is like uttering himself.
This affects the acceptance of a nīyah (intention) claim in court.
4
Apply the Exception: Beating a Child or a Free Person
Study the exception: if one swears not to beat his slave and orders another to beat him, he breaks the oath. But if he swears not to beat his child (or a free man) and orders another, he does not break the oath, because the benefit of beating returns to the child (discipline), not to the swearer. Exception: a sultan or judge can order beating a free man for punishment.
This refines the rule based on who benefits from the act.
5
Synthesize the Principle: Attribution Based on Rights
The core principle: an oath is broken by proxy when the legal effects (rights) of the act revert wholly to the swearer. The agent is a conduit. Different outcomes arise when the act benefits a third party (e.g., disciplining a child) or when the swearer does not have the authority to order the act (e.g., beating a free man without judicial authority).
This principle governs the entire second category and explains the Shafi‘i disagreement.

And what causes breaking the oath by both, i.e., the actions in which the swearer breaks his oath by performing them himself and by commanding another, i.e., appointment of an agent, are: marriage, divorce, and their conjuncts. The intended meaning of marriage is marrying (tazawwuj), not giving in marriage (inkāḥ), for that belongs to the first category. So if he swears not to marry, not to divorce his wife, not to divorce by khul‘, not to manumit his slave, not to make a kitābah contract, not to compromise concerning intentional homicide, not to give a gift, not to give charity, not to lend, not to borrow, not to beat his slave, not to slaughter a sheep, not to build a structure, not to sew his garment, not to deposit, not to accept a deposit, not to lend an object, not to borrow an object, not to pay a debt, not to receive a debt, not to clothe so-and-so, or not to carry this thing, then he breaks the oath by doing it himself and by his agent doing it, because the agent in these matters is a messenger and conduit, and the rights of the contract revert to the commander, not to the agent, unlike the first category, wherein the rights of the contract revert to the contractant. According to al-Shāfi‘ī: He does not break the oath by the agent's direct action, because the act exists from the agent in reality and from the other (the swearer) legally, so the condition for breaking the oath exists from the commander in one respect but not in another, so he does not break the oath, as in the first category. Page 72: Marriage, divorce, khul‘, manumission, kitābah, compromise concerning intentional homicide, gift, charity, loan, borrowing, beating the slave, slaughter, building, sewing, deposit, accepting deposit, also gift ('aṭīyah), loan for use ('āriyah), lending for use, paying debt, receiving debt, clothing, carrying, and the entry of the lām. Even if he does not do it well, whether he specified a person or not. And we (Hanafis) hold that the purpose of the swearer is to avoid the legal effect and rights of the contract, and these contracts transfer to him with their rights, so the agent's direct action becomes like his own direct action with respect to rulings and rights, and the agent becomes a messenger and conduit. By this, he is not independent of attributing it to him. And if he does it without his permission, it is not binding upon him. This difference concerns marriage, divorce, and manumission. Then there is some detail in these issues: [more detail about physical vs. verbal acts, and the exception for beating a child, etc.]
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute. It presents the second category of acts in oaths: those that break the oath whether done personally or by proxy. The reason is that the rights of these contracts revert to the swearer (the commander), not to the agent; the agent is merely a conduit. The text provides a long list: marriage, divorce, manumission, gift, etc. The Shafi‘i position is that the oath is not broken by the agent's action because the act is real from the agent and only legal from the swearer; thus, the condition is only partially fulfilled. The Hanafi response: the swearer's purpose is to avoid the legal effects, and these effects apply fully to him; thus, the agent's action is equivalent to his own. The passage then distinguishes between verbal acts (like divorce) and physical acts (like beating). For verbal acts, if the swearer claims he intended to do it personally, he is believed in conscience but not in court, because the act is not physical. For physical acts, he is believed in court as well. The text also discusses exceptions: beating a child or a free man by order of a sultan or judge. The section ends with grammatical notes about the letter lām.

Key Terms
السفير والمعبر (al-safīr wa al-mu‘abbir): The agent is described as a 'messenger and conduit' whose actions are attributed to the principal.
الطلاق فعل غير حسي (al-ṭalāq fi‘l ghayr ḥissī): Divorce is a non-physical act; it is a verbal utterance that causes legal consequences.
الحنث (al-ḥinth): Breaking an oath; violation of the sworn condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone swears 'I will not marry' and then appoints an agent to marry a woman for him, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because the agent is a messenger and the rights revert to him.
B) No, because the marriage was performed by the agent, not by him personally.
C) Yes, only if he intended to marry personally.
D) No, because marriage is a non-physical act.

Key Takeaway
An oath involving contracts like marriage, divorce, or gift is broken by proxy because the rights of the contract revert to the swearer, making the agent a mere conduit.

The Effect of 'Lam' in Oath Formulations
Explaining the ruling with linguistic proof for three oath categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify oath formulations into three categories and determine whether the oath is broken by agency or by ownership.

Core Idea
The 'lam' in oaths indicates specification; its legal effect depends on whether the action can be deputized or not.

The preposition 'lam' (ل) in oath formulations like 'If I sold you...' or 'If I entered your house...' carries subtle legal meaning that determines when the oath is broken. Hanafi jurists have analyzed this based on the linguistic principle of ikhtiṣāṣ (specification) and the nature of the action — whether it admits deputization (niyābah) or not. In this lesson, we will unpack the three categories and their rulings, supported by the proof from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Look at how the 'lam' attaches: (1) to a verb that allows deputization (e.g., 'sold you'), (2) to a verb that does not allow deputization (e.g., 'entered you'), (3) to a specific object (e.g., 'your garment').
Category 1: deputizable actions; Category 2: non-deputizable actions; Category 3: direct object specification.
2
Determine the meaning of 'lam' in each category
In Category 1, 'lam' indicates ownership of the action — meaning the action must be done by the oath-taker as an agent of the other person. In Categories 2 and 3, 'lam' indicates ownership of the object — the oath is broken only if the object is owned by the other person.
The linguistic basis: 'lam' denotes ikhtiṣāṣ (specification), and the strongest form of specification is ownership.
3
Apply to given examples
For 'If I sold you a garment' (Category 1): the oath is broken only if the sale is by the command of the other person, regardless of ownership. For 'If I entered your house' (Category 2): the oath is broken only if the house is owned by the other person, regardless of command. For 'If I sold your garment' (Category 3): the oath is broken only if the garment is owned by the other person.
The text emphasizes that in Category 2 and 3, command is irrelevant; only ownership matters.
4
Analyze the proof (istidlal)
The proof is linguistic: 'lam' is for ikhtiṣāṣ. For deputizable actions, the 'lam' can indicate ownership of the action (the right to command), so it is interpreted that way. For non-deputizable actions or objects, ownership of the action is impossible, so the 'lam' must indicate ownership of the object.
This is a form of ta'līl (reasoning) based on the nature of the action and the dominant meaning of 'lam'.
5
Extend to other actions
The text lists many deputizable actions: sale, purchase, hire, craftsmanship, tailoring, construction, etc. It also lists non-deputizable actions: entering a house, striking, etc. The same principle applies.
For example, 'If I struck your child' — the oath is broken only if the child is owned by the other person (Category 2).

والمراد بدخول اللام على الفعل قربها بالمجاورة، وقيدنا بلام الاختصاص احترازاً عن لام التعريف. والحاصل أن اللام إذا دخلت على فعل يحتمل النيابة كالبيع تكون لملك الفعل، وهو أن يفعله بأمره سواء كان العين ملكه أو لا، وإذا دخلت على فعل لا يحتمل النيابة كأكل الطعام أو دخلت على العين تكون في الصورتين لملك العين سواء باعه بأمره أو لا، علم أنه ثوبه أو لا، سواء قدمت اللام أو أخرت؛ لأن اللام دخل على ما لا يملك وهو الفعل، أو على ما يملك وهو العين، فوجب صرفها إلى ما يملك وهو العين، وقد أمكن بتأخير اللام عن العين. وأما في الفعل الأول فكل واحد منها محتمل، فوجب الترجيح بالقرب، والمجاورة تكميل من الأفعال التي يحنث فيها بالأمر: الهدم والقطع والقتل وضرب الزوجات والولد الصغير وتسليم الشفعة والإذن والنفقة والوقف والأضحية والجنس والتعزير بالنسبة إلى القاضي والسلطان، والوصية والحوالة والكفالة والقضاء والشهادة والإقرار والتولية، ونظم السيد الحموي المسائل التي لا يحنث فيها بفعل الوكيل؛ لأنها الأقل مشيراً إلى أنه يحنث فيما عداها بفعل الوكيل فقال:

(عيني) لاختصاص الفعل خبر لقوله: "دخول اللام وقد مر أن المراد بالدخول هو المجاورة لا حقيقة الدخول، والمعنى أن مجاورة اللام بهذه الأفعال أي البيع والشراء وغيرهما مثل أن يقول: بعت لك ثوباً أو اشتريت لك عبداً . ونحوهما، فالمراد منها اختصاص هذه الأفعال بالمحلوف عليه بأن يكون البيع والشراء وغيرهما خاصاً بالمحلوف عليه، ولما كان هذه الأفعال مما يجري فيه النيابة فالمعنى أن الحالف يكون نائباً عن المحلوف عليه في هذه الأفعال والنيابة لا يكون إلا بالأمر والوكالة، فلو قال رجل لآخر: إن بعت لك ثوباً فعبدي حر، المراد منه: إن بعت بأمرك أو بوكالتك؛ لأن البيع خاص بالمحلوف عليه، ويكون الحالف نائباً عنه حتى لو دس المحلوف عليه ثوبه في ثياب الحالف فباعه ولم يعلم به، لم يحنث؛ لأنه لم يكن بأمر المحلوف عليه ولا بوكالته (مستخلص، محشي) كان ملكه أي سواء كان الثوب في مثال البيع والشراء والدار في مثال الإجارة. (عيني) أو لا: أي أو لم يكن ملكه حتى لو دس المحلوف عليه ثوبه في ثياب الحالف فباعه ولم يعلم الحالف، لم يحنث ؛ لأن البيع بأمره لم يوجد. (عيني)] عطف على قوله : على البيع أي ودخول اللام على الدخول والضرب ونحوهما من الأفعال التي لا تجري فيها النيابة، ولا تختص بالمحلوف عليه، وأشار بهذا إلى القسم الثاني، وأشار بقوله: "والعين" إلى القسم الثالث: أي ودخول اللام على الذات المشخصة كالثوب ،والدار وهذا مبتدأ وخبره لاختصاصه به يعني دخول اللام على الأفعال الغير المختصة بالمحلوف عليه وعلى الذات المعينة يكون لاختصاص العين بالمحلوف عليه. مثال الأول: إن دخلت لك داراً أو أكلت لك طعاماً وغيرهما، ومثال العين إن بعت ثوباً لك، فاللام في المثالين الأولين دخل على فعل لا يختص بالمحلوف عليه وفي المثال الثاني دخل على الشيء المعين، فهو لاختصاص هذه الأعيان بالمحلوف عليه، واختصاصها به إنما يكون بكونها ملكاً له سواء كانت هذه التصرفات بأمره أو لا، أما في غير العين فلأنها مما لا يجري فيه النيابة، فلا يتصور أن يكون الحالف نائباً . عنه، وأما في العين فلأن اللام لما دخل على العين أي كان أقرب إليه، اقتضى اختصاصه به بأن يكون في ملكه حتى لو دس المحلوف عليه ثوبه في ثياب الحالف ولم يعلم به حنث؛ لوجود البيع منه في ثوب مملوك للمحلوف عليه، وكذلك إذا أكل طعامه أو دخل داره، ولا فرق في -
Book of Oaths. Chapter on oaths concerning sale, purchase, and marriage... And [actions] in which deputization does not apply, like entering a house and striking a child, or [the oath] enters upon a specific object like a garment, for example. So these are three categories. In the first category, the 'lam' is for the specification of the action to the one sworn concerning (al-mahluf 'alayhi), such that if he says: 'If I sold you a garment, then my slave is free' or 'my wife is divorced,' he does not break his oath until he sells him a garment by his command; because the meaning of 'If I sold you a garment' is 'by your agency and command.' So if he sells it by his command, he breaks his oath whether the garment was his property or not, even if the one sworn concerning slipped his garment into [the oath-taker's goods] and the oath-taker sold it without his knowledge, he does not break his oath. In the last two categories, the 'lam' is for the specification of the object to the one sworn concerning, so even if he said in the second category: 'If I entered a house for you, then my slave is free,' or said in the third category: 'If I sold a garment of yours, then my slave is free,' he does not break his oath until the house or garment is the property of the one sworn concerning, whether the one sworn concerning commanded him to do so or not. And because the ruling is unified in the last two categories, the Shaykh combined them by making 'and on the object' dependent on 'and on entering' as will come. And the reason is that the 'lam' is for specification by original linguistic meaning, and its strongest aspect is ownership. So if it adjoins the action, it necessitates his ownership of the action without the object, if that action is from the first category, but if it is from the second category, it does not convey ownership of the action because it is impossible, and it conveys ownership of the object; because the meaning of his statement: 'If I entered a house for you' is 'if I entered a house owned by you,' and similarly if it adjoins the object as in the third category when he says 'I sold a garment of yours' i.e., a garment owned by you, then it necessitates ownership of the object absolutely, because all objects can be owned. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/367] And what is meant by the entry of the 'lam' upon the action is its proximity by adjacency, and we restrict it to the 'lam of specification' to avoid the 'lam of definition.' The conclusion is that if the 'lam' enters upon an action that admits deputization like sale, it is for ownership of the action, which is that he does it by his command whether the object is his property or not. If it enters upon an action that does not admit deputization like eating food or enters upon an object, then in both cases it is for ownership of the object, whether he sells it by his command or not, whether he knows it is his garment or not, whether the 'lam' is placed before or after; because the 'lam' entered upon what cannot be owned (the action) or upon what can be owned (the object), so it must be diverted to what can be owned, i.e., the object, and that is possible by delaying the 'lam' after the object. As for the first action, each of them is possible, so preference must be given by proximity, and adjacency is sufficient. Among the actions where one breaks the oath by command: demolition, cutting, killing, striking wives and young children, handing over pre-emption, permission, maintenance, endowment, sacrifice, genus, ta'zir for the judge and sultan, bequest, transfer, guarantee, adjudication, testimony, confession, and appointment. Al-Sayyid al-Hamawi arranged the cases where one does not break the oath by the agent's act because they are few, indicating that he breaks the oath in other cases by the agent's act, saying: Purchase, Sale, Division, Hire, Striking a branch, then your reconciliation with property, Litigation, and hiring a dwelling; its conclusion, and when the organizer, a flower of his unique pearls, sets it in verse. (Fath) Book of Oaths. Chapter on oaths concerning sale, purchase, and marriage... On sale, purchase, hire, craftsmanship, tailoring, and construction like 'If I sold you a garment' for the specification of the action to the one sworn concerning, meaning by his command whether it is his property or not, and on entering. i.e., by the person sworn concerning? i.e., the action sworn concerning? The one sworn concerning? On sale: i.e., on what can be owned by contract like sale and purchase, such as saying: 'If I sold you a garment, then my slave is free.' (Ayni) And purchase: like 'If I bought you a garment, then my slave is free.' (Ayni) And hire: like 'If I hired you a house, then my slave is free.' (Ayni) And craftsmanship: like 'If I made you a ring, then my slave is free.' (Ayni) And tailoring: like 'If I sewed you a garment, then my slave is free.' (Ayni) And construction: like 'If I built you a house, then my slave is free.' (Ayni) Like 'If I sold': the author mentioned one example and sufficed with it for the remaining examples because they are obvious. (Ayni) 'For the specification of the action' is the predicate of his statement 'Entry of the lam,' and it has been mentioned that what is meant by entry is adjacency, not actual entry. The meaning is that the adjacency of the lam to these actions—i.e., sale, purchase, etc., such as saying 'I sold you a garment' or 'I bought you a slave' and the like—means the specification of these actions to the one sworn concerning, in that the sale and purchase etc. are specific to the one sworn concerning. Since these actions admit deputization, the meaning is that the oath-taker is a deputy for the one sworn concerning in these actions, and deputization occurs only by command and agency. So if a man says to another: 'If I sold you a garment, then my slave is free,' what is meant is: 'If I sold by your command or agency,' because the sale is specific to the one sworn concerning, and the oath-taker is a deputy for him, so much so that if the one sworn concerning slipped his garment into the oath-taker's clothes and he sold it without knowing, he does not break his oath because it was not by the command or agency of the one sworn concerning. (Mukhtasar, Muhashshi) 'Was his property': i.e., whether the garment in the example of sale and purchase and the house in the example of hire. (Ayni) 'Or not': i.e., or was not his property, even if the one sworn concerning slipped his garment into the oath-taker's clothes and he sold it without the oath-taker knowing, he does not break his oath because the sale by his command did not occur. (Miskin) 'And on entering': [i.e., the entry of the lam upon what cannot be owned by contract, such as saying: 'If I entered a house for you, then my slave is free.' (Ayni)] This is a conjunction to his statement 'on sale,' i.e., the entry of the lam on entering and striking and similar actions that do not admit deputization and are not specific to the one sworn concerning. He indicated the second category by this, and indicated the third category by his statement 'and the object': i.e., the entry of the lam upon the specific entity like a garment and house. This is a subject and its predicate is 'for its specification to him,' meaning the entry of the lam upon actions that are not specific to the one sworn concerning and upon the specific entity is for the specification of the object to the one sworn concerning. Example of the first: 'If I entered a house for you' or 'ate food for you' and the like. Example of the object: 'If I sold a garment of yours.' So the lam in the first two examples entered upon an action that is not specific to the one sworn concerning, and in the second example it entered upon the specific thing. So it is for the specification of these objects to the one sworn concerning, and their specification to him is only by them being his property, whether these actions were by his command or not. As for other than the object, because they do not admit deputization, it is inconceivable that the oath-taker is a deputy for him. As for the object, because the lam entered upon the object i.e., it was closer to it, it required its specification to him by being in his ownership, so much so that if the one sworn concerning slipped his garment into the oath-taker's clothes and he did not know, he breaks the oath because the sale occurred by him in a garment owned by the one sworn concerning. Similarly if he ate his food or entered his house. And there is no difference in -
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal commentary on the interpretation of the preposition 'lam' (ل) in oath formulations. It distinguishes three categories of oaths: (1) where the 'lam' attaches to a verb that can be performed by a deputy (like sale, purchase, hire), (2) where it attaches to a verb that cannot be deputized (like entering a house, striking), and (3) where it attaches to a specific object (like 'your garment'). The core ruling is that in the first category, the 'lam' indicates that the action must be performed by the oath-taker as an agent of the person sworn concerning (al-mahluf 'alayhi), i.e., by the latter's command or agency. The oath is only broken if the action is done by the oath-taker at the behest of the one sworn concerning, regardless of ownership of the object. In the second and third categories, the 'lam' indicates ownership of the object by the one sworn concerning. Thus, for example, if someone swears 'If I sold you a garment' (first category), he only breaks his oath if he sells by the other's command, even if the garment belonged to the other. But if he swears 'If I entered your house' (second category) or 'If I sold your garment' (third category), he breaks the oath only if the object (house or garment) is owned by the other person, whether or not he acted by command. The commentary explains that this is because the 'lam' denotes specification (ikhtisas), and its strongest sense is ownership. When it adjoins an action that can be owned (i.e., deputizable), it indicates ownership of the action (i.e., the right to command it); when it adjoins an action that cannot be owned or an object, it indicates ownership of the object. The author provides numerous examples and lists actions where the same ruling applies, such as demolition, cutting, killing, striking spouses, etc. The text also discusses the ruling when the one sworn concerning secretly places his garment among the oath-taker's goods: if the oath-taker sells it unknowingly, he does not break an oath of the first type (because there was no command), but does break an oath of the third type (because the object was owned by the other).

Key Terms
لام الاختصاص (lām al-ikhtiṣāṣ): The preposition 'lam' used to indicate specification or particularization of an action or object to a person, often implying ownership or agency.
المحلوف عليه (al-maḥlūf ʿalayhi): The person on whose behalf or concerning whom the oath is taken; the one to whom the action or object in the oath is attributed.
النيابة (al-niyābah): Deputization or agency; the legal capacity for one person to act on behalf of another.
الملك (al-milk): Ownership or property right; a key factor in determining whether an oath is broken when the 'lam' indicates specification of an object.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in the passage, in which of the following oaths does the 'lam' indicate ownership of the object?
A) If I sold you a garment
B) If I bought you a house
C) If I entered your house
D) If I sewed you a shirt

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oath jurisprudence, the 'lam' denotes specification; its legal effect hinges on whether the action can be deputized: if yes, the oath requires agency; if no, it requires object ownership.

Oaths on Deputizable Actions: The Condition of Command
Ruling on the condition of command in oaths on deputizable actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when an oath concerning a deputizable action is broken, and apply the condition of command to new scenarios.

Core Idea
An oath on a deputizable action is broken only if the act is done by the other’s command, not merely on their property.

In Hanafi oaths, when someone swears to perform an action that can be delegated (like selling or hiring) for another person, the presence of the lam (ل) indicates that the action is specific to that person. This specificity implies agency: the oath-taker acts as a deputy. Consequently, the oath is only broken if the action is performed by the explicit command or agency of the person sworn by. This lesson unpacks this principle through the examples of sale, purchase, hire, and craftsmanship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Action and Oath Structure
Determine what the oath-taker swears to do (e.g., sell, hire, build) and for whom. The phrase 'for you' (لك) introduces the lam that makes the action specific.
The lam is critical; without it, the oath would be general.
2
Recognize the Role of Niyabah
Recognize that deputizable actions require agency. The oath-taker is considered a proxy (na'ib) for the person sworn by.
Niyabah requires explicit command or wakalah.
3
Apply the Condition of Command
The oath is broken only if the action is performed by the command of the person sworn by. If the action occurs without command, even if it benefits them, the oath remains unbroken.
This is the central ruling.
4
Distinguish from Ownership
Ownership of the object is irrelevant. The key is whether the action was commanded. For example, selling the other's property without command does not break the oath.
This distinction prevents confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(عيني) أو لا: أي أو لم يكن ملكه حتى لو دس المحلوف عليه ثوبه في ثياب الحالف فباعه ولم يعلم الحالف، لم يحنث ؛ لأن البيع بأمره لم يوجد. (مسكين)
On selling, buying, hiring, craftsmanship, tailoring, and building: like 'if I sell you a garment' – because the action is specific to the person sworn by, whether it was by his command or not, and whether it was his property or not. And on entering: namely, the entry of the lam on what is not acquired by contract...
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage addresses oaths involving actions that can be performed by proxy, such as selling, buying, hiring, etc. The key point: when the lam (ل) enters on such actions (e.g., 'if I sell FOR you'), it indicates that the action is specifically for the benefit of the person being sworn by. The implication is that the oath-taker is acting as a deputy (na'ib) for that person, which requires command or agency (amr or wakalah). Thus, if the oath-taker sells without the other's command, even if the item was owned by the other, he does not break the oath because the action was not done by the other's order. The commentary emphasizes that the lam here indicates ownership of the action, not necessarily ownership of the object.

Key Terms
النيابة (al-niyabah): Acting as a deputy or agent; in oaths, it implies the oath-taker is acting on behalf of the person sworn by.
المحلوف عليه (al-mahloof alayh): The person for whose benefit the action is sworn to be done, or the subject of the oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says, 'If I sell a garment for you, my slave is free,' and the other person secretly places his own garment among the man's clothes and the man sells it without knowledge, does he break the oath?
A) Yes, because he sold the other's property.
B) No, because he did not act by the other's command.
C) Yes, because the sale occurred.
D) No, because the garment was not his property.

Key Takeaway
For an oath on a deputizable action to be broken, the act must be by the other's command, not merely on their property or for their benefit.
