Lam on Non-Deputizable Actions and Specific Objects
Ruling on the interpretation of the lam in oaths on non-deputizable actions and specific objects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the lam in an oath on a non-deputizable action or a specific object indicates ownership of the object and apply that rule to determine whether an oath is broken.

Core Idea
The lam on non-deputizable actions signals ownership, not command – acting on the owned object breaks the oath.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies a crucial nuance in Hanafi oath law: when a person swears using the preposition 'lam' (for/upon) with actions like entering, eating, or specific objects like a garment, what exactly does it mean? While for commercial transactions the lam indicates 'by your command,' here it points to ownership of the object being acted upon. Understanding this difference prevents confusion and ensures correct application of oath rulings. We'll dissect the rule through examples and see how ownership of the object is the decisive factor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage presents two distinct cases: (1) actions that cannot be deputized (entering, beating, eating, drinking), and (2) specific tangible objects (al-ayn) like a garment or house. Recognize that these are treated differently from actions like selling where deputization is possible.
Non-deputizable actions are those where you cannot have someone else do them on your behalf in a way that makes the act yours.
2
Understand the function of the lam
In both cases, the lam does not indicate 'by the command of' the person sworn by. Instead, it indicates that the object involved must be owned by that person. For non-deputizable actions, this is because deputization is impossible; for specific objects, it is because the lam directly ties the object to the person.
The phrase 'la ikhtisasa lahu' (no specificity to him) for actions, and 'ikhtisas al-ayn' for objects.
3
Analyze the provided examples
First example: 'If I enter a house for you' – the house must belong to the addressee. Second example: 'If I sell a garment of yours' – the garment must be owned by the addressee. The conditional action (selling) is done on the owned object.
Note that for 'selling' (which is deputizable), the lam would indicate command, but here the example is about a specific object, not the deputizable action.
4
Derive the ruling and apply to edge cases
The ruling: the oath is broken whenever the action (even if performed without command) is done on an object owned by the person sworn by. The passage mentions that if the owner secretly places his garment among the oath-taker's clothes and the latter sells it unknowingly, he still breaks the oath because the object was owned by the other.
This shows that knowledge and permission are irrelevant – ownership is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى الدخول: [ أي دخول اللام على ما لا يملك بالعقد بأن قال: إن دخلت لك داراً فعبدي حر. (عيني)] عطف على قوله : على البيع أي ودخول اللام على الدخول والضرب ونحوهما من الأفعال التي لا تجري فيها النيابة، ولا تختص بالمحلوف عليه، وأشار بهذا إلى القسم الثاني، وأشار بقوله: "والعين" إلى القسم الثالث: أي ودخول اللام على الذات المشخصة كالثوب ،والدار وهذا مبتدأ وخبره لاختصاصه به يعني دخول اللام على الأفعال الغير المختصة بالمحلوف عليه وعلى الذات المعينة يكون لاختصاص العين بالمحلوف عليه. مثال الأول: إن دخلت لك داراً أو أكلت لك طعاماً وغيرهما، ومثال العين إن بعت ثوباً لك، فاللام في المثالين الأولين دخل على فعل لا يختص بالمحلوف عليه وفي المثال الثاني دخل على الشيء المعين، فهو لاختصاص هذه الأعيان بالمحلوف عليه، واختصاصها به إنما يكون بكونها ملكاً له سواء كانت هذه التصرفات بأمره أو لا، أما في غير العين فلأنها مما لا يجري فيه النيابة، فلا يتصور أن يكون الحالف نائباً . عنه، وأما في العين فلأن اللام لما دخل على العين أي كان أقرب إليه، اقتضى اختصاصه به بأن يكون في ملكه حتى لو دس المحلوف عليه ثوبه في ثياب الحالف ولم يعلم به حنث؛ لوجود البيع منه في ثوب مملوك للمحلوف عليه، وكذلك إذا أكل طعامه أو دخل داره، ولا فرق في -
And on entering: i.e., the entry of the lam on what is not acquired by contract, such as if he said: 'If I enter a house for you, my slave is free.' ... And the lam entering on the specific entity (al-ayn) as in 'if I sell a garment of yours.' So the lam in the first two examples enters on an action not specific to the person sworn by, and in the second example on the specific thing. Thus it is for the specificity of the object to the person sworn by, and its specificity is only by it being his property, whether these actions were by his command or not.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage distinguishes two more cases. First, actions that cannot be deputized—like entering, beating, eating, drinking. When the lam enters on such actions (e.g., 'if I enter a house for you'), it does NOT indicate command because these actions cannot be performed by proxy. Instead, the lam indicates that the object (the house, food, etc.) must belong to the person sworn by. Second, the lam may enter on a specific object (al-ayn) such as 'a garment of yours' in an oath about selling. In that case, the lam indicates ownership of the garment. Thus, if the person sworn by secretly places his garment among the oath-taker's clothes and the oath-taker sells it unknowingly, he does break the oath because he sold the other's property—even without command. The same applies to eating his food or entering his house. The key difference from the first section is that here the determinant is ownership of the object, not command over the action.

Key Terms
العين (al-ayn): A specific tangible object (e.g., a garment, house).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says, 'If I enter a house for you, my slave is free,' and he enters a house belonging to that person without his command, does he break the oath?
A) Yes, because the house is owned by that person.
B) No, because he did not act by command.
C) Yes, only if the person commanded him.
D) No, because entering cannot be deputized.

Key Takeaway
For oaths on non-deputizable actions and specific objects, the lam on the object indicates ownership, not command; acting on the owned object breaks the oath even without permission.

Effect of Intention and Option in Oaths of Sale
Ruling on intention and the effect of option in oaths about selling or buying

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rulings on the effect of intention when it burdens the oath-taker and the role of khiyar al-shart in determining the outcome of an oath concerning sale or purchase of a slave.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, an oath-taker's self-burdening intent is accepted; a sale with option retains ownership, so the slave is freed.

This lesson delves into two subtle aspects of oaths in Hanafi fiqh: how the oath-taker's intention is assessed and how a stipulated option (khiyar al-shart) affects the fulfillment of an oath. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly applying the rulings on oaths, particularly in transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath's condition and the claimed intention
Examine the phrase used in the oath. If the oath-taker claims he intended a meaning that makes the oath more burdensome (e.g., interpreting a general sale as a sale of a specific owned item), his claim is accepted. If his claimed intention would lighten the oath, it is rejected.
This is based on the principle that the benefit of the doubt is given to the one who imposes hardship on himself.
2
Determine the presence of khiyar al-shart
Check whether the sale or purchase was executed with a stipulated option (khiyar al-shart). This option allows one or both parties to cancel the contract within a specified period.
Khiyar al-shart is distinct from khiyar al-‘ayb (option of defect) and khiyar al-ru’yah (option of inspection).
3
Apply the Hanafi ruling on ownership transfer with option
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, khiyar al-shart for the seller prevents ownership from leaving him. For the buyer, it prevents the item from entering his ownership. Therefore, if the oath is 'if I sell' or 'if I buy' with option, the condition is fulfilled while the slave is still in the oath-taker's ownership, resulting in the slave being freed.
This is specific to khiyar al-shart; other options do not prevent ownership transfer.
4
Contrast: the ruling without option
If the sale is executed without any option, ownership transfers upon contract. The condition of the oath is met, but because the slave has left the oath-taker's ownership, the oath dissolves (tanhallu) and the slave is not freed.
The oath dissolves because the condition is satisfied, but the consequence (freeing) cannot occur as the object of the oath is no longer in possession.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Imam Abu Hanifa: with khiyar al-shart, the oath breaks and the slave is freed. This is the view presented in the text.
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree on ownership transfer, but the text adopts Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن نوى غيره صدق فيما عليه. إن بعته أو ابتعته فهو حر فعقد أي الحالف أي غير مقتضى ظاهر كلامه أي إن اشتريته (عيني) القسم الثاني أي الأفعال الغير المختصة بالمحلوف عليه بينما إذا قال: إن أكلت طعاماً لك، وبينما إذا قال: إن أكلت لك طعاماً؛ لأن ملك العين، هو المراد دون ملك الفعل، بخلاف القسم الأول كما مر. (مستخلص) والضرب: أي وعلى الضرب كإن ضربت لك عبداً فعبدي حر. (عيني) والأكل: أي وعلى الأكل كإن أكلت لك طعاماً فعبدي حر. (عيني) والشرب: أي وعلى الشرب كإن شربت لك ماء فعبدي حر. (عيني) والعين: أي دخول اللام أيضاً على الذات المعينة كالثوب مثلاً . (عيني) لاختصاصه: أي يكون دخول اللام في هذين القسمين لاختصاص العين بالمحلوف عليه (عيني) بأن كان أي المحلوف به أي الثوب والدار مثلاً أمره أي سواء كان أمره بذلك أو لم يأمره حتى لو قال: إن دخلت لك، ويحنث كيف ما كان بعد أن كانت الدار ملكه، وكذا إن بعت ثوباً لك. (عيني) وإن نوى غيره إلخ : اعلم أن في هذا الكلام ضرباً من الغموض، فيحتمل أن يكون معناه إن نوى القسم الثاني بالأول بأن قال: إن بعت لك ثوباً فعبدي حر، وقال: نويت إن بعت ثوباً لك أي مملوكاً له يصدق؛ لأنه شدد على وهو معنى قول المصنف : صدق" فيما عليه ، فإن كلمة "على" يستعمل في الفرد ويصدق فيما له نفع بأن باع ثوباً غير مملوك للمحلوف عليه لكن بأمره ثم قال: نويت القسم الثاني لا يصدق؛ لأنه يدعي عدم الحنث وفيه نفع له، ويحتمل أن يكون هذا الكلام راجعاً إلى أول هذا الباب بأن قال فيما يحنث بالمباشرة لا بالأمر: نويت أن لا آمر به الغير أيضاً فيصدق؛ لأنه تشديد على نفسه، وإلى الأول إشارة في الكفاية، وإلى الثاني إشارة في الهداية. (مستخلص) نفسه صدق فيما عليه أي في صورة يكون فيها تشديد على نفسه لا في صورة له فيها تخفيف. (عيني) إن بعته إلخ: أي لو قال رجل: إن بعت هذا العبد فهو حر، أو قال: إن اشتريت هذا العبد فهو حر، فعقد الحالف بشرط الخيار يحنث، فيعتق العبد، وقيد بالخيار؛ لأنه لو قال: إن بعته فهو حر، فباعه بيعاً صحيحاً بلا خيار لا يعتق؛ لزوال ملكه وتنحل اليمين؛ لتحقق الشرط، ويحنث بشرط الخيار ؛ لوجود الشرط وهو البيع والشراء مع قيام الملك عند الإمام؛ إذ المبيع لا يخرج عن ملك البائع باشتراط الخيار له اتفاقاً، وخيار المشتري وإن منع من دخوله في ملك المشتري عنده لكن العتق معلق بتعليقه، والمعلق كالمنجز، ولو نجز العتق بعد الشراء بالخيار انفسخ الخيار، ووقع العتق فكذا إذا علق، وقيدنا بخيار الشرط؛ لأن خيار العيب والرؤية لا يمنع زوال المبيع عن ملك البائع، وشرط الخيار للبائع مانع من زواله عن ملكه. (فتح عيني) فعقد: أي البائع في الأول والمشتري في الثاني.
And if he intends something else, he is believed regarding what is upon him (i.e., if it burdens himself). If I sell it or buy it, he is free, and he contracts (with option). ... And if he intends the second category with the first, he is believed because it is a burden upon himself. ... (And the case of) 'If I sell him' etc.: as if a man said, 'If I sell this slave, he is free,' or 'If I buy this slave, he is free,' and he contracts with a condition of option (khiyar al-shart), he breaks the oath and the slave is freed. This is restricted to khiyar al-shart because if he sells without option, the slave does not become free because ownership passes and the oath is dissolved. With option, ownership remains for the seller, so the condition is fulfilled and the oath breaks.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage covers two sub-issues. First, regarding intention (niyyah): if the oath-taker claims he intended a different meaning than the apparent one, his claim is accepted if it makes the oath more burdensome for himself (e.g., interpreting 'if I sell for you' as 'if I sell a garment owned by you' which is stricter). But if he claims an interpretation that would make the oath lighter (i.e., avoid breaking), it is not accepted. Second, the text discusses oaths about selling or buying a specific item, like 'if I sell this slave, he is free.' If the sale is executed without any option (khiyar), the slave is not freed because ownership transfers and the condition is not technically met? Actually, the text says: if sold without option, the oath is dissolved because the condition is met? Wait: 'لا يعتق لزوال ملكه وتنحل اليمين لتحقق الشرط' — it says he does not become free because ownership ends, but the oath is dissolved because the condition is fulfilled? That seems contradictory. Upon careful reading: The phrase 'لا يعتق' means he does not become free? Let's read carefully: 'فلو قال: إن بعته فهو حر، فباعه بيعاً صحيحاً بلا خيار لا يعتق؛ لزوال ملكه وتنحل اليمين؛ لتحقق الشرط' This is tricky: The oath was 'if I sell him, he is free.' If he sells without option, the condition (sale) is fulfilled, but the slave does not become free because ownership has left him? Actually, the slave is the object of the oath: the condition of sale triggers the freeing. But if the sale is complete, the slave is no longer in his possession to be freed? Instead, the oath dissolves (tanhallu al-yameen) because the condition is met. The result is that the slave is not freed, because the sale removed him from the owner's property before the slave could be freed. With option, however, the ownership remains with the seller during the option period, so the condition is fulfilled while the slave is still in his possession, so the slave becomes free. This is a nuanced point. The text then explains that this applies only to khiyar al-shart, not khiyar al-‘ayb or khiyar al-ru’yah, because those do not prevent transfer of ownership. The final sentence identifies the contracting parties.

Key Terms
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): An option stipulated in a contract that allows one or both parties to cancel within a specified period.
تنحل اليمين (tanhallu al-yameen): The oath is dissolved, meaning its obligation ends and no expiation is needed.
يحنث (yahantuthu): He breaks the oath, meaning the condition is met and the consequence (e.g., freeing) is triggered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, what happens when a man swears 'If I sell this slave, he is free' and then sells the slave with a stipulated option (khiyar al-shart) for the seller?
A) The slave is freed because the condition is met while ownership remains.
B) The slave is not freed because the sale is incomplete until the option expires.
C) The oath dissolves because the condition is met but ownership passes.
D) The sale is void because the oath conflicts with the contract.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the effect of an oath on sale/purchase depends critically on the presence of khiyar al-shart; without it, the oath dissolves, but with it, the consequence (e.g., freeing a slave) takes effect due to retained ownership.

6 min
Oath in a Sale with Option (Khiyar)
Ruling on oath broken by sale with option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a sale with a right of rescission (khiyar) triggers an oath conditioned on the sale, and explain the legal reasoning using ownership transfer.

Core Idea
A sale with khiyar transfers ownership, thus breaking an oath conditioned on the sale.

In Hanafi law, an oath conditioned on a sale can be complicated by the presence of an option (khiyar). Many assume that because the buyer can rescind, the sale is not fully effective. However, the Hanafi school holds that such a sale is valid and transfers ownership, even if subject to rescission. This lesson unpacks the ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq, clarifying when an oath is broken and why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states that an oath is broken (حنث) by a sale with an option (بالخيار). This means the condition of the oath is fulfilled upon the contract, regardless of the option.
Note the contrast: corrupt (فاسد) and suspended (موقوف) sales also break the oath, but void (باطل) sales do not.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is implicit: the sale with khiyar is a valid ('sahih') sale that transfers ownership. The text says: 'ولقيام الملك عند وجود الشرط' (ownership is established upon the existence of the condition). This is the ratio legis.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The oath's condition is 'if I sell'. The sale occurs at the moment of contract. Ownership transfers immediately, even while the option is pending. Thus the condition is met, and the vow (e.g., freeing a slave) becomes obligatory.
This distinguishes khiyar from a void sale where no ownership transfers.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The passage does not mention dispute, but some later Hanafi sources note that Abu Yusuf differed for corrupt sales. Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may treat a sale with option as incomplete, thus not triggering the oath.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi: the oath is broken.
5
Apply the ruling
Practical example: A person swears, 'If I sell this slave, he is free.' He sells the slave with a three-day option. According to Hanafi law, the slave is freed immediately upon the contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالخيار حنث، وكذا بالفاسد، والموقوف لا بالباطل. إن لم أبع فكذا فأعتق أي بشرط الخيار أي الحالف ذلك العبد حنث فيعتق العبد لوجود شرط العتق وهو البيع أو الشراء، ولقيام الملك عند وجود الشرط. (عيني)
He breaks the oath with an option (to rescind), and likewise with a corrupt (sale), and with a deferred (sale) but not with an invalid one. If I do not sell then such and such, then he frees (a slave) meaning by a condition of option. That is, the one who swears (concerning) that slave breaks the oath, so the slave is freed due to the existence of the condition of freedom which is the sale or purchase, and due to the ownership being established when the condition occurs.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that an oath concerning a sale or purchase is broken even if the transaction includes a right of rescission (khiyar al-shart). The reason is that such a sale is legally valid during the option period; it is a true sale that transfers ownership subject to rescission. Therefore, if a man swears 'If I sell this slave, he is free' and then sells him with an option, the slave is freed because the condition (the sale) has occurred and ownership has transferred (at least temporarily). The commentator notes that this is based on the principle that a sale with khiyar is effective in terms of ownership and thus triggers the oath.

Key Terms
الخيار (al-khiyar): The right to rescind a contract within a specified period; a conditional option that does not invalidate the contract but gives a party the choice to uphold or cancel it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of sale does NOT break an oath?
A) Sale with option (khiyar)
B) Corrupt sale (fasid)
C) Void sale (batil)
D) Suspended sale (mawquf)

Key Takeaway
A sale with a right of rescission (khiyar) is considered a valid sale that transfers ownership, thus breaking an oath conditioned on the sale.

Oath in a Corrupt Sale (Fasid) and Abu Yusuf's Dispute
Expansion on corrupt sale breaking oath with scenarios for buyer/seller

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a corrupt sale (fasid) breaks an oath and explain the difference between the majority position and Abu Yusuf's view, applying the correct ruling to scenarios involving seller and buyer.

Core Idea
A corrupt sale breaks an oath according to the majority of Hanafi imams; Abu Yusuf disagrees unless the sale includes an option.

This lesson examines a critical intra-Hanafi dispute: whether a corrupt sale (bay' fasid) triggers an oath violation (hanth). The three leading imams (Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, Zufar) hold that it does, while Abu Yusuf restricts it to sales with an option. The passage also details scenarios for seller and buyer, linking ownership transfer to the consequence of freeing a slave. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to apply the school's ruling in oath-related cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Disputed Ruling
Recognize that the core question is whether a corrupt sale (fasid) causes the oath to be broken (hanth).
The sale is fasid, not batil – this distinction matters for ownership transfer.
2
State the Majority Position (Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, Zufar)
These three imams hold that a corrupt sale does break the oath, because ownership transfers, even if the contract has a defect.
This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).
3
State Abu Yusuf's Position and His Exception
Abu Yusuf holds that a corrupt sale does not break the oath, unless the sale includes an option (khiyar) from the start.
His reasoning may be that a fasid sale is not a complete transfer without an option.
4
Apply to Seller Scenarios
If the swearer is the seller: the slave is freed only if the slave is still in the seller's possession; if delivered, ownership has transferred and the oath is broken but the slave is not freed.
The key variable is possession with liability (damān).
5
Apply to Buyer Scenarios
If the swearer is the buyer: the slave is freed only if the buyer has possession with liability; otherwise not.
Ownership is established by possession with liability in a corrupt sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا بالفاسد: أي لو حلف لا أو لا يشتري وباع بيعاً فاسداً أو اشترى شراء فاسداً يحنث، فإن كان الحالف هو البائع، فصورة من باع بيعاً فاسداً وأعتق العبد الذي حلف بشرط بيعه ينظر: فإن كان العبد في يد المشتري بحيث يكون ضمانه على المشتري بمثل رهن أو أمانة أو غصب لا يعتق؛ لأنه كما يتم البيع يزول عن ملكه كالبيع الصحيح البات، وينبغي أن ينحل اليمين، وإن كان العبد في يد البائع عتق؛ لأنه لا يزول ملكه قبل التسليم إلى المشتري، وإن كان الحالف بعتقه هو المشتري فاشتراه فاسداً، فإن كان في يده مضمونا عليه يعتق لدخوله في ملكه كما تم البيع، وإلا فلا يعتق؛ لعدم ملكه، وعن أبي يوسف: أنه لا يحنث بالفاسد، وإلا بما فيه خيار لأحدهما أصلاً، وبه قالت الثلاثة.
And likewise with the corrupt: meaning if he swore 'I do not sell' or 'I do not buy' and he sold a corrupt sale or bought a corrupt purchase, he breaks the oath. If the one who swore is the seller, the scenario of one who sold a corrupt sale and freed the slave that he swore upon condition of its sale: it is considered: if the slave is in the hand of the buyer such that its liability is upon the buyer by way of pledge, trust, or usurpation, he is not freed; because when the sale is completed, it leaves his ownership like a definitive valid sale, and the oath should be dissolved. And if the slave is in the hand of the seller, he is freed because his ownership does not leave before delivery to the buyer. If the one who swore its freedom is the buyer and he bought it corruptly, then if it is in his hand with liability upon him, it is freed due to his ownership as the sale is completed; otherwise it is not freed due to lack of ownership. And from Abu Yusuf: that he does not break the oath with corrupt (sale), except with one that includes an option for one of them originally, and the three (imams) said that.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage expands the ruling on corrupt sales. A corrupt sale (fasid) is one that is valid in essence but has a defect in its conditions, e.g., selling for an uncertain price or with a prohibited element. The Hanafi position is that such a sale breaks an oath because it is a true sale that transfers ownership, albeit with a flaw. The text details two scenarios: if the person swearing is the seller, the slave is freed only if the slave remains in the seller's possession (i.e., not yet delivered); if delivered, the slave is not freed because ownership has transferred. If the person swearing is the buyer, the slave is freed if the buyer has possession with liability (damān). Abu Yusuf disagrees: he holds that a corrupt sale does not break the oath, except when it includes an option (khiyar) from the start. This ikhtilaf is noted, and the phrase 'wa bihi qālath al-thalāthah' indicates that the other three (Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and possibly Zufar) agree on the opposite position.

Key Terms
البيع الفاسد (al-bay' al-fāsid): A sale that is valid in its essence (asl) but defective in its conditions (awsāf), such as selling something unknown or for an uncertain price, but which can become valid if the defect is removed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi imams, does a corrupt sale (fasid) break an oath concerning that sale?
A) No, never.
B) Yes, always.
C) Yes, but only if ownership transfers with possession.
D) No, only a valid sale breaks the oath.

Key Takeaway
A corrupt sale breaks an oath per the majority of Hanafi imams; Abu Yusuf restricts this to sales with an option.

Oaths in Deferred and Invalid Sales
Ruling on deferred sale breaking oath but invalid sale not, with distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a deferred sale (mawquf) and an invalid sale (batil) in terms of their effect on oaths, and apply the Hanafi ruling on hanth (breaking the oath).

Core Idea
A deferred sale breaks an oath; an invalid sale does not — because only a legally recognized sale triggers the oath.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies a nuanced aspect of oaths (ayman) in sales. When someone swears a conditional oath related to a sale, the type of sale determines whether the oath is broken. A deferred (mawquf) sale, though pending ratification, is considered a valid contract in principle and therefore breaks the oath. However, an invalid (batil) sale, lacking essential elements, is not considered a sale at all legally, so the oath remains intact. The text also provides the Hanafi criteria for distinguishing batil from fasid, and notes a difference of opinion from Abu Yusuf regarding free women and umm walad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a conditional oath involving sale or purchase. The ruling is whether the oath is broken (hanth) or not. Two types of sales are examined: deferred (mawquf) and invalid (batil).
Focus on the 'aqd itself, not the physical transfer.
2
Define the two sale types
A deferred sale is one executed without the owner's permission, pending ratification. An invalid sale lacks a fundamental element, such as the subject matter not being property in any religion (e.g., carrion, free person).
Batil is distinct from fasid (corrupt) in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Apply the ruling to the deferred sale
If the seller swears 'If I sell X, my slave is free' and sells X via a fuduli (unauthorized agent), the sale is mawquf. The oath is broken because the contract has been made, even if not yet ratified.
Similarly, if the buyer swears and buys from a fuduli, the oath is broken.
4
Apply the ruling to the invalid sale
If the seller swears and then sells carrion or a free person, the contract is batil. The oath is not broken because batil is not considered a sale in reality or in effect.
The text emphasizes that batil does not produce any legal effects of sale.
5
Analyze the distinction between batil and fasid
Batil occurs when one of the exchanged items is not property in any revealed religion. Fasid occurs when it is property in some religions (e.g., wine). For oaths, only batil fails to break the oath; fasid generally does break it (though not covered in detail here).
This distinction is critical for correct fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والموقوف : أي وكذا يحنث بالبيع والشراء الموقوفين في المسألة السابقة، فإن كان البائع البيع الموقوف أن يقول: إن بعت عبد فلان فعبدي "حر" فباع عبد فلان بغير إجازته عتق عبد الحالف لوجود الشرط، وإن كان الحالف هو المشتري فصورته أن يقول: "إن اشتريت عبداً فهو . حر" فاشترى عبداً فضولي حنث بالشراء. (عيني، فتح) لا بالباطل أي لو قال له: "إن بعته أو ابتعته فهو حر، فعقد البيع الباطل أو الشراء الباطل بأن باعه بالميتة أو اشتراه بها لا يحنث؛ لأنه ليس ببيع وشراء حقيقة ولا حكماً . لا يفيد شيئاً من حتى أحكام البيع والشراء وإن اتصل به القبض والفرق بين الباطل والفاسد أن أحد العوضين إذا لم يكن مالاً في دين سماوي فالبيع باطل كبيع الميتة والدم والحر، وكذا البيع بها. وإن كان في بعض الأديان مالاً وأمكن اعتباره مثمناً فالبيع فاسد كبيع العبد بالخمر، وإن تعين كونه مبيعاً فباطل أيضاً كبيع الخمر بالدراهم، أو الدراهم بالخمر، ولو اشترى مدبراً أو أم ولد لا يحنث ولو قضى القاضي بجوازه يحنث للحال؛ لأن قضاءه يؤثر في إزالة المانع من الجواز، فيقتصر على وقت القضاء فيحنث حينئذ، والمكاتب كالمدبر في رواية، ولو حلف أن هذا الحر فباعه بر؛ لأن البيع الصحيح لا يتصور فيه فانعقد على الباطل، وكذا لو عقد يمينه على الحرة أو أم الولد، وعن أبي يوسف في الحرة وأم الولد ينعقد على الصحيح. (عيني، فتح)
And the deferred: meaning he likewise breaks the oath with the deferred sale and purchase in the previous issue. If the seller is the one who made the deferred sale by saying: 'If I sell the slave of so-and-so then my slave is free' and he sold the slave of so-and-so without his permission, the slave of the swearer is freed due to the existence of the condition. And if the swearer is the buyer, its scenario: he says: 'If I buy a slave, he is free' and he bought a slave from a fudūlī (unauthorized agent), he breaks the oath by the purchase. Not with the invalid: meaning if he said to him: 'If I sell it or buy it, he is free' and he made a contract of invalid sale or invalid purchase by selling it for carrion or buying it with carrion, he does not break the oath; because it is not a sale or purchase in reality or in effect. It does not bring about any of the rulings of sale and purchase even if possession is taken. And the difference between the invalid and the corrupt: that if one of the two compensations is not property in a revealed religion, the sale is invalid, like selling carrion, blood, or a free person, and likewise buying with them. And if in some religions it is property and it is possible to consider it as a price (thaman), then the sale is corrupt, like selling a slave for wine. And if it is specifically determined to be an object of sale (mabī'), it is also invalid, like selling wine for dirhams, or dirhams for wine. And if he buys a mudabbar or umm walad, he does not break the oath, and if the judge rules its permissibility, he breaks the oath at that time; because the judge's ruling is effective in removing the impediment to permissibility, so it is limited to the time of the ruling, so he breaks the oath then. And the mukātab is like the mudabbar in one narration. And if he swore that this free person, then he sold him, he is free; because a valid sale is not conceivable for him, so it is concluded as invalid. And likewise if he made his oath upon a free woman or umm walad, and from Abu Yusuf concerning the free woman and umm walad, it is concluded as valid.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage covers two types: deferred (mawquf) and invalid (batil) sales. A deferred sale is one that is conditional on the consent of a third party, e.g., a sale by a fudūlī (unauthorized agent). Such a sale is not immediately effective but becomes valid upon ratification. The Hanafi ruling is that a deferred sale breaks the oath because it is a sale in principle; the condition (the sale) exists, and the oath is about the 'aqd itself. The text gives examples: if the seller swears and sells a slave owned by another without permission, the slave is freed because the contract occurred. If the buyer swears and buys from a fudūlī, the oath is broken. However, an invalid (batil) sale does not break the oath because it is not considered a sale in the legal sense—it lacks an essential element, such as the object of sale being non-property (e.g., carrion, blood, free person). The text provides a detailed distinction: batil is when one of the exchanged items is not considered property in any revealed religion (e.g., carrion, blood, free person). If it is considered property in some religions (like wine), the sale is fasid (corrupt). Additionally, buying a mudabbar or umm walad does not break the oath because the sale is not valid in origin, but if a judge rules it valid, the oath is broken from that point. Abu Yusuf has a difference regarding free woman and umm walad: he holds the oath is valid (i.e., the sale is valid for breaking the oath), whereas the majority say it is invalid.

Key Terms
البيع الموقوف (al-bay' al-mawqūf): A sale that is contingent upon the ratification of the owner or principal; it is not immediately effective but is considered a valid contract once ratified.
البيع الباطل (al-bay' al-bāṭil): A sale that is void ab initio because it lacks a fundamental pillar, such as the subject matter being non-property in any religion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between a batil (invalid) sale and a fasid (corrupt) sale?
A) Batil involves items that are not property in any religion; fasid involves items that are property in some religions.
B) Batil is always about price, fasid is about the object.
C) Batil can become valid later; fasid cannot.
D) Batil breaks the oath; fasid does not.

Key Takeaway
A deferred sale (mawquf) breaks an oath because it is a valid contract in principle, while an invalid sale (batil) does not because it lacks legal recognition.

Oath Conditioned on Not Selling and Manumission/Tadbir
Ruling on oath conditioned on not selling after freeing or declaring tadbir

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that removing the possibility of sale (through manumission or tadbir) immediately fulfills the condition of an oath conditioned on not selling, and identify the rationale that speculative future possibilities are ignored.

Core Idea
If a man swears 'If I do not sell this slave, then...' and then frees him or declares tadbir, the oath is broken immediately—present impossibility suffices; speculative future sales do not matter.

This lesson examines a specific case in oath law: a man swears not to sell his slave, then performs an act that makes the slave unsaleable. The Hanafi school holds that the condition of 'not selling' is instantly met because the ability to sell is lost. We will explore the reasoning behind this ruling, including the rejection of hypothetical future events, and see how this principle applies to similar situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath condition and consequence
The man swears: 'If I do not sell this slave, my wife is divorced.' The condition is the absence of sale; the consequence is divorce.
2
Define the triggering acts: manumission and tadbir
Manumission is immediate freeing; tadbir is a declaration of freedom upon the master's death. Both make the slave unsellable in practice.
Tadbir is considered as preventing sale for oath purposes, even though a mudabbar can theoretically be sold with a judge's permission – but that possibility is ignored.
3
Apply the reasoning: loss of mahalliyyah (potential for sale)
Because the slave is no longer free to be sold (either freed or made mudabbar), the condition 'not selling' has been realized – the inability to sell is factual.
The text uses the term 'fawat al-mahalliyyah' – loss of the potential for sale.
4
Address the counterargument about judicial permission
One might object that a mudabbar can still be sold if a judge permits. The Hanafi response: such a ruling would rescind the tadbir, and the law does not build on hypothetical future judicial decisions.
This shows the principle: rulings are based on current facts, not speculative possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن لم أبع فكذا: أي من قال: إن لم أبع هذا العبد فامرأته طالق فأعتق العبد أو دبره طلقت امرأته وحنث في يمينه؛ لأن الشرط هو عدم البيع وقد تحقق؛ لفوات المحلية، فتحقق العجز عن البيع، فإن قيل: المحلية في التدبير باقية فإنه يمكن بيع المدبر إذا قضى القاضي بجوازه، قلنا: عند القضاء بجوازه ينفسخ التدبير وفوات المحلية إنما كان باعتبار بقاء التدبير والحكم لا يبتني على ما يظهر عند قضاء القاضي في المجتهدات، ولا يقال: لم يقع اليأس من بيع أم الولد بجواز أن ترتد وتلحق بدار الحرب ثم تسبى وتسترق؛ لأنا نقول: الحالف عقد يمينه على البيع باعتبار هذا الملك، وذلك لا يمكن بعد هذه التصرفات؛ ولأن هذه الحالات موهومات والأحكام لا تبتني على الموهومات، فتحقق اليأس عن البيع، والمراد من قوله: "أو "دبر" هو التدبير المطلق؛ لأنه يمتنع به البيع. (مستخلص، فتح)
If I do not sell then such and such: i.e., one who says: If I do not sell this slave, then my wife is divorced, then he frees the slave or makes him mudabbar, his wife is divorced and he breaks his oath; because the condition is the absence of sale and it has been realized due to the loss of potential (al-mahalliyyah), so the inability to sell is verified. For if it is said: the potential in tadbir remains, because a mudabbar can be sold if the judge rules its permissibility, we say: when the judge rules its permissibility, the tadbir is rescinded, and the loss of potential was only due to the persistence of tadbir, and the ruling is not based on what appears at the judge's ruling in matters of ijtihad. And it cannot be said: despair of selling an umm walad has not occurred because it is possible that she apostatizes, joins the land of war, then is captured and enslaved; because we say: the swearer made his oath upon selling based on this ownership, and that is not possible after these dispositions; and because these states are imaginary (mawhūmāt), and rulings are not based on imaginary things. So the despair of selling is realized, and what is meant by his saying 'or tadbir' is absolute tadbir, because it prevents sale.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage addresses a case where a man swears: 'If I do not sell this slave, my wife is divorced.' If he later frees the slave or makes him a mudabbar (i.e., declares that the slave will be free upon his death), the condition of 'not selling' is considered to have been fulfilled because these acts render the slave unsellable. The text discusses potential objections: one might argue that a mudabbar can still be sold if a judge rules it permissible, but the response is that such a judicial ruling would itself invalidate the tadbir, and the ruling is based on the current impossibility, not on speculative future events. Similarly, for an umm walad (a slave who has borne her master's child), it is theoretically possible that she could become sellable if she apostatizes and is captured, but such possibilities are too speculative to consider. Thus, the oath is broken immediately upon the manumission or tadbir, and the wife is divorced. The term 'tadbir' here means absolute tadbir, which definitively prevents sale.

Key Terms
التدبير (al-tadbīr): A declaration by the master that a slave will be free upon the master's death; it suspends sale ability.
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A female slave who bears a child to her master; she becomes free upon his death and cannot be sold during his lifetime according to Abu Hanifa.
حنث (hanth): Breaking an oath by failing to fulfill its condition or by doing the opposite of what was sworn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The wife is not divorced because the slave can still be sold with a judge's permission.
B) The wife is divorced immediately because the slave is no longer freely marketable.
C) The wife is divorced only if the slave is actually freed.
D) The oath remains pending until the master's death.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oath law, an act that makes sale impossible immediately fulfills a condition of 'not selling', and speculative future events are disregarded.

Testimony of Negation: When Does It Free a Slave?
Ruling on witnesses testifying to slaughter while master claims Hajj

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Muhammad al-Shaybani and the Two Shaykhs on accepting testimony of negation coupled with a positive, and apply the condition that the positive must have a judicial claimant.

Core Idea
Testimony of negation is not accepted unless the implied positive fact has a judicial claimant.

A man swears to free his slave if he does not perform Hajj that year. Later he claims he performed Hajj, but two witnesses testify they saw him slaughter an animal in Kufa—implying he did not go to Hajj. The Hanafi school splits: do the witnesses' words free the slave? This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind the dispute, focusing on the nature of testimony and the role of judicial claimants (mutalib).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Oath and the Dispute
The master says 'My slave is free if I do not perform Hajj this year,' then claims he performed Hajj. Witnesses testify they saw him slaughter an animal in Kufa, implying no Hajj. Does the slave become free?
The condition is 'not performing Hajj'; witnesses indirectly negate it.
2
State the Two Hanafi Positions
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the Two Shaykhs) say testimony is on negation and thus not accepted; Muhammad al-Shaybani says it is accepted because the witnesses affirm a known positive (slaughter) which necessarily implies non-Hajj.
Muhammad's view: the positive fact (slaughter) is the subject of testimony; negation follows as a necessary consequence.
3
Trace the Legal Principle Behind Each
Both sides agree testimony is for affirmation, not negation. But Muhammad argues that when negation is a necessary implication of a positive, the testimony is valid. The Two Shaykhs insist the positive must itself be judicially relevant (i.e., have a claimant), which slaughter lacks here.
Key concept: 'judicial relevance' (mā lā yadkhulu taḥta al-qaḍāʾ li-ʿadam al-muṭālib).
4
Analyze the Objection and Response
The text raises an objection: in inheritance, witnesses who say 'This is the heir and we know no other heir' are accepted even though they include a negation. The response: in inheritance, the positive (being heir) is judicially relevant; here, slaughter has no claimant, so the negation remains the purpose.
The objection strengthens Muhammad's reasoning; the response clarifies the Two Shaykhs' key distinction.
5
Conclude the Mufta Bihi
The passage indicates the Two Shaykhs' view is the relied-upon position (though not explicitly stated as mufta bihi). In practice, testimony of negation is not accepted unless the positive implied has a judicial claimant.
This reflects the Hanafi trend of preferring the Two Shaykhs in issues of testimony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبده حر إلخ أي من قال عبده حر إن لم أحج العام ثم قال: حججت، وشهد شاهدان أنه بالكوفة لم يعتق العبد عند الشيخين وقال محمد يعتق؛ لأن هذه شهادة قامت على أمر معلوم، وهو التضحية، ومن ضرورته انتفاء الحج، فتحقق الشرط فيعتق ولهما: أن هذه الشهادة قامت على النفي، والشهادة شرعت للإثبات دون النفي فلا تقبل؛ لأن المقصود من هذه الشهادة نفي الحج لا إثبات التضحية؛ لأنه لا مطالب لها، فصار كما إذا شهدا أنه لم يحج. فإن قيل: الشهادة على النفي إنما لا تقبل إذا لم تكن مقرونة بالإثبات أما إذا قرنت بالإثبات تقبل كشهود الإرث إذا قالوا: إن هذا وارث فلان ولا نعلم له وارثاً غيره تقبل حتى يدفع المال إلى المشهود له، والمشهود به ههنا أمر ثبوتي وهو التضحية والنفي ثبت ضمناً، فينبغي أن تقبل بالطريق الأولى، قلنا: التضحية وإن كان أمراً ثبوتياً لكنها مما لا تدخل تحت القضاء لعدم المطالب، فلم تكن معتبرة فبقي النفي مقصوداً بخلاف الإرث. لم يعتق : أي لم تقبل هذه الشهادة و لم يعتق العبد عندهما.
His slave is free etc.: meaning one who said 'My slave is free if I do not perform Hajj this year' then said 'I performed Hajj', and two witnesses testified that he was in Kufa, the slave is not freed according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), and Muhammad said he is freed, because this testimony is based on a known matter, which is the slaughter (of the sacrificial animal), and its necessary consequence is the non-occurrence of Hajj, so the condition is realized and he is freed. Their (the two shaykhs') argument: this testimony is based on negation, and testimony is legislated for affirmation, not negation, so it is not accepted, because the purpose of this testimony is to negate Hajj, not to affirm slaughter, as there is no claimant for it. So it is like if they testified that he did not perform Hajj. If it is said: testimony of negation is only not accepted if not coupled with affirmation; but if coupled with affirmation it is accepted, like inheritance witnesses who say 'This person is the heir of so-and-so and we do not know of any other heir' – it is accepted so that the wealth is given to the beneficiary. And the subject of testimony here is a positive matter, which is slaughter, and negation is established implicitly, so it should be accepted all the more. We say: slaughter, although positive, is not subject to judicial proceedings due to absence of a claimant, so it is not considered, and negation remains the purpose, unlike inheritance. 'He is not freed': meaning this testimony is not accepted and the slave is not freed according to the two shaykhs.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage discusses an oath where a man says, 'My slave is free if I do not perform Hajj this year.' Later he claims he performed Hajj, but two witnesses testify that he was in Kufa (i.e., did not go to Hajj). The issue is whether the slave is freed. According to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), the slave is not freed because the testimony is effectively about a negative (not performing Hajj), and testimony is meant for positive facts. Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees, arguing that the witnesses are testifying to a positive fact (that he slaughtered an animal for the sacrifice), which necessarily implies he did not perform Hajj, so the condition is met. The passage then considers an objection: testimony on negation is accepted when coupled with affirmation, as in inheritance cases where witnesses say 'This is the heir and we know no other heir' – the negation of other heirs is accepted because it is coupled with a positive statement. Here too, the positive fact of slaughter is coupled with the negation of Hajj. The response is that slaughter in this context is not a matter that can be adjudicated (no claimant), so it is not considered, leaving the negation as the main purpose. Thus, the testimony is not accepted, and the slave not freed according to the two shaykhs. This is from the Mustakhlas commentary.

Key Terms
الشهادة على النفي (al-shahadah 'ala al-nafy): Testimony about a negative fact, such as that someone did not perform Hajj.
المطالب (al-mutalib): Claimant or person who can demand a right in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), why is the testimony about the man being in Kufa not accepted to free the slave?
A) Because the witnesses are not reliable.
B) Because testimony on negation is not accepted, and the implied positive fact (slaughter) has no claimant.
C) Because the man admitted to performing Hajj.
D) Because the condition was not properly stated.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, testimony of negation is only accepted if the positive fact implied has a judicial claimant (mutalib); otherwise, the testimony remains about negation and is rejected.

Oath about wife wearing her spun cloth: sacrificial animal
Intra-madhhab dispute on whether wife's spinning triggers a vowed sacrifice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa differs from his two companions on whether wearing cloth from the wife's spinning obligates a hadiy, and identify the role of custom (urf) in establishing ownership.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, custom can transform an act into a cause of ownership, as when a wife spins her husband's cotton — the spinning itself makes the yarn his property.

This passage addresses a classic Hanafi dispute over a vow involving a wife's spinning. When a man swears that if he wears cloth from his wife's spinning, he must sacrifice a hadiy in Mecca, does a later purchase of cotton and subsequent spinning and wearing trigger the vow? Abu Hanifa says yes, relying on custom; his two students say no. Understanding this dispute reveals how custom (urf) functions as a source of law in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
State the oath and the scenario
Identify the exact wording of the vow: 'If you wear from your spun cloth, then it is a hadiy.' The husband buys cotton after the oath, wife spins and weaves it, husband wears the garment.
The cotton is purchased after the oath — this is the contested point.
2
Identify the Hanafi position and the dissenting position
Abu Hanifa holds the hadiy is obligatory; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold it is not. Pinpoint the exact condition in dispute: ownership of the cotton at the time of the oath.
3
Examine the dalil of Abu Hanifa
Custom (urf) that wives spin from husband's cotton; analogy: even without permission, the woven cloth belongs to husband. This establishes the wife's spinning as a cause of ownership.
The key phrase: 'Do you not see that if he buys cotton and she spins and weaves without permission, it becomes his property?'
4
Examine the Sahibayn's counter-argument
Vows require ownership at the time of oath; wearing does not create ownership (could be borrowed); wife's spinning does not transfer ownership (she might use her own cotton).
Their principle: 'The vow is only valid regarding owned property or property owned on the day of vow.'
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the consensus case
Under Abu Hanifa, husband must sacrifice in Mecca; under Sahibayn, he does not. If the cotton was owned at the time of the oath, all agree the hadiy is owed.
Consensus (ijma) shows the core principle is agreed — the dispute is only about the role of custom when ownership is not explicit at the time of oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن لبست من غزلك إلخ [ أي لو قال رجل لامرأته: إن لبست إلخ. (عيني)] المراد من الهدي التصدق به بمكة؛ لأن الهدي اسم لما يهدى إلى مكة، أي من حلف لامرأته إن لبست من مغزولك فهو هدي، فاشترى قطنا فغزلته المرأة ونسج ثوباً فلبسه فهو هدي عند أبي حنيفة, وقالا : ليس عليه أن يهدي حتى تغزل من قطن كان في حلف؛ لأن النذر إنما يصح في الملك أو مضافاً يوم الملك، ولم يوجد؛ لأن اللبس ليس من أسباب الملك؛ إذ قد يلبس عارية، وكذلك غزل المرأة ليس من أسباب ملك الزوج؛ لأنها قد تغزل من قطنها، ولأبي حنيفة: أن غزل المرأة عادة يكون من قطن الزوج، والمعتاد المراد، أن الزوج لو اشترى ألا ترى فغزلته ونسجته بغير إذنه يكون المنسوج للزوج، فثبت أن غزل المرأة سبب لثبوت ملك الزوج في المغزول، وقوله: "ونسج" يروى مجهولاً، وفي "الهداية": "فنسجه" بصيغة المعلوم، وهذا إيماء إلى أنه لا فرق بينهما إذا نسجه الزوج أو نسجت المرأة، وأفاد بقوله: "فملك" أنه لو كان القطن مملوكاً له وقت الحلف فغزلته ولبس الزوج الثوب، فإنه هدي بالإجماع.
If you wear from your spun cloth etc. [meaning if a man said to his wife: If you wear etc. (Ayni)] What is meant by hadiy is to give it in charity in Mecca, because hadiy is what is sent to Mecca. That is, if a man swore to his wife: If you wear from your spun cloth, then it is a hadiy, and he bought cotton, and the woman spun it and wove a garment and he wore it, it is a hadiy according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He does not have to offer a hadiy unless she spun from cotton that was in his possession at the time of the oath, because a vow is only valid regarding owned property or property owned on the day of vow, and that is not found, because wearing is not a cause of ownership; he might wear it as a loan, similarly the wife's spinning is not a cause of ownership for the husband, as she might spin from her own cotton. Abu Hanifa's argument: that a wife's spinning is customarily from the husband's cotton, and custom is what is intended. Do you not see that if a husband buys cotton and she spins and weaves it without his permission, the woven garment belongs to the husband? This establishes that the wife's spinning is a cause for the husband's ownership of the spun material. The word 'and wove' is narrated in the passive, and in Al-Hidayah it is in the active form 'he wove', indicating that there is no difference whether the husband wove or the wife wove. And he benefited by saying 'he owned' that if the cotton was owned by him at the time of the oath, and she spun and the husband wore the garment, then it is a hadiy by consensus.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage addresses an oath where a man says to his wife: 'If you wear from your spun cloth, then it is a hadiy (sacrificial animal to be given in Mecca).' The husband then buys cotton, the wife spins and weaves it into a garment, and he wears it. According to Abu Hanifa, he must offer a hadiy because, by custom, a wife spins from her husband's cotton, and her spinning causes the yarn to become his property. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: they require the cotton to have been owned by the husband at the time of the oath, and they argue that wearing does not establish ownership, nor does the wife's spinning transfer ownership. Abu Hanifa rebuts by pointing out that customarily, a wife spins from her husband's cotton, and if she spins without permission, the product still becomes the husband's property. The commentary notes that in Al-Hidayah, the verb is active ('he wove'), implying it does not matter whether the husband or wife does the weaving. If the cotton was already owned by the husband at the time of the oath, then offering the hadiy is agreed upon (by consensus). This shows the role of custom (urf) in determining ownership and the validity of vows.

Key Terms
هدي (hadiy): A sacrificial animal sent to Mecca to be slaughtered and given in charity.
غزل (ghazl): Spinning of cotton into thread by the wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does the husband owe a hadiy when he wears cloth from his wife's spinning of cotton he bought after the oath?
A) Because the wife owns the cloth.
B) Because customarily the wife spins from the husband's cotton, and her spinning makes the yarn his property.
C) Because he swore an oath and must fulfill it regardless.
D) Because wearing the cloth constitutes acceptance.

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) can serve as a legal cause for ownership in Hanafi fiqh, affecting the fulfillment of vowed sacrifices.

Oath on Jewelry: Gold Ring and Pearl Necklace
Intra-Hanafi dispute over oath interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on whether a plain pearl necklace breaks an oath not to wear jewelry, and state the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Core Idea
Oaths are interpreted by custom, but the Sahibayn override custom with Quranic text; the fatwa follows their view.

When someone swears not to wear jewelry (حلي), what constitutes 'jewelry' can be disputed. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq examines two cases: a gold ring (unanimously jewelry) and a plain pearl necklace (disputed). Understanding the reasoning of Abu Hanifa versus the Two Companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) reveals how custom and scripture interact in Hanafi oath interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the oath
The passage considers two acts: wearing a gold ring (clearly jewelry) and wearing a plain pearl necklace (disputed). The oath is 'I will not wear jewelry'.
2
Recognize the disagreement
For the pearl necklace, there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa says wearing it does not break the oath unless the pearls are set; the Sahibayn say it does break the oath even if plain.
The text says the mention here absolutely is the view of the two (Sahibayn).
3
Examine each side’s proof
Sahibayn quote Quran 16:14, which calls pearls 'hilyah' (jewelry). Abu Hanifa relies on custom ('urf): unset pearls are not considered jewelry by people; the Quranic usage is metaphorical.
Abu Hanifa’s method: oaths follow customary meanings.
4
Note the tarjih
The text concludes that the fatwa is on the view of the Sahibayn, meaning the operative ruling is that wearing a plain pearl necklace breaks the oath.
The phrase indicates that this is the relied-upon position for issuing fatwas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولبس خاتم ذهب مبتدأ وخبره: "لبس حلي" أي من حلف لا يلبس حلياً، فلبس خاتم ذهب بفتح التاء وكسرها حنث؛ لأنها حلي؛ ولهذا لا يحل استعماله للرجال، وقوله: "أو عقد لؤلؤ" فالمذكور هنا على الإطلاق وهو قولهما، فيحنث إذا قال: لا يلبس حلياً فلبس؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَتَسْتَخْرِجُوا مِنْهُ حِلْيَةً تَلْبَسُونَهَا ﴾ (النحل:١٤)، وإنما يستخرج من البحر اللؤلؤ، وعند أبي حنيفة: لا يحنث بلبس مطلقاً؛ لأنه لا يتحلى به عرفاً إلا مرصعاً . الأيمان على العرف وسمي حلية في الآية مجازاً، وقيل: هذا اختلاف عصر وزمان، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا لبس عقداً من زبرجد أو زمرد غير مرصع والفتوى على قولهما.
And wearing a gold ring—subject and predicate: 'wearing jewelry'—meaning one who swore 'I will not wear jewelry' and then wore a gold ring (with fatha or kasra on the ta') breaks the oath, because it is jewelry; for this reason its use is not permissible for men. And his statement 'or a pearl necklace': what is mentioned here absolutely is the view of the two (Sahibayn), so he breaks the oath if he says 'I will not wear jewelry' and then wears it, due to the verse: 'And you extract from it jewelry which you wear' (Quran 16:14), and pearls are extracted from the sea. According to Abu Hanifa: he does not break the oath by wearing it absolutely, because it is not considered jewelry by custom unless it is set with precious stones. Oaths are based on custom, and it is called jewelry in the verse metaphorically. It was said: this is a difference of time and era. On this difference, if he wears a necklace of chrysolite or emerald without setting, the fatwa is on the view of the two (Sahibayn).
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage deals with oaths not to wear jewelry (حلي). It first states that wearing a gold ring breaks such an oath, as it is undeniably jewelry. Then it discusses a pearl necklace: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, wearing a plain pearl necklace also breaks the oath, because pearls are explicitly called 'hilyah' in the Quran (16:14). Abu Hanifa, however, holds that wearing a plain pearl necklace does not break the oath, because by custom, pearls are not considered jewelry unless set (e.g., with precious stones). Oaths are interpreted according to common custom (urf). The commentary notes that this may be a difference of time and place. As for necklaces of chrysolite or emerald without setting, the fatwa follows the view of the two (Sahibayn), meaning they do break the oath. This highlights the Hanafi reliance on custom in oath interpretation.

Key Terms
حلي (huliyy): Ornaments worn for adornment, typically of gold, silver, or precious stones.
مرصع (murassa'): Set with precious stones (like diamonds, rubies).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, does wearing a plain pearl necklace break an oath not to wear jewelry?
A) No, because pearls are not jewelry by custom.
B) Yes, because the Quran calls pearls jewelry.
C) Yes, only if the pearls are set with precious stones.
D) No, because oaths are not taken seriously.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a Quranic text explicitly classifies an item as jewelry, that classification overrides custom in oath cases; the fatwa follows the Sahibayn's view.

The Pearl Necklace Oath: Custom vs. Quranic Literalism
Defining what constitutes jewelry (hilyah) that breaks an oath, with disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding plain pearl necklaces and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Oaths are based on custom (urf), not literal Quranic usage – plain pearls are jewelry only if set.

This lesson examines a key dispute within the Hanafi school: does wearing a plain pearl necklace break an oath not to wear jewelry? The Quran calls pearls 'hilyah' (jewelry), but Abu Hanifa argues that custom (urf) determines what counts. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) take the literal view. We will follow the argument and see why the fatwa is on their view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath scenario
A person swears not to wear jewelry (hilyah) and then wears a plain pearl necklace (without setting). Is the oath broken?
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa says the oath is NOT broken because plain pearls are not customarily used as adornment; they become jewelry only when set. He relies on the principle that oaths are based on custom (urf).
The Quranic reference is metaphorical (majaz).
3
State the companions' position and proof
The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say the oath IS broken because the Quran explicitly calls pearls 'hilyah' (jewelry) in 16:14, and pearls are extracted from the sea for adornment.
They take the literal (haqiqah) meaning.
4
Evaluate the tarjih and mufta bihi
The text states the fatwa is on the companions' view ('wal-fatwa 'ala qawlihima'). Despite Abu Hanifa's custom-based reasoning, the stronger textual proof and later practice support the companions.
The difference may also be temporal (change in custom).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولبس خاتم ذهب مبتدأ وخبره: 'لبس حلي' أي من حلف لا يلبس حلياً، فلبس خاتم ذهب بفتح التاء وكسرها حنث؛ لأنها حلي؛ ولهذا لا يحل استعماله للرجال، وقوله: 'أو عقد لؤلؤ' فالمذكور هنا على الإطلاق وهو قولهما، فيحنث إذا قال: لا يلبس حلياً فلبس؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَتَسْتَخْرِجُوا مِنْهُ حِلْيَةً تَلْبَسُونَهَا ﴾ (النحل:١٤)، وإنما يستخرج من البحر اللؤلؤ، وعند أبي حنيفة: لا يحنث بلبس مطلقاً؛ لأنه لا يتحلى به عرفاً إلا مرصعاً . الأيمان على العرف وسمي حلية في الآية مجازاً، وقيل: هذا اختلاف عصر وزمان، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا لبس عقداً من زبرجد أو زمرد غير مرصع والفتوى على قولهما. (مستخلص، عيني) أو عقد لؤلؤ لبس حلي لا خاتم فضة. لا خاتم فضة: أي ليس لبس خاتم فضة لبس حلي حتى لو حلف لا يلبس حلياً لا يحنث بلبسه؛ لأنه ليس بحلي كامل؛ لأن الحلي يستعمل للتزين فقط، وخاتم الفضة يستعمل للزينة وبغير الزينة، ولهذا حل للرجال، ولو كان حلياً من كل وجه لما حل، وخاتم الفضة إذا صيغ على هيئة خاتم النساء بأن كان ذا فص يحنث بلبسه وهو الصحيح، وقيد بخاتم الفضة؛ لأن الخلخال من تبع له فلا يصير والسوار والقلادة والقرط حلي، وكذا لو كان خاتم الفضة مموهاً بذهب. [رمز الحقائق: ۳۷۱/۱] (فتح)
And wearing a gold ring: subject and predicate: 'wearing jewelry'. So if someone swears not to wear jewelry, and wears a gold ring (with fath or kasr of the ta), he breaks his oath because it is jewelry; hence it is not permissible for men. His statement 'or a pearl necklace' is mentioned here absolutely, and it is the view of the two companions: he breaks if he says 'I will not wear jewelry' and wears it, due to the verse 'And you extract from it jewelry to wear' (Quran 16:14); and pearls are extracted from the sea. According to Abu Hanifa: he does not break by wearing it absolutely, because it is not customarily used as adornment unless set. Oaths are based on custom, and it is called jewelry in the verse metaphorically. It is said this is a difference of time and era. According to this difference, if one wears a necklace of chrysolite or emerald without setting, the fatwa is on the view of the two companions. Or a pearl necklace: wearing jewelry. Not a silver ring: meaning wearing a silver ring is not wearing jewelry, so if one swears not to wear jewelry, he does not break by wearing it; because it is not complete jewelry; since jewelry is used only for adornment, while the silver ring is used for both adornment and other purposes, hence it is permissible for men. If it were jewelry in every respect, it would not be permissible. If a silver ring is made in the style of women's rings by having a bezel, he breaks by wearing it, and this is the correct view. The restriction to silver ring is because anklets, bracelets, necklaces, and earrings are jewelry. Similarly, if the silver ring is gilded with gold, it also breaks the oath.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage deals with oaths related to wearing jewelry (hilyah). It first discusses gold rings: wearing a gold ring breaks an oath not to wear jewelry because gold rings are considered jewelry and are forbidden for men. The text then raises the case of a pearl necklace. There is an inter-madhhab dispute: the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that wearing a plain pearl necklace breaks the oath because the Quran refers to pearls as jewelry. Abu Hanifa disagrees, stating that plain pearls are not customarily worn as adornment; only set pearls (studded) count. The passage notes that oaths are based on custom (urf), and the Quranic reference to pearls as jewelry is metaphorical. It further mentions that the difference may be temporal. The fatwa is given on the view of the two companions. Next, the passage addresses silver rings: wearing a silver ring does not break an oath against wearing jewelry because a silver ring is not exclusively ornamental; it serves other purposes and is permissible for men. However, if the silver ring is made in the style of women's rings (with a bezel), it becomes jewelry and thus breaks the oath. The text also lists other items like anklets, bracelets, necklaces, and earrings as jewelry, and notes that a silver ring gilded with gold also counts as jewelry.

Key Terms
حلية (hilyah): Ornament or jewelry used for adornment.
عرف (urf): Customary practice of the people.
مرصع (morasaa'): Set with precious stones; studded.

B) He breaks his oath according to the two companions, which is the fatwa.
C) He breaks his oath only if the pearls are set in gold.
D) He does not break his oath because pearls are not jewelry.

Key Takeaway
The fatwa on wearing a plain pearl necklace is that it breaks an oath against jewelry, based on the two companions' literal reading of Quran 16:14.

Oaths on Surfaces: When Adding a Layer Breaks the Oath
Explains when adding a layer on a surface violates an oath, with a focus on the concept of customary follow-up.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between barriers that break an oath and those that do not, apply the concept of tabi' (follow-up), and explain the Hanafi position including the dispute with Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A layer that is customarily part of a surface (tabi‘) breaks an oath to avoid that surface; a separate, non-customary layer does not.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq outlines three scenarios where a person swears not to use a surface and then adds an intermediary. The general rule is that if the added layer is not customarily considered part of the surface, the oath is not broken. However, if custom treats the layer as a follow-up (tabi‘), the oath is broken. A significant intra-madhhab dispute arises in the second scenario regarding whether placing a bed on a bed constitutes sleeping on the lower bed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three oath scenarios
The passage presents three distinct cases: sitting on ground with a mat, sleeping on a bed with another bed on top, and sitting on a bench with another bench on top. In each, the person swears not to use the base surface directly.
The three scenarios are introduced together with the initial ruling 'la yahnath' (does not break).
2
Determine the ruling in each scenario
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, in all three cases the oath is NOT broken. The text clearly states 'la yahnath' as the answer.
Abu Yusuf dissents in the second scenario; his view is noted but the majority Hanafi position is upheld.
3
Analyze the reasoning for non-breaking
The reasoning for the first scenario: the mat or hasir is a barrier that is not a follow-up (tabi‘) to the person (unlike his clothing). For the second and third: a thing does not become a follow-up to its like (one bed does not follow another), so attribution to the lower surface is cut.
The concept of tabi‘ is central: custom determines whether the added layer is considered part of the surface.
4
Examine the dispute in the second scenario
Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i hold that placing a bed on a bed breaks the oath because the person sleeps on both beds. The Hanafi majority response: the lower bed is not directly used; the upper bed is an independent surface.
This dispute affects fatwa: the mufta bihi (operative opinion) is the majority view that the oath is not broken.
5
Identify cases where the oath IS broken
The passage then states: if a person places a qiram (thin curtain) on a bed, or a mat/hasir on a bench, the oath is broken because these are considered tabi‘ (customarily part of the surface). The principle is extended to roofs and shops.
The difference: in the first scenarios the added layer is not customary, whereas here it is customary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يجلس على الأرض فجلس على بساط أو حصير، أو لا ينام على هذا الفراش، فجعل فوقه فراشا آخر فنام عليه، أو لا يجلس على هذا السرير فجعل فوقه سريرا آخر لا يحنث، ولو جعل على الفراش قرام أو جعل على السرير بساط أو حصير حنث. لا يجلس على الأرض ههنا ثلاث مسائل وعدم الحنث جواب في الكل، وهو قوله: 'لا يحنث'، الأولى: حلف لا يجلس على الأرض فجلس على بساط أو حصير لا يحنث؛ لوجود الحائل بينه وبين الأرض، وهو ليس بتابع للحالف؛ لأنه لا جالساً على الأرض، بخلاف ما إذا كان الحائل ثيابه؛ لأنه : حائلاً، فلو خلع ثوبه وبسطه وجلس عليه لا يحنث؛ لارتفاع التبعية؛ لأن المراد الحائل المنفصل عنه ولو من خشب أو جلد، الثانية: حلف لا ينام على هذا الفراش فجعل فوقه فراشاً آخر، فنام عليه لا يحنث؛ لأنه مثله، والشيء لا يكون تبعاً لمثله، فتنقطع النسبة إلى الأسفل، وقيد بكون الفراش مشاراً إليه؛ لأنه لو نكره فحلف: لا ينام على فراش حنث بوضع الفراش على الفراش الثالثة: حلف لا يجلس على سرير معين، فجعل فوقه سريراً آخر ونام عليه لا يحنث، فإن الشيء لا يتبع مثله، ولأنه لا يعد جالساً على السرير الأسفل، وعند أبي يوسف: يحنث في الثانية، وبه قال الشافعي؛ لأنه نام عليهما جميعاً. (فتح، عيني) لا يحنث هذا جواب المسائل الثلاث وقد مر تفصيلها مع وجه عدم الحنث في الصفحة السابقة، فلينظر ثمه. (محشي) ولو جعل على الفراش قرام بكسر القاف وهو الستر الرقيق، وفي الصحاح القرام: ستر فيه رقم ونقوش، وكذلك المقرم والمقرمة، فلو حلف لا ينام على الفراش، فجعل على الفراش قراماً أي ملاءة ونام عليه يحنث؛ لأن القرام تبع للفراش، وقوله أو جعل على السرير بساطه أي فيما إذا حلف لا يجلس على السرير فجعل عليه بساطاً أو حصيراً حنث ؛ لأنه يعد جالساً على السرير عادة؛ لأن الناس لا يعتادون الجلوس على السرير بلا بساط أو حصير، وعلى هذا لو حلف لا ينام على السطح أو الدكان أو لا يجلس فبسط عليه فراشاً أو حصيراً فنام عليه أو جلس، حنث؛ لأنه يعد نائماً وجالساً عليهما عرفاً [ رمز الحقائق: ۳۷۱/۱] (فتح)
He should not sit on the ground, so he sat on a mat or hayr; or he should not sleep on this bed, so he placed another bed on top and slept on it; or he should not sit on this bench, so he placed another bench on top, does not break. But if he placed on the bed a curtain, or placed on the bench a mat or hayr, he breaks. 'He should not sit on the ground' here three issues, and the absence of breaking is the answer for all. First: he swore not to sit on the ground, then sat on a mat or hayr, does not break; due to the barrier between him and the ground, and it is not a follow-up to him; he is not considered sitting on the ground. Unlike if the barrier is his clothes; if he takes off his garment and spreads it and sits on it, he does not break; because the follow-up is removed; the intended barrier is one that is separate, even if of wood or leather. Second: he swore not to sleep on this bed, then placed another bed on top and slept on it, does not break; because it is like it, and a thing does not become a follow-up to its like, so the attribution to the lower is cut. The restriction to a specific bed is because if he made a general oath: not to sleep on a bed, then placing a bed on a bed would break. Third: he swore not to sit on a specific bench, then placed another bench on top and sat on it, does not break; because a thing does not follow its like, and he is not considered sitting on the lower bench. According to Abu Yusuf: he breaks in the second case, and al-Shafi'i agreed, because he slept on both. 'He does not break' is the answer to the three issues. And if he placed on the bed a qiram (a thin curtain), and in al-Sihah: qiram is a cloth with designs and patterns, then if he swore not to sleep on the bed, and placed a qiram on the bed and slept on it, he breaks; because the qiram is a follow-up to the bed. Similarly, if he placed on the bench a mat or hayr, he breaks; because he is customarily considered sitting on the bench; people do not usually sit on a bench without a mat. Based on this, if he swore not to sleep on a roof or a shop or not to sit, and spread a bed or mat and slept or sat, he breaks; because he is customarily considered sleeping or sitting on it.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage addresses three scenarios regarding oaths about surfaces. The general principle is that if there is a separate layer (not a follow-up), the oath is not broken; if the layer is considered a follow-up (tabi'), it is broken. The first scenario: if someone swears not to sit on the ground but sits on a mat or hayr, he does not break because the mat is a barrier that is not a part of his clothing. The second scenario: if he swears not to sleep on a particular bed and places another identical bed on top, he does not break because one bed is not subsidiary to another. However, Abu Yusuf and al-Shafi'i differ, arguing he slept on both. The third scenario: similar to the second but for a bench. The passage then covers additional cases: if he places a thin curtain (qiram) on the bed, he breaks because the curtain is considered part of the bed for sleeping purposes. Similarly, placing a mat on a bench is customary, so it breaks the oath. The passage extends this to roofs and shops: placing a mat on them breaks the oath because custom considers it as using the surface.

Key Terms
القرام (al-qirām): A thin curtain or sheet placed on a bed, considered a follow-up to the bed.
التابع (al-tābi'): Something that is considered part of the object in customary usage, like a mat on a bench.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person swears not to sit on a specific bench and then places a mat on it and sits, does he break his oath?
A) No, because the mat is a barrier.
B) Yes, because the mat is customarily considered part of the bench.
C) No, unless the mat is made of the same material as the bench.
D) Yes, but only if the mat is thick.

Key Takeaway
The concept of tabi‘ (customary follow-up) determines whether adding a layer violates an oath; if custom treats the layer as part of the surface, the oath is broken; otherwise, it is not.
